201 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Butt v. State of California


No. S020835


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


4 Cal. 4th 668; 842 P.2d 1240; 15 Cal. Rptr. 2d 480; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 6139; 93 Daily Journal DAR 152; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 39



December 31, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. C91-01645, Ellen Sickles James, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, we reverse the trial court's remedial order of May 2, 1991, insofar as it approves funding of an emergency loan to the District from appropriations for the Oakland Unified School District and the Greater Avenues for Independence program. In all other respects, the court's orders of April 29 and May 2, 1991, are affirmed. The Court of Appeal is directed to remand the cause to the trial court for such further proceedings as may be appropriate under the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant government sought review of a decision from the Superior Court for Contra Costa County (California) that directed appellant to ensure that students of a certain school district would receive a full school term or its equivalent. Appellant contended that it did not have a constitutional duty other than to provide an equal allocation of funds to all school districts within the state.


OVERVIEW: Respondent concerned parent filed for a temporary and permanent injunction seeking to force appellant government to ensure that a local school district would not close its schools six weeks before the end of the year due to budgetary problems. The lower court, after a hearing, found that appellant was responsible for the fundamental educational rights of students and that it must remedy a local district's inability to provide an education basically equivalent to other districts in the state. The lower court ordered appellant to ensure that the students would receive a full term or its equivalent. The lower court also approved an emergency plan and an emergency loan to accomplish the plan. Appellant contended that it had no constitutional duty other than to allocate educational funds equally between the various school districts within the state. The court affirmed the lower court's decision and found that public education was a fundamental concern of appellant and it had to provide all students a basic fundamental education. The court found that the lower court's order was proper except where it ordered money be distributed from specially earmarked funds.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court order that directed appellant government to ensure that students of a certain school district would receive a full school term or its equivalent. However, the court set aside that portion of the order that distributed money from funds that had been validly earmarked for other purposes by the legislature.

 

202 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Proctor


No. S004555, Crim. No. 23185


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


4 Cal. 4th 499; 842 P.2d 1100; 15 Cal. Rptr. 2d 340; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 6123; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 10386; 92 Daily Journal DAR 17443



December 28, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied March 10, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 1276. Mosk, J., and Kennard, J., are of the opinion the petition should be granted.
Certiorari Granted December 6, 1993, 62 U.S.L. Week 3393 [126 L.Ed 2d 564, 114 S. Ct. 598].
Rehearing denied by People v. Proctor, 1993 Cal. LEXIS 1276 (Cal., Mar. 10, 1993)
Motion granted by, Writ of certiorari granted Proctor v. California, 510 U.S. 1010, 114 S. Ct. 598, 126 L. Ed. 2d 564, 1993 U.S. LEXIS 7819 (1993)
Affirmed by, Sub nomine at Tuilaepa v. California, 512 U.S. 967, 114 S. Ct. 2630, 129 L. Ed. 2d 750, 1994 U.S. LEXIS 5084 (1994)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Shasta County, No. 74024, Joseph H. Redmon, Judge, and Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 65383, Sheldon H. Grossfeld, Judges.
People v. Proctor, 1992 Cal. LEXIS 6348 (Cal., Dec. 28, 1992)



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant murderer appealed, by authority of Cal. Penal. Code. § 1239(d), his convictions and death penalty sentence entered in the Superior Court of Shasta County (California) for first degree murder, forcible rape, and first degree burglary in violation of Cal. Penal. Code. §§ 187, 189, 261(2), and 460, respectively. Defendant's assertions of error included venue, sufficiency of the evidence, jury deliberations, and instructions.


OVERVIEW: The evidence at trial established that defendant entered the residence of a widow living alone, and raped, tortured, and murdered her. He then transported her body in the trunk of her automobile to a site near a lake where he pushed the body off an embankment. The injuries to the victim were severe and found to have been inflicted for the purpose of causing fear and pain. Defendant also had three prior convictions and was sentenced to death. The court affirmed the judgment of conviction and sentence in its entirety. The court found that the denial of his motion to change venue during the guilt phase was proper and that the evidence was sufficient to establish all the necessary elements of the crime and the aggravating factors. The court noted that the matters pertaining to jury deliberations did not constitute error. As for the penalty phase, the court noted that no prosecutorial misconduct occurred and defendant waived this issue by his failure to object. The court found that no instructional error took place during the penalty phase and the statute was constitutional and denial of the application for modification was proper and supported by the evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions and death penalty sentence for first degree murder, forcible rape, and first degree burglary finding that defendant's allegations of error were without merit or waived by failure to object in the trial court.

People v. Tuilaepa


No. S004786, Crim. No. 26420


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


4 Cal. 4th 569; 842 P.2d 1142; 15 Cal. Rptr. 2d 382; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 6121; 92 Daily Journal DAR 17469; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 10402



December 28, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied March 10, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 1278.
Certiorari Granted December 6, 1993, 62 U.S.L. Week 3393 [126 L.Ed 2d 563, 114 S. Ct. 598].
Rehearing denied by People v. Tuilaepa, 1993 Cal. LEXIS 1278 (Cal., Mar. 10, 1993)
Stay granted by People v. Tuilaepa, 1993 Cal. LEXIS 2968 (Cal., June 2, 1993)
Motion granted by, Writ of certiorari granted, in part Tuilaepa v. California, 510 U.S. 1010, 114 S. Ct. 598, 126 L. Ed. 2d 563, 1993 U.S. LEXIS 7818 (1993)
Affirmed by Tuilaepa v. California, 512 U.S. 967, 114 S. Ct. 2630, 129 L. Ed. 2d 750, 1994 U.S. LEXIS 5084 (1994)
Application granted by People v. Tuilaepa (Paul P.), 2006 Cal. LEXIS 13254 (Cal., Oct. 23, 2006)
Application granted by People v. Tuilaepa, 2008 Cal. LEXIS 9416 (Cal., July 30, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A035060, Elsworth M. Beam, Judge.
People v. Tuilaepa, 1992 Cal. LEXIS 6351 (Cal., Dec. 28, 1992)



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that convicted him of first degree murder, attempted robbery, six counts of robbery, and two counts of assault with a firearm and sentenced him to death. Defendant appealed, alleging that he was prejudiced because he was shackled in court and improper evidence of other crimes was admitted. He also argued that the death penalty was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted defendant of first degree murder and attempted robbery, six counts of robbery, and two counts of assault with a firearm. An allegation that defendant personally used a rifle in the commission or attempted commission of each of the foregoing offenses was found to be true. An allegation that defendant inflicted great bodily injury in committing one of the robberies and both of the assaults was also found to be true. Finally, the jury found to be true a special circumstance that the murder occurred during the commission of a robbery. Following a penalty trial, the jury sentenced defendant to death, and the trial court denied the automatic motion to modify the verdict. The appeal was automatic. Defendant challenged the guilty judgment on the ground he was improperly shackled in court, that evidence of other crimes was improperly admitted, and that the death penalty was unconstitutional. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court entirely finding that no prejudicial error at the guilt or penalty phases of defendant's trial occurred.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court entering the convictions against defendant because there was no prejudicial error committed during the guilt or the penalty phase of defendant's trial despite the fact that defendant was shackled in court, and the trial court admitted evidence of other crimes.

 

204 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Noguera


No. S005170, Crim. No. 26428


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


4 Cal. 4th 599; 842 P.2d 1160; 15 Cal. Rptr. 2d 400; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 6122; 92 Daily Journal DAR 17480; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 10409



December 28, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied March 10, 1993, Reported at 1993 Cal. LEXIS 1279.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-53691, Robert R. Fitzgerald, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction and death sentence for first degree murder in violation of Cal. Penal. Code § 187, 189 entered in the Superior Court of Orange County (California). Defendant raised issues pertaining to the evidence, accomplice evidence, and the jury instructions in the guilt phase. In the penalty phase, he raised issues concerning voir dire, improper prosecutor arguments and evidence, and the trial court's instructions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant murdered a woman in an apparent scheme to obtain money from her insurance. The victim's daughter, and another man, who were witnesses at trial, were participants in defendant's plan according to defendant. Defendant was also angry at the victim's interference in his relationship with her daughter. The daughter was arrested, charged an convicted as a juvenile for first-degree murder. Defendant denied any involvement in the offense, but he was convicted. During the penalty phase, the jury found that defendant had used a deadly weapon and had committed the murder for financial gain and, thus, sentenced defendant to death. Defendant alleged many errors. The court first noted that the alleged evidentiary errors either fell within a recognized exception to the hearsay rule or were harmless and not prejudicial to defendant. The court also rejected defendant's claims of error related to the testimony and instructions of the accomplice witness. The court also examined defendant's assertions of error during the penalty phase and found that the prosecutor had not used improper arguments nor had the trial court improperly instructed the jury during the penalty phase.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and death sentence for first degree murder. The court found that most of defendant's allegations of error were not legally sufficient to warrant reversal and errors that did exist were harmless error.

 

205 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



City of Huntington Beach v. Board of Administration


No. S024687.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


4 Cal. 4th 462; 841 P.2d 1034; 14 Cal. Rptr. 2d 514; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 6117



December 24, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C724751, Kurt J. Lewin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed with directions to vacate the order of the superior court denying a peremptory writ of mandamus and to remand the case to that court for proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeal (California), which affirmed the trial court's denial of appellant's petition for writ of administrative mandamus. Respondent retirement board adopted the administrative law judge's proposed decision to grant respondent employees' request for reclassification in a retirement plan membership based on their custodial functions as jailers.


OVERVIEW: Respondent retirement board adopted the administrative law judge's proposed decision to grant respondent employees' request for reclassification in a retirement plan membership based on their custodial functions as jailers. The reclassification as local safety members afforded respondent employees with superior retirement benefits compared to those previously provided. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment of the lower court, which had affirmed the trial court's order, denying a peremptory writ of mandamus requested by appellant city. Cal. Gov't Code § 20020 authorized local safety member status to certain police department employees, but neither included nor excluded jail employees as local policemen. The court held that the supervision and custody of jail inmates did not constitute active law enforcement for purposes of § 20020. Appellant had not opted to treat respondent employees as local policemen under Cal. Gov't Code § 20020.9, and their custodial functions did not constitute active law enforcement under § 20020; thus, they failed to qualify for local safety member status.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court, directing it to vacate the trial court's order, denying a peremptory writ of mandamus requested by appellant city. The court held that respondent employees did not qualify for local safety member status under the Public Employees' Retirement System with respect to their custodial duties and responsibilities as jailers because appellant elected not to treat respondents as local policemen.

 

206 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Clark v. Burleigh


No. S020854


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


4 Cal. 4th 474; 841 P.2d 975; 14 Cal. Rptr. 2d 455; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 6119



December 24, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Monterey County, No. 90060, John F. Herlihy, Judge. * 


* Judge of the Santa Clara Superior Court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed with directions to affirm the judgment of the trial court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted review of a judgment entered by a California court of appeals, which declared Cal. Elec. Code § 10012.1 unconstitutional in plaintiff registrar's action to challenge the candidate statement filed by defendant judicial candidate.


OVERVIEW: Defendant judicial candidate filed a candidate's statement pursuant to Cal. Elec. Code § 10012 prior to a county judicial election. He devoted the bulk of his statement to an attack on the incumbent. Plaintiff registrar filed suit alleging that the statement violated § 10012.1, which limited the content of a candidate's statement to information about the candidate. The trial court found that the statement violated § 10012.1. The appellate court reversed, concluding that § 10012.1 violated the free speech guaranty of U.S. Const. amend I. The court reversed. It first concluded that the relevant forum was the candidate statement itself and that the statement was a nonpublic forum. Applying a deferential standard of review, the court held that § 10012.1 did not violate defendant's First Amendment rights because the content restriction was reasonably related to the statutory goal of providing voters with information about judicial candidates and there remained numerous other channels by which a judicial candidate could convey criticism of an opponent to the voters. The court also found that § 10012.1 did not violate defendant's equal protection rights.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court because the content restrictions imposed on defendant's judicial candidate statement by the election code were permissible under the First Amendment and did not violate defendant's equal protection rights. The court directed the appellate court to affirm the trial court's judgment.

 

207 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Johnson v. Bradley


No. S021118


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


4 Cal. 4th 389; 841 P.2d 990; 14 Cal. Rptr. 2d 470; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 6118



December 24, 1992, Decided



DISPOSITION:  The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, state assemblyman, state senator, and city council member, sought review of a California appellate court's denial of their request for a writ of mandamus to invalidate a campaign reform measure adopted by the voters of the City of Los Angeles and to enjoin respondents, mayor, members of the city council, city controller, and city clerk, from enforcing the measure.


OVERVIEW: The voters of Los Angeles adopted a campaign finance reform measure that provided for public funding of campaigns for city offices. Petitioners, state assemblyman, state senator, and city council member, filed suit in the appellate court for a writ of mandamus to invalidate the measure and to enjoin respondents, mayor, members of city council, city controller, and city clerk, from enforcing it on the grounds that it violated Cal. Gov't Code § 85300, which banned public financing of campaigns. The appellate court denied the writ. The court granted review and affirmed. The court first determined that the measure implicated a municipal affair under Cal. Const. art. XI, § 5 and that it posed a conflict with state law. The court then concluded that § 85300 was a matter of statewide concern, as it was reasonably calculated to address the state's interest in the integrity of elections, but that it was not narrowly tailored to meet that concern because the use of public funds to finance elections would not have a corrupting influence on the elections and a ban on public funding would frustrate the goal of electoral integrity. The court thus held that the reform measure was enforceable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of the request by petitioners, state assemblyman, state senator, and city council member, for a writ of mandamus to invalidate a municipal campaign finance reform measure because the state statute banning public funding of elections was not narrowly tailored to address the state's interest in the integrity of elections.

 

208 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



In re Marriage of Hilke


No. S025205


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


4 Cal. 4th 215; 841 P.2d 891; 14 Cal. Rptr. 2d 371; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 6102; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 10122; 92 Daily Journal DAR 17019



December 17, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 175282, Ronald C. Stevens, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant ex-husband challenged an order of the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California), which held that the marital residence that he and respondent ex-wife, now deceased, had owned as joint tenants was community property under Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.1.


OVERVIEW: The trial court held that a marital residence that appellant ex-husband and respondent ex-wife, now deceased, held in joint tenancy was community property under Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.1. The court of appeals reversed that order, finding that appellant's survivorship right as a joint tenant prevailed. The court reversed that judgment on appeal, finding that the case involved property division on dissolution of marriage under Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.1(d) because respondent's death did not deprive the trial court of its retained jurisdiction to decide collateral property rights. The court also reasoned that Cal. Civ. Code § 4800.1(a)(3) explicitly stated that § 4800.1 applied to a property division on dissolution of a marriage regardless of when it was acquired. The court also held that appellant was not deprived of a vested property interest without due process of law because his survivorship interest was subject to the condition precedent that he outlive respondent. The court further concluded that the house's deed did not contain a clear statement that it was separate property, and the record contained no proof as to a written agreement that it was separate property.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a judgment that appellant ex-husband's survivorship right as a joint tenant prevailed in a dispute as to the marital residence that he and respondent ex-wife, now deceased, had held as joint tenants. The court held that the survivorship interest was not vested due to the condition precedent of outliving respondent and that the governing statute applied to property that was acquired before its enactment.

 

209 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Sandoval


No. S004765. Crim. No. 26405


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


4 Cal. 4th 155; 841 P.2d 862; 14 Cal. Rptr. 2d 342; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 6098; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 10012; 92 Daily Journal DAR 16700



December 14, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified February 10, 1993. Rehearing Denied February 10, 1993, Reported at 1993 Cal. LEXIS 586.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A760386, Richard P. Kalustian, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted defendant of four counts of first degree murder under Cal. Penal Code § 187 with a multiple-murder special-circumstance finding under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(3). Defendant was also convicted of one count of attempted murder under Cal. Penal Code §§ 664 and 187.


OVERVIEW: Defendant shot three victims at a park, two of whom were members of a different gang than defendant. About two weeks later, he shot and killed a couple he once met at the home of a witness. Defendant was convicted by a jury of four counts of first degree murder under Cal. Penal Code §187, with a multiple-murder special-circumstance finding under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(3), and one count of attempted murder under Cal. Penal Code §§ 664 and 187. The court affirmed defendant's convictions, holding that the trial court's refusal to sever the offenses was not an abuse of discretion due to the fact that defendant failed to show a substantial danger of potential prejudice from the joinder of the offenses. The court further held that evidence of defendant's gang membership was relevant to the motive for two of the murders and therefore admissible. The court held that victim impact evidence was admissible at the penalty phase, and that although biblical references in the prosecutor's closing argument were improper, there was no reasonable possibility that the jury would have reached more favorable verdicts had the misconduct not occurred.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for murder and attempted murder because he failed to show that joinder of the charges against him deprived him of a fair hearing and, thus, the trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying his motion for severance. It was not error to admit evidence of defendant's gang membership or victim impact, and improper biblical references in the prosecutor's final argument were not reversible error.

 

210 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Hawthorne


No. S004707, Crim. No. 25218


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


4 Cal. 4th 43; 841 P.2d 118; 14 Cal. Rptr. 2d 133; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 5778; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9719; 92 Daily Journal DAR 16285



December 3, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified January 27, 1993. Rehearing Denied January 27, 1993, Reported at 1993 Cal. LEXIS 417.
Rehearing denied by People v. Hawthorne, 1993 Cal. LEXIS 417 (Cal., Jan. 27, 1993)
Motion denied by People v. Hawthorne, 1993 Cal. LEXIS 3670 (Cal., July 14, 1993)
Writ of certiorari denied Hawthorne v. California, 510 U.S. 1013, 114 S. Ct. 605, 126 L. Ed. 2d 570, 1993 U.S. LEXIS 7678 (1993)
Application granted by People v. Hawthorne, Jr., (Anderson), 2011 Cal. LEXIS 7725 (Cal., July 20, 2011)
Application denied by People v. Hawthorne, 2013 Cal. LEXIS 7930 (Cal., Sept. 25, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A386104, Ronald S. W. Lew, Judge.
In re Hawthorne, 1992 Cal. LEXIS 5981 (Cal., Dec. 2, 1992)
People v. Hawthorne, 1992 Cal. LEXIS 6058 (Cal., Dec. 3, 1992)



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of his conviction for first degree murder in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California). The jury found appellant guilty of two first degree murders and found true the special circumstance allegation of multiple murder. After a penalty verdict of death was returned, the trial court denied defendant's motions for a new trial and for modification of the verdict.


OVERVIEW: A jury found appellant guilty of the first degree murders of two men pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 187 and found true the special circumstance allegation of multiple murder pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 190.2 (a)(3). Appellant charged the prosecutor with prejudicial misconduct during the guilt phase argument because she quoted from a dissent in a United States Supreme Court case, to the effect that law enforcement had an obligation to ascertain the true facts surrounding a crime, while the defense did not. In affirming the conviction, the court disapproved the comment, but ruled that appellant was not prejudiced in light of the admonishments issued by the trial court. Appellant also objected to the prosecutor's suggestion during the penalty phase argument that the jury consider defendant's youth as a factor in aggravation. Appellant argued that under Cal. Penal Code § 190.3, factor (i), age could only function as a factor in mitigation. In affirming the sentence, the court ruled that while the jury must be allowed to consider all relevant mitigating evidence including age, no rationale precluded its consideration as an aggravating factor.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's first degree murder convictions and death penalty sentence. The court held that the United States Constitution did not require the jury to find beyond a reasonable doubt that the prosecution proved each aggravating factor, that the circumstances in aggravation outweighed those in mitigation, or that death was the appropriate penalty.

 

211 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Johnson


Nos. S004778, S012228. Crim. No. 26412


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


3 Cal. 4th 1183; 842 P.2d 1; 14 Cal. Rptr. 2d 702; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 5693; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9582; 92 Daily Journal DAR 15971



November 30, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied January 20, 1993.
Rehearing denied by People v. Johnson, 1993 Cal. LEXIS 221 (Cal., Jan. 20, 1993)
Stay granted by People v. Johnson, 1993 Cal. LEXIS 2188 (Cal., Apr. 21, 1993)
Writ of certiorari denied Johnson v. California, 510 U.S. 836, 114 S. Ct. 114, 126 L. Ed. 2d 80, 1993 U.S. LEXIS 5261 (1993)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 33309, Coleman F. Fannin, Judge.
People v. Johnson, 1992 Cal. LEXIS 5969 (Cal., Nov. 30, 1992)



DISPOSITION: The judgment in No. S004778 is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, via an automatic appeal, challenged his convictions for murder, attempted murder, robbery in an inhabited dwelling, and first-degree burglary obtained in the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), and alleged that the trial court erred in not granting his motion for an order for preservation of material offered or received into evidence in his capital trial.


OVERVIEW: In an automatic appeal, defendant challenged his convictions of various capital crimes and the trial court's denial of his motion for an order for preservation of material offered or received into evidence at trial. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment in its entirety because the court had not found any errors warranting reversal. Defendant's purported appeal of the trial court's denial of his motion was dismissed because the court held that the trial court's postjudgment order had not affected the defendant's substantial rights because the material sought to be preserved was already part of the record preserved for defendant's automatic appeal of his capital conviction. The court held that although the trial court may well have had the inherent power to issue an order for the preservation of the materials, its refusal to do so could not have prejudiced defendant inasmuch as the materials were already part of the record.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed in its entirety and defendant's appeal of the trial court's denial of his motion for an order for preservation of material offered or received into evidence in his capital trial was dismissed, because the material sought were already part of the record and the denial of his motion did not affect his substantial rights.

 

212 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Payton


No. S004437. Crim. No. 22511, No. S004437. Crim. No. 25552


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


3 Cal. 4th 1050; 839 P.2d 1035; 13 Cal. Rptr. 2d 526; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 5669; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9415; 92 Daily Journal DAR 15669



November 23, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied February 10, 1993, Reported at 1993 Cal. LEXIS 585.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-45040, Donald A. McCartin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. The petition for a writ of habeas corpus is denied on the merits.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a habeas corpus petition consolidated with his automatic appeal, petitioner challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which convicted him of felony murder and rape of a boarder, and of the attempted murders of a boarding-house owner and her son. Petitioner argued a death penalty was imposed because his defense counsel failed to present evidence of his mental disorder caused by Vietnam combat experiences.


OVERVIEW: In a petition consolidated with his automatic appeal, petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus to challenge his conviction for the first-degree murder and rape of a woman and two attempted murders. A jury found appellant murdered a female boarder with a butcher knife and attacked the boarding house owner and her 10-year-old son. The surviving victims testified that appellant stopped at the boarding house in which he used to live, drank three beers with the owner, and then asked to sleep on a couch. He then attacked the victims. Petitioner contended his counsel ineffectively failed to investigate or present evidence of petitioner's mental disorder caused by Vietnam combat experiences. Petitioner also contended the trial court erred in failing to instruct the jury on the required intent to kill in felony murder. The appellate court affirmed the judgment and denied the habeas corpus petition, holding that intent to kill was no longer a required element of a felony-based special circumstance. The appellate court also held that because petitioner invented his combat experiences after he was convicted there was no evidence to alert defense counsel to a need to explore the defense.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment and denied the habeas corpus petition because an intent to kill was no longer a required element of a felony-based special circumstance. Because petitioner's claims regarding his combat experiences were found to be totally false, the court held the failure to present that evidence did not undermine confidence in the guilt or penalty verdict.

 

213 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Hill


No. S005169. Crim. No. CR26427


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


3 Cal. 4th 959; 839 P.2d 984; 13 Cal. Rptr. 2d 475; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 5500; 92 Daily Journal DAR 15770; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9338



November 19, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. C-84675, Robert K. Byers, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which convicted him of two first-degree murders in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 187, and one count of robbery with the personal use of a firearm in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 211. The jury returned a sentence of death. Under Cal. Penal Code § 1239(b), defendant's appeal was automatic.


OVERVIEW: The bodies of a man and his four-year-old son were found by police on the floor of a jewelry store. Each victim had been shot in the head at close range with a .38 caliber gun. The lower court convicted defendant of two first-degree murders, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 187 and one count of robbery with the personal use of a firearm, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 211. The jury found to be true the special circumstances that the murders were convicted during a robbery and that defendant was guilty of multiple murders. The jury returned a sentence of death. The lower court judgment was affirmed. The reviewing court held, among other things, that defendant was not illegally arrested or detained and that his statements to police were voluntary. Defendant's waiver of Miranda rights was not ineffectual. Defendant was not entitled to have a witness deprived of his constitutional right against self-incrimination. Defendant failed to raise the ineffective-waiver argument in support of his motion under Miranda, and the argument was therefore waived. At the time of his trial, defendant had no statutory right to only those jurors who resided in the county of the trial.


OUTCOME: The lower court judgment that convicted defendant of two first-degree murders and one count of robbery was affirmed. The court held, among other things, that defendant was not illegally arrested or detained and that his statements to police were voluntary. Defendant's waiver of Miranda rights was not ineffectual. Defendant was not entitled to have a witness deprived of his constitutional right against self-incrimination.

People v. Wilson


No. S006546


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


3 Cal. 4th 926; 838 P.2d 1212; 13 Cal. Rptr. 2d 259; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 5468; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9196; 92 Daily Journal DAR 15266



November 12, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion denied by People v. Wilson, 1993 Cal. LEXIS 537 (Cal., Jan. 27, 1993)
Writ of certiorari denied California v. Wilson, 507 U.S. 1006, 123 L. Ed. 2d 269, 113 S. Ct. 1648, 1993 U.S. LEXIS 2458 (1993)
Related proceeding at People v. Wilson, 2005 Cal. LEXIS 7289 (Cal., July 11, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A029492, Richard F. Charvat, Judge.
People v. Wilson, 1992 Cal. LEXIS 5537 (Cal., Nov. 12, 1992)



DISPOSITION: As noted, in the companion habeas corpus proceeding (In re Wilson, supra, post, p. 945) we have concluded that the underlying judgment must be vacated in its entirety on the ground of ineffective assistance of counsel. Accordingly, the appeal is moot and is ordered dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted defendant of first degree murder, robbery, using a firearm in the commission of each offense, and the special circumstance of committing murder during the robbery, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §§ 187, 189, 211, 12022.5(a), and 190.2(a)(17)(i), and the imposition of the death penalty. Defendant also filed a petition for habeas corpus.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder, robbery, using a firearm in the commission of each offense, and the special circumstance of committing murder during the robbery, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §§ 187, 189, 211, 12022.5(a), and 190.2(a)(17)(i). Defendant appealed and filed for a writ of habeas corpus on the issue of ineffective assistance of counsel. Because the court granted the writ, stating that the failure of defendant's trial counsel to object to certain evidence amounted to ineffective assistance of counsel, the appeal was rendered moot, the judgment was vacated, and the matter was remanded for a new trial. In addressing several evidentiary matters likely to arise in retrial, the court found that the trial court properly exercised its discretion and weighed the probative value of photographs of victim against their prejudicial effect before admitting them. The court found that evidence of defendant's debt was improperly admitted to establish defendant's motive to commit the robbery and murder. The court concluded that jury instructions omitting lesser offenses were not error because the evidence did not support instructions on lesser offenses.


OUTCOME: The court found that defendant's appeal of his murder conviction was moot because the court vacated the judgment against defendant by granting defendant's petition for writ of habeas corpus, based on ineffective assistance of counsel. In an effort to avoid errors on retrial, the court approved the admission of photographs of the victim and the jury instructions omitting lesser offenses, but rejected evidence of indebtedness to show motive.

Board of Supervisors v. Local Agency Formation Com.


No. S023805


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


3 Cal. 4th 903; 838 P.2d 1198; 13 Cal. Rptr. 2d 245; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 5466; 92 Daily Journal DAR 15115; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9150



November 9, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 358798, James Timothy Ford, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The Court of Appeal's judgment is reversed with instructions to direct the trial court to enter judgment for defendant with respect to the constitutionality of section 57103. The Court of Appeal is to take such further action on the question of attorney fees as this disposition warrants.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city incorporation commission challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which held that Cal. Gov't Code § 57103 was unconstitutional on plaintiffs', social, labor, law enforcement, and business organizations, petition for writ of mandate and complaint for injunctive and declaratory relief.


OVERVIEW: Residents of an unincorporated area of a county sought to incorporate into a city, and filed a petition with defendant, a local agency formation commission, for incorporation. Defendant ordered a confirming election to be held only within the territory of the proposed city, in accordance with Cal. Gov't Code § 57103. Plaintiffs, various social, labor, law enforcement, and business organizations, challenged the constitutionality of the voting limitations on the ground that § 57103 denied them equal protection of the laws. The trial court entered judgment in plaintiffs' favor on certain environmental impact issues but refused to find the voting limitation in the act unconstitutional. The appellate division reversed and held that § 57103 was unconstitutional. The court reversed and held that § 57103 was constitutional because § 57103 did not impinge on the right to vote in a manner that required the application of a strict scrutiny analysis. Further, the court held that its prior decision in another case was not to be followed where it was inconsistent with the court's opinion.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that a city incorporation voting statute was unconstitutional and held that the statute did not deny plaintiffs, social, labor, law enforcement, and business organizations, equal protection because strict scrutiny was not required. The court instructed the appellate division to direct the trial court to enter a judgment for defendant city incorporation commission with respect to the constitutionality of the statute.

 

216 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



City of National City v. Wiener


No. S020887


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


3 Cal. 4th 832; 838 P.2d 223; 12 Cal. Rptr. 2d 701; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 5193; 92 Daily Journal DAR 14709; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8917



October 29, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified January 21, 1993. Rehearing Denied January 21, 1993, Reported at 1993 Cal. LEXIS 402.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 578784, Vincent P. Di Figlia, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We hold that National City's ordinance is constitutional under the First Amendment. Accordingly, the judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed, and the matter remanded with instructions to reinstate the trial court's judgment.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged the judgment of the Court of Appeal (California), which reversed the trial court's decision to permanently enjoin respondent adult bookstore owners from operating an "adult" business and granting appellant's request for a declaration that respondents' store constituted a public nuisance in violation of National City, Cal., Municipal Code § 18.69.030, which provision respondent claimed violated the U.S. Const. amend. I.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court, reversing the trial court's judgment in favor of appellant city, found that National City, Cal., Municipal Code § 18.69.030 was constitutional on its face but that, as applied, it failed to provide reasonable alternative avenues of communication. The court reinstated the trial court's judgment, concluding that the ordinance was a reasonable content-neutral time, place, and manner regulation that could survive a U.S. Const. amend I challenge, passing a two-part test: the ordinance was designed to serve a substantial governmental interest in reducing the secondary effects of "adult" businesses, relieving appellant from some of the regulatory burden by shifting it to the private sector and protecting the commercial tax base, and the ordinance allowed for reasonable alternative avenues of communication because shopping centers and abundant land for commercial development were available. The court further held that the U.S. Const. amend I inquiry was not concerned with economic impact, and even considerable economic difficulty in locating "adult" uses fell short of establishing that respondent adult bookstore owners were unreasonably restricted by the ordinance.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court and held that appellant city's ordinance was constitutional under the First Amendment because reasonable alternative avenues of communication existed. The matter was remanded with instructions to reinstate the trial court's judgment declaring respondent adult bookstore owners' store to be a public nuisance and permanently enjoining them from operating an "adult" business.

Woosley v. State of California


No. S014557


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


3 Cal. 4th 758; 838 P.2d 758; 13 Cal. Rptr. 2d 30; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 5058; 92 Daily Journal DAR 14528; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8788



October 26, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified on Denial of Rehearing December 31, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 6438.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. CA000499, Lester E. Olson, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the state of California, Department of Motor Vehicles (DMV), and the State Board of Equalization (SBE), filed a petition for review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) in a class action challenging appellants' practice of charging higher annual vehicle license fees and use taxes on passenger vehicles originally sold outside of California.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff filed a class action suit against appellant state, including the Department of Motor Vehicles (DMV) and the State Board of Equalization (SBE), for a refund of license fees and use taxes imposed by the DMV through its practice of charging higher vehicle license fees and use taxes on passenger vehicles originally purchased in other states. The trial court entered judgment in favor of plaintiff, and the court of appeals affirmed the judgment. The supreme court affirmed the decision of the court of appeals to the extent that the DMV violated the commerce clause of the U.S. Constitution by imposing higher license fees and use taxes on vehicles originally purchased outside of California. The court reversed the remainder of the decision of the court of appeals and held that that persons who paid use taxes, prior to when the DMV altered its practice of collecting use taxes based on the actual cost of a vehicle, were not entitled to refunds. Further, the court held that the class claim, as filed by plaintiff, was not authorized by statute. Therefore, the class on whose behalf the action was maintained included only persons who timely filed valid claims for refunds.


OUTCOME: In class action suit for refund of license fees and use taxes imposed by DMV, the court affirmed part of the decision of the court of appeals because the DMV violated the commerce clause of the U.S. Constitution by imposing higher license fees and use taxes on vehicles originally purchased outside of California. The court reversed the portion of the court of appeals' decision that held that persons who paid use taxes were entitled to refunds.

PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. CR-19889, John H. Barnard, Judge.

Penal Code § 187, and found to be true 12 multiple-murder, special-circumstance allegations under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(3).

At another hospital, a patient died while defendant was on duty after displaying the same symptoms as the patients at the previous hospital. Defendant was charged with murdering a total of 12 patients under Cal. Penal Code § 187. The prosecution argued that defendant had killed the patients by injecting them with massive overdoses of lidocaine. Defendant waived his right to a jury trial and the trial judge convicted him of 12 counts of first degree murder, found to be true 12 multiple-murder, special-circumstance allegations under Cal. The court affirmed defendant's conviction in other respects, but ordered that 11 of the 12 special circumstances be stricken as duplicative. The court held that substantial evidence supported the trial court's finding that an overdose of lidocaine caused the death of each of the 12 patients, and that in each case defendant administered the fatal dose.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's conviction of defendant for 12 counts of murder in the first degree. The court held that that substantial evidence supported the trial court's finding that an overdose of lidocaine caused the death of each of the 12 patients, and that in each case defendant administered the fatal dose. The court, however, ordered that 11 of the 12 special circumstances be stricken as duplicative.

Central Pathology Service Medical Clinic, Inc. v. Superior Court


No. S021168


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


3 Cal. 4th 181; 832 P.2d 924; 10 Cal. Rptr. 2d 208; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 3492; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6732; 92 Daily Journal DAR 10744



July 31, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied September 24, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 4744.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. NWC 054572, David A. Horowitz, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We conclude the Court of Appeal erred in summarily denying defendants' petition for a writ of mandate. Accordingly, the alternative writ of mandate is discharged, and the matter is remanded to the Court of Appeal with directions to issue a peremptory writ of mandate directing the trial court to vacate its order granting plaintiffs leave to amend their complaint and directing the trial court to conduct further proceedings in accordance with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, health care providers, challenged the order of the Court of Appeals (California) summarily denying their application for writ of mandate to compel respondent trial court to vacate its order holding Cal. Civ. Proc. Code 425.13 did not apply to restrict the punitive damages claim by plaintiff patients, real parties in interest. Real parties in interest brought suit against petitioners for medical malpractice and intentional torts.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff patients, here, real parties in interest, brought suit against petitioners, health care providers, for medical malpractice and intentional torts and sought punitive damages. The trial court held that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code 425.13 did not apply to restrict the claim for punitive damages and allowed real parties in interest to amend their complaint. The court of appeals summarily denied petitioner's application for writ of mandate to compel respondent trial court to vacate its order. The court issued an alternative writ of mandate and held that respondent trial court erred in allowing the amended complaint. The court held that real parties in interest, who sought punitive damages for an injury that was directly related to the professional services provided by a health care provider acting in its capacity as such, were required to comply with Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 425.13(a). The court held that the causes of action for fraud and intentional infliction of emotional distress directly related to the manner in which petitioners provided professional services.


OUTCOME: The court held that the court of appeals erred in summarily denying the request by petitioners, health care providers, for a writ of mandate. The court issued an alternative writ of mandate and held that the trial court erred in allowing patients, real parties in interest, to amend their complaint. Injury was directly related to the professional services provided by petitioners; therefore, punitive damages were barred.

People v. Clark

July 30, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by People v. Clark, 1992 Cal. LEXIS 4760 (Cal., Sept. 23, 1992)
Stay granted by People v. Clark, 1992 Cal. LEXIS 6380 (Cal., Dec. 30, 1992)
Writ of certiorari denied Clark v. California, 507 U.S. 993, 123 L. Ed. 2d 166, 113 S. Ct. 1604, 1993 U.S. LEXIS 2283 (1993)
Application granted by People v. Clark (Douglas Daniel), 2004 Cal. LEXIS 3071 (Cal., Apr. 14, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. A361671 and A377385, Ricardo A. Torres, Judge.
People v. Clark, 1992 Cal. LEXIS 3938 (Cal., July 30, 1992)



DISPOSITION: The attempted murder and mayhem convictions are reversed. All but one of the multiple-murder special-circumstance findings are set aside. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California), convicting him of six counts of first degree murder, Cal. Penal Code § 187; one count of mutilation of human remains, Cal. Health & Saf. Code § 7052); and attempted murder and mayhem, Cal. Penal Code § 187/664, 203; and sentencing him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder, Cal. Penal Code § 187; mutilation of human remains, Cal. Health & Saf. Code § 7052; and attempted murder and mayhem, Cal. Penal Code § 187/664, 203; and he was sentenced to death. Defendant appealed, arguing that the trial court erred in refusing to grant his request to act as co-counsel, that the trial court erred in denying his motion to suppress statements, and that the trial court erred in permitting posthypnotic testimony from a witness. The court ruled that defendant did not have a right both to be represented by counsel and to participate in the presentation of his own case, and that defendant did not unequivocally assert his right to self-representation. Further, the court held a desire to have an attorney in the future, coupled with an unambiguous willingness to talk in the meantime, was not an invocation of the right to counsel requiring cessation of a police interrogation. The court affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded the judgment, concluding that testimony from a witness who underwent hypnosis for the purpose of restoring his memory was inadmissible as to all matters relating to those events.


OUTCOME: Judgment convicting and sentencing defendant was affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded where the trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying defendant access to a law library, defendant failed to demonstrate that his right to assistance of counsel would have been impaired if his motion to replace his court-appointed attorney was denied, and the admission of testimony by a witness who underwent hypnosis was improper.

CASE SUMMARY:

OVERVIEW: Appellant was sentenced to death for his conviction of a murder committed during a robbery of a victim at a drive-through restaurant. The court concluded that the evidence supported the jury's verdict that appellant killed in order to advance the purpose of robbery.

People v. DeSantis


No. S004706, Crim. No. 25187


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA

Penal Code §§ 190.2(a)(17)(vii) and (b). Appellant was sentenced to death on the murder charge and prison terms on the other counts.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was sentenced to death for the premeditated murder of a victim during a burglary. Appellant appeared at the home of the victims posing as a telephone repairman and subsequently shot both the husband and wife and took personal property and the couple's vehicle. The court affirmed the trial court judgment in its entirety.

Moore v. California State Bd. of Accountancy


No. S017399


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


2 Cal. 4th 999; 831 P.2d 798; 9 Cal. Rptr. 2d 358; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 3044; 92 Daily Journal DAR 9506; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5942



July 2, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied August 26, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 4391.
Mosk, J., Kennard, J., and George, J. are of the opinion the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 863037, Thomas J. Dandurand, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, an unlicensed individual and associated others, sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which granted respondent state board of accountancy's request for a permanent injunction enjoining appellants from using the words "account", "accounting", or "accounting services" in referring to themselves in violation of the Accountancy Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code, § 5058.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, an unlicensed individual and associated others, filed suit against respondent state board of accountancy seeking declaratory relief that respondent could not enjoin appellants from using the terms "accountant" or "accounting" when referring to themselves or their businesses, and for a permanent injunction ordering respondent to cease all enforcement action against the use of those terms. The trial court denied relief to appellants and granted respondent's request for a permanent injunction, and the lower appellate court affirmed. Affirming the lower appellate court's judgment, the court concluded that the adoption and enforcement of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 5058 was a proper exercise of respondent's authority to administer the Accountancy Act. The court further concluded that the regulatory scheme could constitutionally ban only those uses of the terms "accountant" and "accounting" that might potentially mislead the public regarding the user's licensee status. However, where the terms were used in conjunction with a modifier or modifiers that serve to dispel any possibility of confusion, their use in such a context could not be constitutionally enjoined.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower appellate court's judgment granting respondent state board of accountancy's request for a permanent injunction because the statute could constitutionally ban those uses of the generic terms "accountant" and "accounting" that stood to potentially mislead the public regarding the user's licensee or nonlicensee status.

Habeas corpus proceeding at, Request granted Raley (David) on H.C., 2010 Cal. LEXIS 11913 (Cal., Oct. 15, 2010)

People v. Raley, 1992 Cal. LEXIS 3148 (Cal., June 18, 1992)



DISPOSITION: The conviction for attempted oral copulation is reversed, and the judgment in all other respects is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant's appeal of his convictions was automatic after the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) fixed his penalty at death after convicting him of first degree murder with personal use of a deadly weapon, attempted murder, and attempted oral copulation by force.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder with personal use of a deadly weapon with special circumstance allegations of murder in the commission of a kidnapping and torture murder. He was also found guilty of attempted murder, and the trial court found that he personally used a deadly weapon and inflicted great bodily injury. The trial court also convicted defendant of attempted oral copulation by force with use of a deadly weapon, oral copulation by force with use of a deadly weapon and great bodily injury, and two counts of kidnapping. Defendant received the death penalty, and his appeal was automatic. Defendant challenged his conviction of attempted oral copulation based on sufficiency of evidence. The court reversed defendant's conviction for attempted oral copulation, but otherwise affirmed the judgment, finding that the evidence, which was independently admissible as relevant to the question of defendant's guilt for the other charged crimes, was not sufficient to support the verdict on the count of attempted oral copulation. The court also found that it was not reasonably probable that in the absence of the error, the result would have been more favorable to defendant.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for attempted oral copulation was reversed, but his convictions for murder and attempted murder and his death sentence were affirmed. The evidence was insufficient to support the conviction on the count of attempted oral copulation, but it was not reasonably probable that in the absence of the error, the result would have been more favorable to defendant.

226 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Barclays Bank Internat., Ltd. v. Franchise Tax Bd.


No. S019064


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


2 Cal. 4th 708; 829 P.2d 279; 8 Cal. Rptr. 2d 31; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 1975; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4032; 92 Daily Journal DAR 6327



May 11, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sacramento County, Nos. 325059 and 352061, George E. Paras, Judge. * 


* Retired Associate Justice of the Court of Appeal, Third District, sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed and the cause is remanded to that court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Franchise Tax Board challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), and affirmed by the Court of Appeal that held that California's formula apportionment method of taxation was unconstitutional as applied to foreign-based unitary groups.


OVERVIEW: Respondent taxpayers brought a refund action to recover assessments levied against them by appellant Franchise Tax Board for the 1977 tax year. The assessments were based on a finding that, together with respondents' United Kingdom-based corporate parent and worldwide subsidiaries, respondents comprised a unitary enterprise subjecting it to appellant's three-factor mathematical formula to apportion the interjurisdictional income of a unitary business for state corporate income tax purposes. The appellate court and the trial court held that the formula apportionment method violated the foreign commerce clause and was unconstitutional as applied to foreign-based unitary groups. The court concluded that, although executive officials had repeatedly attempted to prohibit formula apportionment, the United States Congress had repeatedly refused to do so. The court held that there was a clear federal intent not to prohibit the states from employing formula apportionment in taxing multinational businesses. The court held that the question as to whether formula apportionment violated the due process clause was a factual question to be determined by the appellate court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court and concluded the employing of formula apportionment by the state in taxing the income of multinational unitary businesses did not violate the foreign commerce clause. The court remanded to the appellate court to determine if the cost of producing the required financial data for formula apportionment violated respondent taxpayers' due process rights.

 

227 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



DaFonte v. Up-Right, Inc.


No. S022331


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


2 Cal. 4th 593; 828 P.2d 140; 7 Cal. Rptr. 2d 238; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 1783; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3839; 92 Daily Journal DAR 5961; 57 Cal. Comp. Cases 345



May 4, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 383534-5, James L. Quaschnick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal, Fifth Appellate District, is reversed, and the cause is remanded to that court with directions, after briefing and argument by the parties, to determine the amount of the judgment against defendant Up-Right, Inc., in light of the principles herein expressed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant manufacturer appealed a decision from the Court of Appeal, Fifth Appellate District (California), which entered a modified judgment against defendant in an action brought by plaintiff employee for injuries suffered to his arm by a machine made by defendant.


OVERVIEW: The employee was seriously injured when his arm was drawn into the moving conveyor belt of a mechanical grape harvester he was attempting to clean by hand. Workers' compensation paid the employee benefits. The employee filed an action against the manufacturer on theories of negligence and product defect. The jury reduced total damages by 15 percent, the amount attributable to the employee's comparative fault. Both parties appealed the decision. The appellate court dismissed the manufacturer's evidentiary and instructional claims and entered a modified judgment. On further appeal, Cal. Civ. Code § 1431.2 plainly limited a defendant's share of noneconomic damages to his or her own proportionate share of comparative fault. The court held that the statute stated or implied no exception for third party suits by injured employees. The court concluded that the plain language of § 1431.2 eliminated a third-party defendant's joint and several liability to an injured employee for unpaid noneconomic damages attributable to the fault of the employer, who was statutorily immune from suit. The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court and remanded with instructions.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's judgment and remanded the case with instructions because the statute stated or implied no exception for third-party suits by injured employees.

San Francisco Taxpayers Assn. v. Board of Supervisors


No. S018200


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


2 Cal. 4th 571; 828 P.2d 147; 7 Cal. Rptr. 2d 245; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 1834; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3842; 92 Daily Journal DAR 5944



May 4, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 901018, Raymond J. Arata, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer association sought the review of the order of the Court of Civil Appeals (California), which reversed the order of the lower court, which granted appellant's motion for summary judgment and held respondent city and county's actions in not placing spending limitations on retirement contributions pursuant to Cal. Const. amend. XIIIB, § 5, invalid.


OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayer association filed a suit to challenge respondent city and county's exclusion of retirement contributions from the spending limitations placed on their budgets. Appellant argued that the exclusion violated Cal. Cal. Const. amend. XIIIB, § 5. The lower court granted appellant's motion for summary judgment and held that respondents' exclusion of the retirement contributions was invalid; however, the appellate court reversed. The court reversed the order of the appellate court, holding that the plain language of § 5 clearly placed spending limitations on retirement contributions. The court further held that Cal. Cal. Const. amend. XIIIB, § 9, which excluded appropriations for debt service, could not have been construed to include retirement benefits. Finally, the court held that the spending limitations placed on respondents were not of such a significant nature as to violate respondents' rights under the contracts clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 10.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the appellate court, which reversed the lower court's granting of appellant tax payer association's motion for summary judgment in a suit to challenge respondent city and county's actions in not placing spending limitations on retirement contributions; the plain language of the state constitution placed spending limitations on retirement contributions.

The court affirmed defendant's other convictions and his death sentence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction for the murder of the correctional officer and the resulting life imprisonment sentence because the jury was incorrectly instructed regarding proximate causation. The court affirmed defendant's other convictions and his death sentence.

 

231 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Arcadia Unified School Dist. v. State Dept. of Education


No. S021288


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


2 Cal. 4th 251; 825 P.2d 438; 5 Cal. Rptr. 2d 545; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 969; 92 Daily Journal DAR 3578; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2280



March 16, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 361147, Michael J. Virga, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For the foregoing reasons, we conclude that, on its face, section 39807.5 does not violate California's free school guarantee or equal protection clause. Therefore, the judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school districts disputed a decision of the Court of Appeal, Second District, Division Six (California), which reversed a trial court's judgment for respondent State Department of Education. The issues were whether appellants were collaterally estopped and whether Cal. Educ. Code § 39807.5, on its face, violated the free school guarantee, Cal. Const. art. IX, § 5, or the equal protection clause, Cal. Const. art. I, § 7(a).


OVERVIEW: Twenty-five appellant school districts and respondent State Department of Education submitted a stipulated statement of facts for judgment to the superior court. Respondent intervenor's motion to dismiss was denied. The superior court ruled that Cal. Educ. Code § 39807.5, which allowed appellants to charge a fee for school transportation, facially violated the free school guarantee, Cal. Const. art. IX, § 5. The court of appeal reversed, and appellants challenged the reversal. On appeal, the court held that, even if collateral estoppel applied, the case was within the public interest exception. If estoppel were allowed to bar the action, the constitutionality of the statute would never again be litigated. The court held that the guarantee required that educational activities be free. School transportation was a noneducational activity, so the statute did not violate the guarantee. The statute did not violate the equal protection clause, Cal. Const. art. I, § 7(a), because no child would be denied transportation to school based on poverty. The court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed. Appellant school districts were not collaterally estopped. Even if collateral estoppel applied, the case was within the public interest exception to the doctrine. Because the providing of school transportation was a noneducational activity, the statute did not violate the free school guarantee. The statute did not violate the equal protection clause, because no child would be denied transportation due to poverty.

2 Cal. 4th 86; 825 P.2d 781; 5 Cal. Rptr. 2d 796; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 974; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2166; 92 Daily Journal DAR 3841



March 12, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Modification and Rehearing Denied May 14, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 2443.

Habeas corpus proceeding at In re Hardy, 1992 Cal. LEXIS 3059 (Cal., June 17, 1992)
Writ of certiorari denied Reilly v. California, 506 U.S. 1056, 113 S. Ct. 987, 122 L. Ed. 2d 139, 1993 U.S. LEXIS 174 (1993)
Habeas corpus proceeding at Hardy (James Edward) on H.C., 2007 Cal. LEXIS 4122 (Cal., Apr. 18, 2007)
Writ of habeas corpus granted, in part, Writ of habeas corpus denied, in part In re Hardy, 41 Cal. 4th 977, 63 Cal. Rptr. 3d 845, 163 P.3d 853, 2007 Cal. LEXIS 7881 (2007)
Motion granted by People v. Hardy (James Edward) & Reilly (Mark Anthony), 2012 Cal. LEXIS 11342 (Cal., Nov. 20, 2012)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Hardy v. Martel, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 89277 (C.D. Cal., June 7, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A148767, Robert D. Fratianne, Judge.
People v. Hardy, 1989 Cal. LEXIS 1363 (Cal., Mar. 30, 1989)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted them of two counts of first-degree murder, Cal. Penal Code § 187, and of conspiracy to commit murder to collect life insurance proceeds, Cal. Penal Code § 182, and sentenced them to death. The jury sustained six of eight charged special-circumstance allegations for each defendant.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were sentenced to death after being convicted of two counts of first-degree murder, Cal. Penal Code § 187, and of conspiracy to commit murder to collect life insurance proceeds, Cal. Penal Code § 182. On appeal, the court held that the trial court's finding of a very broad conspiracy, involving several persons and continuing up through the trial, was proper. Therefore, admission of much hearsay evidence was proper as admissions of co-conspirators. Any errors in that regard were harmless, due to the overwhelming evidence of guilt. The fact that one defendant sued his attorney during trial did not automatically create a conflict of interest. Comments by the prosecutor and by one defendant's attorney as to a defendant's failure to testify were harmless beyond a reasonable doubt. Jury instructions were proper. In particular, instructions during the penalty phase concerning the aggravating and mitigating factors under Cal. Penal Code § 190.3 were proper. The court struck one of the multiple-murder special-circumstances findings for each defendant as duplicative. Otherwise, the court affirmed the judgment in both the guilt phase and the penalty phase of the trial.


OUTCOME: One of the multiple-murder special-circumstances findings for each defendant was struck as duplicative. Otherwise, defendants' convictions and sentences for first-degree murder and conspiracy to commit murder were affirmed. The finding of a very broad conspiracy was proper. Therefore, admission of much hearsay evidence was proper as admissions made by co-conspirators. Also, instructions concerning the death penalty factors were proper.

People v. Visciotti


No. S004597, Crim. No. 23385


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


2 Cal. 4th 1; 825 P.2d 388; 5 Cal. Rptr. 2d 495; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 968; 92 Daily Journal DAR 3468; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2199



March 12, 1992, Decided  
March 12, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied April 29, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 2132. Mosk. J., is of the opinion the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-50770, Robert R. Fitzgerald, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which convicted him of first-degree murder under Cal. Penal Code §§ 189, 187; attempted murder under Cal. Penal Code § 664/187; and robbery under Cal. Penal Code § 211 and sentenced him to death. The jury found that the murder was committed under the special circumstance of murder in the commission of robbery, and that defendant had personally used a firearm.


OVERVIEW: Appellant lured two former coworkers to a deserted area to rob them. After taking their money, he shot them both, killing one. Appellant was convicted of first-degree murder, attempted murder, and robbery and was sentenced to death. Appellant challenged the judgment on several grounds. He alleged that he committed the crime under the influence of drugs and that he had not planned the murder. He also contended that the trial court expressed a doubt as to his sanity, but did not hold a hearing to determine his competence. Further, he alleged that the jury selection process was defective, thereby constitutionally invalid. The court affirmed, finding that the record did not show that the judge expressed a doubt as to appellant's competence, but merely indicated that experts should be appointed. Further, the court found that intoxication was not a defense. The court found that the jury selection process was conducted so as to protect the right to a randomly selected jury. Finally, the court ruled that the trial court properly conducted a Hovey voir dire, and appellant's absence during some stages of jury selection was not reversible error where he had waived his right to be present.


OUTCOME: Appellant's conviction and sentence for first-degree murder, attempted murder, and robbery were affirmed. The court held that the evidence showed premeditation, that intoxication was not a defense, that there were aggravating circumstances and no overriding mitigating circumstances, that the jury selection process resulted in a random jury, and that the trial court properly conducted a Hovey voir dire.

People v. Hansel


No. S020080


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


1 Cal. 4th 1211; 824 P.2d 694; 4 Cal. Rptr. 2d 888; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 534; 92 Daily Journal DAR 2716



February 27, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-74679, Jean M. Rheinheimer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed. The cause is remanded to the Court of Appeal, which shall direct the superior court to conduct a suppression hearing in a manner consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state challenged a ruling of the court of appeals, which affirmed a ruling of the Superior Court of Orange County (California) and granted defendants' motion to suppress the narcotics found in defendants' home based on a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 1531.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged with various possession of narcotics crimes after a search of defendants' home pursuant to a search warrant resulted in the finding of multiple forms of narcotics. Defendants filed a motion to suppress the drug evidence based on an alleged violation of Cal. Penal Code § 1531 when the warrant was executed. The motion was denied following a preliminary hearing at which defendant called no witnesses and the state called only one of the arresting officers. Defendants renewed their motion prior to trial, and the trial court refused to allow the state to recall the same arresting officer because defendant presented no new evidence. The trial court granted defendants' motion to suppress and the appellate court affirmed. The state challenged these ruling and, on appeal, the Supreme Court of California reversed. That court held that Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5(i) provided the state an unequivocal right to recall witnesses at special hearings on motions to suppress, regardless of whether any new evidence was presented by defendants. The action was remanded for a new trial court suppression hearing.


OUTCOME: The Supreme Court of California reversed the court of appeals ruling, which affirmed a ruling of the Superior Court of Orange County and granted defendants' motion to suppress narcotics evidence. The court held that California law provided the state a right to recall the witnesses called at the previous suppression hearing regardless of whether defendants presented any new evidence.

People v. Howard

Del Monte v. Wilson


S021805


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


1 Cal. 4th 1009; 824 P.2d 632; 4 Cal. Rptr. 2d 826; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 1271; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1519; 92 Daily Journal DAR 2438



February 24, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Ct.App. B040513. Super.Ct. No. CA 001096, Los Angeles County Superior Court


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, governor and California Department of Veterans Affairs, petitioned for review of the judgment of the Court of Appeal (California), that issued a peremptory writ of mandate in favor of plaintiff military veterans and found the restriction of veterans' benefits provided under Cal. Mil. & Vet. Code §§ 890, 980 to persons who were California residents at the time they entered military service violated U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff military veterans petitioned for a writ of mandate because the restriction of veterans' benefits to persons who were California residents at the time they joined the military service under Cal. Mil. & Vet. Code §§ 890 and 980 was a violation of U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The trial court denied the petition and rejected plaintiffs' constitutional claims. The appellate court reversed and remanded with directions to issue the peremptory writ of mandate because the distinction drawn by the statutes violated the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The supreme court found that the benefits distributed pursuant to §§ 890 and 980 were available only to a certain class of resident veterans, those who were California natives or residents at the time they entered active military service. Other resident veterans, who were not state residents when they entered the service, were permanently denied benefits. The supreme court affirmed the appellate court decision and remanded for further proceedings because the statutory scheme was not rationally related to achieving a legitimate state purpose, and hence violated the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the appellate court judgment in favor of plaintiff military veterans because the restriction of state veterans' benefits to persons who were California residents at the time they entered military service was not rationally related to achieving a legitimate state purpose, and violated the federal equal protection clause. The supreme court remanded for further proceedings.
