Lusardi Construction Co. v. Aubry


S011121


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


1 Cal. 4th 976; 824 P.2d 643; 4 Cal. Rptr. 2d 837; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 1270; 30 Wage & Hour Cas. (BNA) 1281; 123 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P57,112



February 24, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: CA-4/1 D007559. San Diego County Superior Court. 579903


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, director of the department of industrial relations, challenged the decision of the Court of Appeal (California), which affirmed the order of the San Diego County Superior Court that appellant could not enforce the prevailing wage law against appellee contractor. Appellee had entered into a contract for the expansion of a public hospital and relied on the hospital's representation that the project was private.


OVERVIEW: A public hospital district (district) entered into a written agreement with a private corporation for a construction project. Under the agreement, the district was appointed as the corporation's agent for all purposes respecting the construction. The district represented to appellee contractor that the expansion project was a private work and not a public work under the prevailing wage law, and appellee entered into a contract for the construction of the expansion project. Appellant, director of the department of industrial relations, informed appellee that the expansion project was a public work. Appellee sued appellant to challenge enforcement of a prevailing wage law. The trial court issued a temporary restraining order and a preliminary injunction restraining appellant from enforcing or attempting to enforce the law, and granted appellee's motion for summary judgment. The appellate court affirmed. The supreme court reversed, holding that the statutory obligation to pay the prevailing wage did not depend on appellee's assent, but appellee may be entitled to indemnity if it reasonably relied on the district's representations that the project was not a public project.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the judgment affirming the trial court's decision to enjoin enforcement of a prevailing wage law against appellee contractor. The supreme court held that the statutory obligation to pay the prevailing wage did not depend on appellee's assent, and that appellant, director of the department of industrial relations, could validly and constitutionally determine that a given project was for a public work.

People v. Pinholster


No. S004616, Crim. No. 23781.

239 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Adoption of Kelsey S.


No. S014775


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


1 Cal. 4th 816; 823 P.2d 1216; 4 Cal. Rptr. 2d 615; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 530; 92 Daily Journal DAR 2629; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1403



February 20, 1992, Decided 
February 20, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] (Ct. of Appeal H005150) (Super. Ct. Nos. A9982 & A9993)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner father appealed from the judgment of the California court of appeal, which affirmed a trial court's ruling that petitioner was not a presumed father, and found by a bare preponderance of the evidence that the child's best interest required termination of petitioner's parental rights.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, father of a child born out of wedlock, sought custody of his child. The trial court awarded temporary custody of the child to its mother and ordered the mother to live with the child in a shelter for unwed mothers. The trial court ruled that petitioner was not a presumed father within the meaning of Cal. Civ. Code § 7004(a)(4). The trial court found by a bare preponderance of the evidence that the child's best interest required termination of petitioner's parental rights. The intermediate appellate court affirmed the judgment. On appeal, the court recognized that Cal. Civ. Code § 7004(a)(4) did not provide petitioner presumed father status based on his unsuccessful attempts to obtain custody over the mother's objection. The court reversed judgment, however, holding that § 7004(a) violated petitioner's equal protection and due process rights to the extent that it allowed a mother unilaterally to preclude her child's biological father from becoming a presumed father and thereby allowed the state to terminate his parental rights on nothing more than a showing of the child's best interest.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment terminating petitioner father's parental rights to his child, holding that the federal constitutional guarantees of equal protection and due process required that petitioner be allowed to withhold his consent to his child's adoption and therefore that his parental rights could not be terminated absent a showing of his unfitness.

Southern Cal. Rapid Transit Dist. v. Bolen


No. S015986


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


1 Cal. 4th 654; 822 P.2d 875; 3 Cal. Rptr. 2d 843; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 8; 92 Daily Journal DAR 1497; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 930

Mosk, J. and Kennard, J., are of the opinion the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C656503, Billy G. Mills, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff transit district sought review of the judgment of the California Court of Appeal, which declared unconstitutional a special benefit assessment district voting scheme that premised its franchise on realty ownership and allotted votes based on the assessed value of the realty.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff transit district filed suit to validate a special benefit assessment district voting scheme that premised the right to vote in the underlying referendum election upon realty ownership and allotted votes based on the assessed realty value. Defendants, transit district secretary and intervenors, asserted that the scheme violated U.S. Const. amend. XIV equal protection guarantees. Defendants failed in the trial court and appealed. The appellate court held that the voting scheme contravened the constitutional "one person, one vote" rule. On appeal, the court reversed, finding that the voting scheme did not require "strict scrutiny" analysis for two reasons. First, the benefit districts lacked indicia of "general governmental powers" and were therefore special-purpose governmental units exempt from the "one person, one vote" rule. Second, the class that the scheme enfranchised was "primarily affected" by or "primarily interested" in the election, while those excluded were in fact substantially less affected or interested. The enfranchisement and vote allocation rules passed "rational basis" review because a state of facts reasonably could be conceived to justify them.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that plaintiff transit district's special benefit assessment district voting scheme violated the "one person, one vote" rule because plaintiff was a special-purpose governmental unit exempt from the rule and the scheme permissibly enfranchised those who were primarily affected by or interested in the underlying election.

 

241 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Wilson v. Eu


No. S022835


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


1 Cal. 4th 707; 823 P.2d 545; 4 Cal. Rptr. 2d 379; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 6; 92 Daily Journal DAR 1267; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 786



January 27, 1992, Decided  
January 27, 1992, Filed



DISPOSITION:  As in Legislature v. Reinecke, supra, 10 Cal.3d at page 407, we have no reason to believe any of the parties to these proceedings will fail to accede to our holding herein, and accordingly no purpose is served by issuing a writ of mandate. The alternative writ of mandate heretofore issued is discharged, and the petition for writ of mandate denied. Each party will bear its own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner governor requested a writ to resolve an impasse created by the failure of petitioner, respondent secretary of state, and real parties in interest California legislature, to adopt congressional, legislative, and State Board of Equalization reapportionment plans in time for the 1992 primary and general elections. The court exercised its original jurisdiction, issued an alternative writ, and appointed masters who submitted a report.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner governor sought a writ of mandate to order apportionment of districts by respondent secretary of state and real parties in interest California legislature before the 1992 primary and general elections. The court ordered three masters to prepare a reapportionment plan because it lacked assurance that the plan would have been validly enacted in time for the 1992 elections. The court approved the masters' report and recommendations and denied the petition for a writ of mandate. The court found that each legislative and congressional district varied by less than one percent and 0.25 percent, respectively, from "ideal" population equality, and that these minor deviations were justified by legitimate state objectives of forming reasonably compact districts, which was part of the 1973 reapportionment criteria outlined by the court in order to comply with the Voting Rights Act of 1965 (VRA), 42 U.S.C.S. § 1971 et seq. The court held that the VRA authorized deliberate construction of minority controlled voting districts, thereby approving a new district with a combined minority population in excess of 85 percent.


OUTCOME: The court adopted the masters' reapportionment plans with minor modifications because the lines drawn in the plan adhered to the legal criteria under federal and state law. The court did not have reason to believe that petitioner governor, respondent secretary of state, and real parties interest California legislature, would not adhere to its holding, so it denied the petition for a writ of mandate and discharged the alternative writ of mandate.

People v. Kelly


No. S005092


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


1 Cal. 4th 495; 822 P.2d 385; 3 Cal. Rptr. 2d 677; 1992 Cal. LEXIS 1; 92 Daily Journal DAR 846; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 520



January 16, 1992, Decided  
January 16, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied March 11, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. SCR 45500, Duke D. Rouse, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The rape conviction is reduced to attempted rape. The robbery conviction, the related firearm-use enhancement, and the robbery-murder special circumstance are reversed. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant automatically appealed from a judgment of death imposed by the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California) due to defendant's conviction of two counts of first degree murder, one count of rape, one count of attempted rape, and one count of robbery, all specially enhanced due to defendant's use of a firearm during their commission.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of two counts of first degree murder, rape, attempted rape, and robbery of one of the victims. Defendant's sentence was enhanced because the jury found he used a firearm to commit the crimes. Because defendant was condemned to death, an appeal automatically ensued. The court affirmed the sentence adjudged but took action on defendant's rape and robbery convictions. The court reduced the rape conviction to attempted rape because the trial court erroneously instructed the jury that it was legally possible for defendant to rape a dead body, an error that the court concluded was not harmless beyond a reasonable doubt. The court reversed defendant's robbery conviction, its related firearm-use enhancement, and the robbery special circumstance because the trial court did not instruct the jury on the lesser included offense of theft despite evidence defendant formed his intent to steal after the death of the victim. The court found that the presence of after-formed intent evidence imposed on the trial court a sua sponte duty to instruct on theft as a lesser included offense. Errors of instruction and argument on defendant's other crimes were harmless.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions of two counts of first degree murder and attempted rape, and affirmed the death penalty imposed, but reduced defendant's conviction for rape to attempted rape because of a prejudicially erroneous jury instruction. The court reversed defendant's robbery conviction due to the prejudicially erroneous omission of a lesser included offense instruction.

People v. Breaux


No. S004760, Crim. No. 26206.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


1 Cal. 4th 281; 821 P.2d 585; 3 Cal. Rptr. 2d 81; 1991 Cal. LEXIS 5682; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 87; 92 Daily Journal DAR 58



December 30, 1991, Decided  
December 30, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied February 26, 1992.
Rehearing denied by People v. Breaux, 1992 Cal. LEXIS 1977 (Cal., Feb. 26, 1992)
Writ of certiorari denied Breaux v. California, 506 U.S. 873, 506 U.S. 874, 113 S. Ct. 214, 121 L. Ed. 2d 153, 1992 U.S. LEXIS 5439 (1992)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Breaux v. Warden, San Quentin State Prison, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 95428 (E.D. Cal., July 9, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 71072, Fred W. Marler, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from his convictions for murder, Cal. Penal Code § 187, robbery, Cal. Penal Code § 211, kidnapping for robbery, Cal. Penal Code § 209, assault with a deadly weapon on a peace officer, Cal. Penal Code § 245, and being an ex-felon in possession of a firearm, Cal. Penal Code § 12021, as well as a death sentence from the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder, robbery, kidnapping for robbery, assault with a deadly weapon on a police officer, and being an ex-felon in possession of a firearm after he robbed a gas station and kidnapped a woman whom he later killed. Defendant was sentenced to death. On appeal, the court affirmed. Defendant contended that the trial court abused its discretion when it denied his motion to recuse the prosecutor for a conflict of interest. Defendant alleged that the prosecutor's wife and the victim were acquaintances. For a motion to recuse to be granted, the conflict of interest had to be of such gravity that it was unlikely defendant would receive a fair trial unless recusal was ordered. The trial court correctly found no personal interests in the case that would create the likelihood of an unfair trial. Therefore, the motion was properly denied. Defendant also alleged that the jury venire did not represent a fair cross-section of the community. Defendant failed to prove a prima facie violation of the fair cross-section requirement because he did not show that the underrepresentation of a distinctive group was due to systematic exclusion in the jury-selection process.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for murder, robbery, kidnapping for robbery, assault with a deadly weapon on a police officer, and being an ex-felon in possession of a firearm and his sentence to be put to death were affirmed. The trial court did not abuse its discretion when it denied defendant's motion for recusal of the prosecutor because there was no conflict of interest depriving defendant of his right to a fair trial.

Bowens v. Superior Court


No. S019774


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


1 Cal. 4th 36; 820 P.2d 600; 2 Cal. Rptr. 2d 376; 1991 Cal. LEXIS 5671; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9917; 91 Daily Journal DAR 15725



December 19, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 105702, William A. McKinstry, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the alternative writ of mandate issued by this court on May 1, 1991, is discharged, and the peremptory writ is denied. The order of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, an indicted defendant, challenged the Court of Appeal (California)'s denial of his application for a writ of mandate and prohibition after respondent, the Superior Court of Alameda County, pursuant to Cal. Const. art. I, § 14.1, denied his motion for a postindictment preliminary hearing.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, a defendant indicted in California, made application for a writ of mandate and prohibition after respondent, the Superior Court of Alameda County, pursuant to Cal. Const. art. I, § 14.1, denied his motion for a postindictment preliminary hearing. The court of appeal denied his application, and he sought review. The court held that upon the June 5, 1990 adoption of an initiative measure designated on the ballot as Proposition 115, identified as the "Crime Victims Justice Reform Act," and enacted as Cal. Const. art. I, §14.1, an indicted defendant was not entitled to, and indeed could not receive, a postindictment preliminary hearing or any other similar procedure in the courts of California. Accordingly, the alternative writ of mandate issued by the court was discharged, and the peremptory writ was denied. The court affirmed the order of the court of appeal.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that upon enactment of Cal. Const. art. I, §14.1, petitioner, an indicted defendant, was not entitled to, and could not be afforded, a postindictment preliminary hearing. The alternative writ of mandate was therefore discharged, and the order of the court of appeal was affirmed.

Rider v. County of San Diego


No. S017917


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


1 Cal. 4th 1; 820 P.2d 1000; 2 Cal. Rptr. 2d 490; 1991 Cal. LEXIS 5808; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9927; 91 Daily Journal DAR 15689



December 19, 1991, Decided

PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. 194690, Gordon R. Burkhart, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal validating the Agency's tax levy is reversed and that court is directed to resolve any remaining appellate contentions of the Agency and the County consistent with our opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff county taxpayers sought review of the judgment of the Court of Appeal (California) in favor of defendant county and others in its reversal of the trial court order in plaintiffs' favor that ruled a county sales tax invalid because it violated § 4 of Proposition 13.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff county taxpayers filed a suit challenging the validity of a tax imposed on sales occurring in the county because the tax violated the supermajority vote requirements of Cal. OUTCOME: The court concluded the county sales tax at issue was invalid because it was not approved by at least two-thirds of the county's voters. The judgment of the court of appeal validating the tax levy was reversed and that court was directed to resolve any remaining appellate contentions.

 

246 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Bacigalupo


No. S004764, Crim. No. 26404.


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


1 Cal. 4th 103; 820 P.2d 559; 2 Cal. Rptr. 2d 335; 1991 Cal. LEXIS 5500; 91 Daily Journal DAR 15109



December 9, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified January 30, 1992.
Rehearing Denied January 30, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 93351, Thomas C. Hastings, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant automatically appealed his conviction and death penalty in the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) for two counts of first degree murder and two counts of robbery, with special circumstances of multiple murder and robbery-murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant robbed and shot to death two brothers for whom he worked at a jewelry store. Defendant confessed to the crimes after a warrantless arrest at his mother's house. A jury convicted defendant of two counts of first degree murder and two counts of robbery, with multiple-murder and robbery-murder special circumstances, sentencing defendant to death. The court affirmed the judgment in its entirety. Exigent circumstances justified defendant's warrantless arrest. Defendant's mistaken belief he was acting under duress did not establish an imperfect duress defense to robbery. The mistake failed to negate the specific intent element. Admission of defendant's prior crimes in another state, which were dismissed incident to a plea bargain, was proper because the truth or falsity of the crimes had not been adjudicated within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 190.3. Under Cal. Penal Code § 190.3(b), evidence of criminal violence that had occurred at any time in the defendant's life was admissible. Defendant's attack against the 1978 death penalty sentencing scheme failed because U.S. Const. amend. VI did not require jury unanimity on the sentencing factors underlying its chosen penalty.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of defendant's convictions for first degree murder and robbery and the death penalty imposed because his warrantless arrest, confession, convictions, and death sentence were proper under the controlling constitutional and statutory provisions.

October 31, 1991

Rehearing denied by People v. Mickey, 1992 Cal. LEXIS 19 (Cal., Jan. 8, 1992)
Stay granted by People v. Mickey, 1992 Cal. LEXIS 1535 (Cal., Apr. 1, 1992)
Writ of certiorari denied Mickey v. California, 506 U.S. 819, 113 S. Ct. 65, 121 L. Ed. 2d 32, 1992 U.S. LEXIS 5621 (1992)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, in part, Writ of habeas corpus granted, in part, Remanded by, Certificate of appealability granted, in part Mickey v. Ayers, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 86717 (N.D. Cal., Nov. 17, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. C-11727, John F. Cruikshank, Jr., Judge.
People v. Mickey, 1990 Cal. LEXIS 1146 (Cal., Mar. 21, 1990)



DISPOSITION: For the reasons stated above, we set aside one of the multiple-murder special-circumstance findings and both of the intentional-murder-for-financial-gain special-circumstance findings. We affirm the judgment in all other respects. It is so ordered.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On automatic appeal, defendant sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), which sentenced him to death under Cal. Penal Code § 190 et seq., on two counts of first degree murder under four special circumstances: multiple murder, intentional murder for financial gain, felony-murder-robbery, and felony-murder-burglary.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and death sentence on two counts of first degree murder. The court set aside one multiple-murder special-circumstance finding under Cal. Penal Code § 190 et seq., because one case could only support one such finding as a matter of law. The court set aside both intentional-murder-for-financial-gain special-circumstance findings because the deaths were not consideration for or prerequisites to financial gain. Defendant fled to Tokyo after the murders. On his return flight to the United States and during a layover in Honolulu, defendant made certain admissions to the law enforcement officers accompanying him. The court found that the inflight and Honolulu admissions were voluntary under U.S. Const. XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, §§ 7, 15, and Miranda because the officers did not intend to "soften" him up, there was no custodial interrogation on the flight, there was no coercive activity by the state, there was no violation of the Edwards rule, and defendant waived his Miranda rights in Honolulu. Defendant's letters to his wife were not privileged because he believed that the authorities were reading his mail and had no privacy expectations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions and death sentence for first degree murder, but set aside one of the multiple-murder special-circumstance findings and both of the intentional-murder-for-financial-gain special-circumstance findings. Miranda did not apply to inflight admissions because there was no custodial interrogation. Spousal privilege did not protect letters because defendant believed authorities were reading his mail.

Legislature v. Eu


No. S019660


Supreme Court of California


54 Cal. 3d 492; 816 P.2d 1309; 286 Cal. Rptr. 283; 1991 Cal. LEXIS 4529; 91 Daily Journal DAR 12510; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8160



October 10, 1991 



DISPOSITION:  We conclude that, except for the restriction on pensions of incumbent legislators, Proposition 140 is constitutionally valid. The petition for a peremptory writ of mandate is granted to the extent it seeks to compel respondents to refrain from enforcing section 4 of Proposition 140 as applied to incumbent legislators. In all other respects, the petition for mandate is denied. The temporary stay issued on June 14, 1991, is vacated. The parties should bear their own costs. (See Raven v. Deukmejian, supra, 52 Cal.3d at p. 356.)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, the California State Legislature, voters, and taxpayers, filed suit against respondent various state officials claiming that the Political Reform Act of 1990 passed by referendum of the state's voters, which imposed a term limit upon state legislators and limited pension benefits, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: California voters passed a constitution ballot initiative title the Political Reform Act of 1990, which imposed a term limit on state legislators, cut their budgets, and limited their pension benefits. Petitioners filed suit against respondents claiming that the constitutional amendments were unconstitutional. The court held that it was the court's solemn duty to jealously guard the precious initiative power, and to resolve any reasonable doubts in favor of its exercise. The court stated that the ballot was constitutional, and did not violate any federal constitutional right of access to the ballot or U.S. Const. amend. I. The court also stated that the ballot did not interfere with the legislative branch, or amount to a bill of attainder. However, the court stated that the pension benefits restrictions, even though valid, could not be imposed upon incumbent legislators because they had a property right in their pension prior to the ballot's passage.


OUTCOME: The court held that statute constitutional amendment with imposed term limits, cut legislature budgets and limited pension benefits was constitutional, and did not violate any federal or state right. However, the court stated that the limited pension benefits could not be applied to current legislatures, as they had a vested property right in their pension prior to the ballot being passed.

Izazaga v. Superior Court


No. S017642


Supreme Court of California


54 Cal. 3d 356; 815 P.2d 304; 285 Cal. Rptr. 231; 1991 Cal. LEXIS 3743; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7072; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7187; 91 Daily Journal DAR 10764



August 30, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's application for a rehearing was denied October 24, 1991, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the application should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Tulare County, No. 29058, Robert C. Van Auken, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Because we conclude that, properly construed, the new discovery chapter enacted by Proposition 115 is, on its face, constitutionally valid under the federal and state Constitutions, the alternative writ of mandate issued by this court on February 22, 1991, is discharged, and the peremptory writ is denied. The order of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner appealed an order of the Court of Appeals (California), which denied a writ of mandate or prohibition against respondent superior court for granting unconstitutional reciprocal discovery pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1054.3.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner contended that the reciprocal discovery ordered by respondent superior court under the newly enacted Cal. Const. art. I, § 30(c) and Cal. Penal Code § 1054.3 was unconstitutional. The court found that the disclosure of defense witnesses prior to trial did not violate the self-incrimination privilege under U.S. Const. amend V, because third party statements were not personal to a defendant. Although the self-incrimination privilege under Cal. Const. art. I, § 15 was much broader, the court determined that Cal. Const. art. I, § 30(c) provided a limitation to the privilege. Because § 1054.3 provided for reciprocal discovery favoring the defendant and did not override the prosecution's duty to disclose exculpatory evidence, the court found no due process violation. Last, the court determined that the reciprocal discovery did not limit a defendant's right to effective assistance of counsel by chilling trial preparation or requiring the discovery of attorney work product. Concluding that reciprocal discovery was constitutionally valid, the court affirmed the order of the appellate court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of petitioner's writ of mandate or prohibition against respondent superior court's order for reciprocal discovery because the newly enacted reciprocal discovery provisions were constitutionally valid under federal and state law.

Walnut Creek Manor v. Fair Employment & Housing Com.


No. S015131


Supreme Court of California


54 Cal. 3d 245; 814 P.2d 704; 284 Cal. Rptr. 718; 1991 Cal. LEXIS 3630; 91 Daily Journal DAR 10695; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6997



August 29, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 304628, David A. Dolgin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed in part and affirmed in part. That part of the Court of Appeal judgment relating to punitive damages is reversed. That part of the Court of Appeal judgment relating to emotional distress compensatory damages is modified with directions to order the superior court to modify its writ to strike the award of compensatory damages for emotional distress and, as modified, is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant apartment complex and the fair housing commission challenged the decision of the Superior Court, Contra Costa County (California), which determined that award of general compensatory damages for emotional distress constituted an unconstitutional exercise of judicial power by a nonjudicial body and should be stricken.


OVERVIEW: An individual alleged that appellant apartment complex discriminated against him. The administrative law judge found the claim of racial discrimination meritorious and awarded unspecified compensatory damages and punitive damages. The trial court remanded the case to the fair housing commission with directions to reconsider the finding of marital status discrimination and limit punitive damages. The appellate court affirmed in part and reversed in part. The court concluded that while the statute authorized the commission to award compensatory damages, an administrative award of compensatory damages for emotional distress violated the judicial powers clause of the California Constitution, but that the section was severable in its applications. The court further held that the act authorized only one punitive damages award against a respondent for a course of discriminatory conduct against the same individual on the same unlawful basis.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the court of appeals' decision relating to punitive damages and modified the judgment relating to emotional distress compensatory damages because the court held that the statute did not authorize the award of compensatory damages for emotional distress, but that the section was severable in its application.

Adams v. Murakami


No. S003530


Supreme Court of California


54 Cal. 3d 105; 813 P.2d 1348; 284 Cal. Rptr. 318; 1991 Cal. LEXIS 3448; 91 Daily Journal DAR 10059; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6506



August 15, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C418409, Leon S. Kaplan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed to the extent that it affirmed the award of punitive damages. The Court of Appeal is directed to remand this action to the trial court for further proceedings in accord with this opinion. Defendant is awarded his costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant doctor appealed the judgment of the California Court of Appeal affirming an award of punitive damages in plaintiff patient's action for medical malpractice, battery, and intentional infliction of emotional distress after plaintiff allegedly became pregnant by another patient while she was under defendant's care and gave birth to a son who was diagnosed as mentally retarded and autistic.


OVERVIEW: Defendant doctor was the attending physician for plaintiff patient while she was a resident at the hospital where defendant was employed. Plaintiff filed an action against defendant for medical malpractice, battery, and intentional infliction of emotional distress, alleging that she became pregnant by another patient while she was hospitalized and gave birth to a son who was diagnosed as mentally retarded and autistic. A jury returned a verdict for plaintiff on her causes of action for medical malpractice and intentional infliction of emotional distress and awarded punitive damages. Defendant appealed, contending that the punitive damage award was improper because plaintiff failed to introduce evidence of his financial condition. An appellate court affirmed the award. The court on appeal reversed and remanded, holding that an award of punitive damages could not be sustained on appeal absent evidence of defendant's financial condition, and that Cal. Evid. Code § 500, the traditional allocation of burden of proof, and fundamental fairness require plaintiff rather than the defendant to introduce this evidence.


OUTCOME: The court on appeal reversed the judgment affirming a punitive damage award in favor of plaintiff patient in her action against defendant doctor for alleged medical malpractice, battery, and intentional infliction of emotional distress because the record contained no evidence of defendant's financial condition to support the award. The court directed the appellate court to remand for further proceedings in accordance with the judgment.

In re Horton


No. S012531


Supreme Court of California


54 Cal. 3d 82; 813 P.2d 1335; 284 Cal. Rptr. 305; 1991 Cal. LEXIS 3446; 91 Daily Journal DAR 9909; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6434



August 12, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's application rehearing was denied October 2, 1991. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the application should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Super.Ct. No. A456506.



DISPOSITION: The order to show cause is discharged and the petition for writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was tried by jury in the Los Angeles County Superior Court (California) and found guilty of murder and robbery, with the special circumstances and a prior murder conviction, and was sentenced to death. Defendant sought a writ of habeas corpus or a summary reversal because his trial was presided over by a commissioner, without his or his counsel's written authorization, in violation of Cal. Const. art. VI, § 21.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder and robbery, with special circumstances and a previous murder conviction, and was sentenced to death. A court commissioner conducted the trial, although defendant had never been informed by either the commissioner or his counsel that he had a right to be tried by a superior court judge. At a pre-trial hearing, the commissioner gave a stipulation to counsel for defendant's signature and consent to trial by the commissioner. In the absence of an objection by counsel and defendant, the commissioner assumed that the stipulation had been signed and presided over the trial. The court held that, while Cal. Const. art. VI, § 21 required a stipulation for the court commissioner to sit as a temporary judge, a tantamount stipulation arose because counsel was vested with the general authority to control the procedural aspects of the litigation, counsel did so, and bound defendant by his conduct. The court held that nothing in Cal. Const. art. VI, § 21vested defendant with the sole authority to decide whether to stipulate to trial by commissioner because waiver of trial by a regular superior court judge was not a fundamental, personal right.


OUTCOME: The court discharged the order to show cause and denied defendant's petition for a writ of habeas corpus because defendant's right to waive a trial with a judge was not a fundamental, personal one, which required an admonition and express waiver. The court held that the stipulation necessary to vest the commissioner with the authority to try the case was inferred from the conduct of counsel.

 

255 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Tilbury


No. S012984


Supreme Court of California


54 Cal. 3d 56; 813 P.2d 1318; 284 Cal. Rptr. 288; 1991 Cal. LEXIS 3220; 91 Daily Journal DAR 9444; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6061



August 1, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-53835, Kathleen E. O'Leary, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant insanity acquittee sought review of a decision from the Court of Appeal (California) that reversed the trial court's denial of appellant's request for a jury trial and application for placement in a community mental health program as a supervised outpatient under Cal. Penal Code § 1026.2(e); and remanded the case for a jury trial. The issue before the court was whether appellant was entitled to a jury trial at the first-stage hearing.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, who had been found not guilty by reason of insanity and committed to a state hospital, after he shot and tried to kill six people, requested a jury trial on the issue of his eligibility for placement in a community mental health program as a supervised outpatient pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1026.2(e). The trial court did not empanel a jury, and denied his application. The court of appeals reversed and remanded the case for a jury trial. The court reversed the court of appeals decision. It found that the relevant statute did not purport to give a committed person the right to a jury at the hearing on outpatient placement. The court held that because criminal and civil committees enjoyed the right to jury trials at the same stages of the commitment process, equal protection was not offended. Further, the court found no support for the conclusion that it violated due process for a judge to consider an insanity acquittee's application as insanity acquittees already enjoyed substantial procedural safeguards at placement hearings, and the additions of juries would be more expensive and burdensome without making their outcomes more reliable.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the court of appeals, holding that appellant insanity acquittee was not entitled to a jury trial on the issue of his eligibility for placement in a community mental health program as a supervised outpatient because the relevant statute did not expressly provide for a jury in a placement hearing, and having a judge consider appellant's application did not offend equal protection or violate due process.

 

256 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



California Fed. Savings & Loan Assn. v. City of Los Angeles


No. S013951


Supreme Court of California


54 Cal. 3d 1; 812 P.2d 916; 283 Cal. Rptr. 569; 1991 Cal. LEXIS 3169; 91 Daily Journal DAR 9260; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5945



July 29, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied September 19, 1991. George, J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C579296, Vernon G. Foster, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner savings bank challenged the judgment of the California Court of Appeal, which held that respondent city's license tax on petitioner fell within the municipal affairs of respondent and was therefore authorized by Cal. Const. art. XI, § 5(a). Petitioner sought a refund of this tax on the basis that Cal. Rev. & Tax. § 23182 provided for a state tax of petitioner, which was in lieu of all other taxes.


OVERVIEW: Beginning in 1981, Cal. Rev. & Tax. § 23182 provided for a unitary state income tax on financial corporations, including petitioner savings bank. Though § 23182 provided that the tax was in lieu of all other taxes, except for the initial year, respondent city continued to impose its annual business license tax on petitioner. Petitioner paid the tax under protest, and subsequently filed a lawsuit to obtain a refund of the tax on the basis that it was prohibited by § 23182. Respondent argued that the tax was a municipal affair, and thus protected under Cal. Const. art. XI, § 5(a). In reversing the judgment of the lower court, the court found that in cases of conflict between a state statute and a charter city measure, where the state can demonstrate a true area of statewide concern, the charter measure ceases to be a municipal affair, and thus was not protected under Cal. Const. art. XI, § 5(a). Given the similarity between commercial banks and savings banks, and the almost exclusive regulation of savings banks by either federal or state governments, this court concluded that this was an area of statewide concern.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court, finding that there was a legitimate interest in assessing a unified, standard state tax on banks and financial corporations. Because this taxation was a matter of statewide concern, respondent city's tax ceased to be a municipal affair, and thus was not protected by the home rule provision of the California Constitution.

People v. Sully


No. S004721, Crim. No. 25590


SUPREME COURT OF CALIFORNIA


53 Cal. 3d 1195; 812 P.2d 163; 283 Cal. Rptr. 144; 1991 Cal. LEXIS 2977; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5489



July 11, 1991, Decided

PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. C-14026, Gerald E. Ragan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For the reasons stated above, we find no reversible error in the record. The judgment of death is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), convicting defendant on six counts of first degree murder under Cal. Penal Code § 187, with a special circumstance of multiple murder pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(3), and sentencing him to death.

Other crimes evidence was properly admitted, defendant's confrontation and Miranda rights were not violated, and defendant's motion to change venue was properly denied. The court affirmed the sentence, concluding that no prejudicial errors were made during the penalty phase.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed. Defendant was properly convicted for first-degree murder and sentenced to death, because no reversible error appeared on the record for either the guilt or penalty phases of defendant's trial.

Sands v. Morongo Unified School Dist.


No. S012721


Supreme Court of California


53 Cal. 3d 863; 809 P.2d 809; 281 Cal. Rptr. 34; 1991 Cal. LEXIS 1724; 91 Daily Journal DAR 5389; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3328



May 6, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellants' petition for a rehearing was denied June 27, 1991. Panelli, J., and Baxter, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. BCV 2279, LeRoy A. Simmons, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Because the practice of officially endorsed prayers at public school graduation ceremonies cannot be reconciled with the constitutional principles of religious freedom and official neutrality, we reverse the judgment of the Court of Appeal. The matter is remanded to the Court of Appeal with directions to affirm the judgment of the trial court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants school district sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California) reversing the trial court's judgment in favor of respondents taxpayers in an action brought by respondents pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 526a seeking declaratory and injunctive relief prohibiting appellants from including religious invocations at public school ceremonies.


OVERVIEW: Appellants school district sought review of a judgment from the lower court reversing the trial court's judgment in favor of respondents taxpayers in an action brought by respondents pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 526a. The court reversed the lower court and held that religious invocations and benedictions at public high school graduation ceremonies were constitutionally impermissible because they failed both the effect and the entanglement tests of the U.S. Const. amend. I establishment clause. Because the ceremonies were organized and controlled by the government to celebrate a significant event, words spoken as part of the ceremonies inevitably created a strong appearance of government endorsement. The assertedly "nonsectarian" nature of the prayers did not render their government sponsorship constitutionally acceptable. In addition, the practice of including prayers at public high school graduation ceremonies impermissibly entangled government in religious matters because it involved governmental selection of religious speakers and approval of the content of prayer. Finally, the practice of graduation prayer also violated Cal. Const. art. I, § 4 and art. XVI, § 5.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and held that religious invocations and benedictions at public high school graduation ceremonies were constitutionally impermissible because they failed both the effect and the entanglement tests of the U.S. Const. amend. I establishment clause and the corresponding state constitutional provisions. The prayer created an appearance of government endorsement and involved governmental selection and approval of prayer content.

People v. Frierson


No. S004761, Crim. No. 26221


Supreme Court of California


53 Cal. 3d 730; 808 P.2d 1197; 280 Cal. Rptr. 440; 1991 Cal. LEXIS 1721; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3222; 91 Daily Journal DAR 5215



May 2, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied June 26, 1991.
Rehearing denied by People v. Frierson, 1991 Cal. LEXIS 2900 (Cal., June 26, 1991)
Writ of certiorari denied Frierson v. California, 502 U.S. 1061, 112 S. Ct. 944, 117 L. Ed. 2d 114, 1992 U.S. LEXIS 488 (1992)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Frierson v. Calderon, 968 F. Supp. 497, 1997 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 13622 (C.D. Cal., 1997)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A192962, William C. Beverly, Jr., Judge.
People v. Frierson, 39 Cal. 3d 803, 218 Cal. Rptr. 73, 705 P.2d 396, 1985 Cal. LEXIS 337 (1985)



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) convicting him of first-degree murder, robbery and other charges, and sentencing him to death.


OVERVIEW: This case was before the court for the third time. In previous decisions, the court reversed appellant's conviction because of ineffective counsel and errors in certain special circumstances findings for purposes of capital punishment. On this third appeal, the convictions and death penalty sentence were affirmed. Here, appellant raised numerous arguments, including that the court erred by denying him co-counsel status, not granting him substitute counsel, and denying his eve-of-trial motion to represent himself. The court held that, because appellant was represented by adequate counsel, he had no automatic right to join as co-counsel. In fact, the trial court's discretion to allow such an arrangement was sharply limited and could only be exercised upon a substantial showing that it would promote justice and judicial efficiency. The court found no such showing here. The court also found that the trial court did not err by denying appellant's demand to substitute himself as counsel on the eve of trial in that, under such circumstances, the trial court's denial was within its discretion. Accordingly, the judgment below was affirmed in its entirety.


OUTCOME: Appellant's convictions for first-degree murder, robbery and other charges, as well as his death sentence, were affirmed because the court found no prejudicial errors by the trial court.

People v. Wharton


No. S004769


Supreme Court of California


53 Cal. 3d 522; 809 P.2d 290; 280 Cal. Rptr. 631; 1991 Cal. LEXIS 1608; 91 Daily Journal DAR 4957; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3426



April 29, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The petition of respondent Wharton for a rehearing was denied July 9, 1991, and the opinion was modified. Mosk, J., Broussard, J., and Kennard, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 161315, Bruce W. Dodds, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 161315 (California), which found him guilty of first-degree murder, claiming prosecutorial misconduct, improperly admitted evidence at trial, violation of the psychotherapist-patient privilege, and improper voir dire.


OVERVIEW: Appellant struck the back of his girlfriend's head three times with a hammer, killing her. Appellant hid the body in a barrel, and stole her personal belongings. The police investigated the victim's disappearance, and entered her home. The police encountered the barrel, opened it, and discovered the victim's body. Defendant was arrested and charged with first-degree murder. Defendant waived his Miranda rights, and confessed to the murder. After defendant was found guilty of the murder, he challenged his conviction. The court held that his conversations with his psychotherapist admitted at trial, was not a violation of his psychotherapist-patient privilege under Cal. Evid. Code §1024, because disclosure of the communications was necessary to protect the victim, and disclosure was not itself necessary, but had triggered the therapist's decision to warn the victim. The court also determined that defendant was not entitled to a mistrial due to prosecutorial misconduct in examining witnesses; during voir dire, or his closing arguments. The court further stated that the jury was properly given jury instructions, and defendant did not receive ineffective assistance of counsel.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court, and upheld defendant's conviction for first-degree murder, stating that the prosecutor did not commit misconduct, evidence was properly admitted at trial, defendant's psychotherapist-patient privilege was not violated, and there was no improper voir dire.

People v. Morris


No. S004781, Crim. No. 26415


Supreme Court of California


53 Cal. 3d 152; 807 P.2d 949; 279 Cal. Rptr. 720; 1991 Cal. LEXIS 1218; 91 Daily Journal DAR 3869; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2303



March 28, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied May 22, 1991. Mosk, J., and Broussard, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Joaquin County, No. 39143, William R. Giffen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For the reasons stated above, there was no prejudicial error in the proceedings below. We affirm the judgment of death in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed an automatic appeal from the Superior Court of San Joaquin County (California), which entered a judgment of death after a jury found defendant guilty of first-degree murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 187 and robbery in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 211. The jury also found as a special circumstance that the murder was committed in the perpetration of a robbery in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17)(i).


OVERVIEW: Defendant filed an automatic appeal after the trial court entered a judgment of death under Cal. Penal Code §190.1 et seq. (1978). Defendant was convicted by a jury of first-degree murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 187 and robbery in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 211. The court affirmed. Defendant showed no impairment of his defense resulting from the absence of the prosecutor's jury book at defense counsel table, and defendant was not entitled to reversal. Although some of the trial court's remarks during voir dire were irrelevant and improper, there was no prejudice. The trial court imposed, and the jury accepted the full burden of responsibility, for a death verdict. There was no abuse of discretion in the trial court's decisions overruling defendant's challenges for cause. Defendant failed to exhaust his peremptory challenges and failed to make a showing that the jury was anything but impartial. The cumulative effect of jury selection errors did not deprive defendant of a fair trial. The court found that defendant's crime was a heinous and brutal one. Under all of the circumstances, there was nothing arbitrary or unfair about the imposition of the death penalty.


OUTCOME: Judgment of death was affirmed in its entirety. The court found no prejudicial error in the proceedings below. The cumulative effect of jury selection errors did not deprive defendant of a fair trial. There was nothing in the record of defendant's prior crimes that amounted to an inaccurate rendition of his prior criminal acts.

People v. Beardslee


No. S004609, Crim. No. 23593


Supreme Court of California


53 Cal. 3d 68; 806 P.2d 1311; 279 Cal. Rptr. 276; 1991 Cal. LEXIS 1157; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2101; 91 Daily Journal DAR 3490



March 25, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied June 5, 1991, and the opinion was modified. Mosk, J., and Broussard, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.
Modified by, Rehearing denied by People v. Beardslee, 53 Cal. 3d 1179a, 1991 Cal. LEXIS 2625, 91 D.A.R. 6607 (1991)
Stay granted by People v. Beardslee, 1991 Cal. LEXIS 4264 (Cal., Sept. 18, 1991)
Writ of certiorari denied Beardslee v. California, 502 U.S. 972, 116 L. Ed. 2d 467, 112 S. Ct. 449, 1991 U.S. LEXIS 6718 (1991)
Writ of habeas corpus dismissed Beardslee v. Woodford, 327 F.3d 799, 2003 U.S. App. LEXIS 5379 (9th Cir. Cal., 2003)
Motion granted by People v. Beardslee (Donald Jay), 2004 Cal. LEXIS 10001 (Cal., Oct. 14, 2004)
Stay denied by, Motion denied by People v. Beardslee, 2004 Cal. LEXIS 12222 (Cal., Dec. 14, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. C-10632, Robert D. Miller, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The excess multiple-murder special circumstance and both witness-killing special circumstances are set aside, and the judgment is otherwise affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment by the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), which convicted him of two counts of first degree murder under two special circumstances pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(3), (10). Defendant claimed that the special circumstances should not have been applied and numerous other errors.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of two counts of first degree murder under the special circumstances of concurrent conviction of multiple murders under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(3) and witness killing under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(10). On appeal, defendant claimed numerous errors. The court rejected the contention that the jury should have been instructed on defendant's honest belief in imminent peril as negating malice because the court gave other instructions concerning the effects of threats on the requisite mental state. An admission of a homicide that was illegally obtained by police in another state was properly admitted because defendant's admitted the crime freely under circumstances sufficiently attenuated from the earlier illegality as to dissipate the primary taint. The evidence was insufficient to support the witness-killing special circumstance findings because the two murders were part of the same continuous criminal transaction against both victims. Lastly, the finding of two multiple-murder special circumstances was erroneous. Accordingly, the court affirmed but set aside one multiple-murder special circumstance and both witness-killing special circumstances.


OUTCOME: The court set aside witness-killing special circumstances because the evidence was insufficient to support the finding. The court also set aside the finding of two multiple-murder special circumstances. The court affirmed the remainder of the judgment.

Lebbos v. State Bar of Cal.


No. S011535


Supreme Court of California


53 Cal. 3d 37; 806 P.2d 317; 278 Cal. Rptr. 845; 1991 Cal. LEXIS 1244; 91 Daily Journal DAR 3202; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1884; 1991-2 Trade Cas. (CCH) P69,506



March 18, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's application for a rehearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 16, 1991.
Stay denied by Lebbos v. State Bar of California, 1991 Cal. LEXIS 2067 (Cal., Apr. 17, 1991)
Rehearing denied by Lebbos v. State Bar of California, 1991 Cal. LEXIS 2208 (Cal., May 16, 1991)
Stay denied by, Writ of certiorari denied Lebbos v. State Bar, 502 U.S. 932, 112 S. Ct. 352, 116 L. Ed. 2d 291, 1991 U.S. LEXIS 6250 (1991)
Related proceeding at U.S. Trustee v. Lebbos (In re Lebbos), 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 110588 (E.D. Cal., Oct. 5, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY: Lebbos v. State Bar of California, 1989 Cal. LEXIS 5092 (Cal., Nov. 30, 1989)



DISPOSITION: It is ordered that petitioner Betsey Warren Lebbos be disbarred from the practice of law in the State of California and that her name be stricken from the roll of attorneys. It is further ordered that petitioner Betsey Warren Lebbos comply with rule 955 of the California Rules of Court, and that she perform the acts specified in subdivisions (a) and (c) of that rule within 30 and 40 days, respectively, after the effective date of this order. This order is effective upon the finality of this decision in this court. (See Cal. Rules of Court, rule 953(a).)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Before the court for review was a recommendation from the Review Department of the State Bar Court (California) that petitioner attorney be disbarred for numerous counts of client-related misconduct. Petitioner claimed that state bar lacked jurisdiction and that the disciplinary proceedings were void under the Sherman Antitrust Act, 15 U.S.C.S. § 1 et seq., and the Racketeer Influenced and Corrupt Organizations Act, 18 U.S.C.S. § 1961 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner attorney sought review of a recommendation from the Review Department of the State Bar Court (Department) that petitioner attorney be disbarred. Petitioner was found culpable in nine counts of misconduct involving forty separate acts of such misconduct as commingling client funds, concealing assets, engaging in disruptive and offensive courtroom-conduct, and making frivolous motions to disqualify judges. The court adopted the Department's recommendation and ordered petitioner's disbarment. The court rejected petitioner's claims that the disciplinary proceedings violated Sherman Anti-Trust Act (Act), 15 U.S.C.S. § 1 et seq., and the Racketeer Influenced and Corrupt Organizations Act (RICO), 18 U.S.C.S. § 1961 et seq. The court ruled that the Act was inapplicable to the state disciplinary action and that there was no persuasive evidence of any racketeering activity on the part of the state bar in disciplining her. The court rejected as meritless petitioner's claim that discipline imposed by the integrated bar was an unconstitutional violation of associational or free speech rights.


OUTCOME: The court adopted the recommendation of the Review Department of the State Bar Court and ordered that petitioner attorney be disbarred. The federal Sherman Antitrust Act was inapplicable to the state's disciplinary action; there was no persuasive evidence of any racketeering activity on the part of the state bar in disciplining her. There was no constitutional infirmity regarding the establishment of or procedures used by the state bar.

 

269 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Pensinger


Nos. S004466, S009489, Crim. Nos. 22808, 24569


Supreme Court of California


52 Cal. 3d 1210; 805 P.2d 899; 278 Cal. Rptr. 640; 1991 Cal. LEXIS 901; 91 Daily Journal DAR 2504; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1514



February 28, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied April 24, 1991, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. SCR38261, Ben T. Kayashima, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order to show cause is discharged and the petition for writ of habeas corpus is denied. The finding as to the torture-murder special circumstance is reversed. In all other respects we affirm the judgment and the penalty imposed by the superior court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an appeal combined with a petition for habeas corpus relief, defendant challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California), which convicted him of first-degree murder and sentenced him to death in the murder and sexual mutilation of a 5-month-old infant girl. The state supreme court issued an order to show cause in the habeas corpus petition.


OVERVIEW: Defendant stole his uncle's pickup truck and drove to an out of state bar, where he agreed to go with a woman to collect her children, a 5-year-old boy and 5-month-old girl. While defendant and the woman were in another bar, the boy found defendant's gun and pointed it at a passer-by, who took the gun away. The mother went to report the theft, and defendant drove away, dropping the boy off. The infant was later found sexually mutilated and dead. Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder and sentenced to death. On appeal, defendant asserted numerous errors, including that his intoxication was a defense and that his conviction was improperly based on the testimony of cellmate informants. While reversing the jury's finding as to a torture-murder special circumstance for lack of proof of intent to torture, the state supreme court affirmed the conviction and sentence, holding that a diminished capacity instruction was proper only when substantial evidence of such capacity negated the requisite criminal intent. Moreover, the supreme court held that there was no U.S. Const. amend. VI violation because defendant's cellmates were merely passive listening posts, not government agents.


OUTCOME: The order to show cause was discharged, and the habeas corpus petition was denied. The state supreme court reversed the lower court's finding as to the torture-murder special circumstance because there was no basis for finding that defendant intended to torture his victim, but the court affirmed the lower court's judgment and penalty, holding that the invalidation of one of several aggravating factors did not warrant reversal of the judgment.

Coleman v. Department of Personnel Administration


No. S004129


Supreme Court of California


52 Cal. 3d 1102; 805 P.2d 300; 278 Cal. Rptr. 346; 1991 Cal. LEXIS 983; 91 Daily Journal DAR 2323; 6 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 365; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1400



February 25, 1991 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 332442, Joseph A. DeCristoforo, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed. Each party shall bear its own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee sought review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals (California) that denied appellant's challenge to the termination of his employment with respondent Department of General Services.


OVERVIEW: Appellant employee was terminated by respondent Department of General Services pursuant to Cal. Gov't Code § 19996.2(a). Section 19996.2(a) provided that an employee's absence without leave from work for more than five consecutive days was deemed an automatic resignation. The court of appeals rejected appellant's constitutional challenges to § 19996.2(a). On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals because respondent was not prejudiced by respondent's failure to give him prior notice or an opportunity to respond since he was not working when he was terminated and he suffered no damages. The court held that appellant was entitled to due process because § 19996.2(a) was not self-executing since respondent had to make a factual determination as to whether appellant resigned. The court held that because the factual determinations made under § 19996.2(a) were relatively simple, due process merely required respondent to give appellant written notice that his termination was being considered and an opportunity to present his version of the facts before a neutral fact finder. The court rejected appellant's substantive due process and equal protection challenges.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals in favor of respondent Department of General Services because appellant employee was not entitled to reinstatement and he lost no wages as a result of any due process violation.

People v. Mason


No. S004604, Crim. No. 23519


Supreme Court of California


52 Cal. 3d 909; 802 P.2d 950; 277 Cal. Rptr. 166; 1991 Cal. LEXIS 3; 91 Daily Journal DAR 564; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 376



January 10, 1991 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied March 13, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 72839, Jacqueline Taber, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified by striking all but one of the multiple-murder special-circumstance findings under Penal Code section 190.2, subdivision (a)(3). In all other respects the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction in the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), of the murders of five victims, each in the first degree, Cal. Penal Code § 187, special circumstances pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(3) and Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17)(I), enhancements pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §§ 1203.09 and Cal. Penal Code § 12022.7, that resulted in a sentence of death following a retrial of the penalty phase by a second jury.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted by a jury of murdering four elderly victims, and he also strangled a fellow inmate in jail while awaiting trial. Defendant confessed to the murders of the four, but then recanted at trial. The court affirmed the convictions and sentence of death because it found no prejudice to defendant in consolidation of the charges in one trial where none of the murder counts were stronger or more inflammatory than the others. The prosecutor properly explained his peremptory challenges to negate a case of group bias. Defendant's proposed voir dire questions were improper and the trial court was within its discretion to deny the questions. The allowance of evidence of flight to show consciousness of guilt and the jury instruction on flight were not an abuse of discretion where the facts clearly supported it. The probative value of the photographs admitted outweighed the prejudicial effect. Improper questions by the prosecutor did not make it reasonably probable that a trial without them would have resulted in a verdict more favorable to defendant. The jury charge on mitigation allowed consideration of evidence not associated with the crime.


OUTCOME: The court struck all but one multiple-murder special-circumstance findings, and affirmed the conviction and sentence. Defendant did not demonstrate prejudice based on consolidation, jury voir dire and selection, allowance of evidence of flight, admission of unpleasant photographs, improper questions by the prosecutor or alleged improper jury instructions because the evidence of guilt was overwhelmingly strong against him on every count.

In re Kelley


No. S008887


Supreme Court of California


52 Cal. 3d 487; 801 P.2d 1126; 276 Cal. Rptr. 375; 1990 Cal. LEXIS 5665; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 196; 91 Daily Journal DAR 166



December 31, 1990 


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from No. 87-C-15752.



DISPOSITION: For the foregoing reasons, we order that petitioner Anna Lou Kelley be publicly reproved and placed on disciplinary probation for three years subject to all the conditions set forth by the review department in its recommendation, with the exception of the requirement that petitioner abstain from the use of intoxicants during such period. This order is effective upon finality of this decision in this court. (See Cal. Rules of Court, rule 953(a).)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney challenged the recommendation of the Review Department of the State Bar Court (California) to the effect that petitioner be publicly reproved and placed on disciplinary probation for three years on several conditions, including abstinence from the use of intoxicants and referral to the State Bar Program on Alcohol Abuse.


OVERVIEW: At petitioner attorney's initial conviction of driving with a blood-alcohol level in excess of the legal limit, she was given 36 months probation. Before the probationary period expired, petitioner was again convicted of driving with a blood-alcohol level in excess of the legal limit. Following an administrative hearing, the Review Department of the State Bar Court recommended that petitioner be publicly reproved and placed on disciplinary probation for three years, with the condition that she not use intoxicants during this period, and that she enroll in the Bar's program on alcohol abuse. The court accepted the recommendation, rejecting petitioner's claim that the "other misconduct" basis used to discipline attorneys who were not engaged in activities involving moral turpitude or that directly related to the practice of law, was unconstitutionally vague. Though petitioner's activity did not involve the practice of law there was a nexus between it and her fitness to practice law. The court also found that, except for the requirement that petitioner not use intoxicants during the probationary period, the discipline imposed was appropriate to the nature and extent of the misconduct.


OUTCOME: The court accepted the recommendation of the Review Department of the State Bar Court, finding that the other misconduct basis used to discipline petitioner attorney was not constitutionally vague, that the conduct was related to petitioner's fitness to practice law, and that except for the prohibition on petitioner's use of intoxicants for a three year period, which the court found overbroad, the discipline was suited to the misconduct.

People v. Kaurish


No. S004640, Crim. Nos. 23882, 25746


Supreme Court of California


52 Cal. 3d 648; 802 P.2d 278; 276 Cal. Rptr. 788; 1990 Cal. LEXIS 5660; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 147; 91 Daily Journal DAR 248



December 31, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied February 27, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A378410, Kathleen Parker, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We affirm the judgment in its entirety and deny the petition for habeas corpus.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus for review of the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which was consolidated with the automatic appeal of the judgment of death imposed after his conviction for committing lewd and lascivious acts, oral copulation and first degree murder.


OVERVIEW: At defendant's trial for first degree murder, committing lewd and lascivious acts on an individual under 14, and oral copulation, the prosecution introduced expert evidence linking defendant's leg injuries with those that would occur from a struggle between defendant and his alleged victim. Defendant's expert testified that the injuries pre-dated the alleged crimes. In rebuttal, the prosecution provided the testimony of the arresting officers and a statement of defendant after his arrest that his injuries occurred on the night of the alleged incident while he was elsewhere. Because there was also evidence that defendant had not been at the location he claimed, the court ruled that it was not error to admit this rebuttal evidence. Many of the errors claimed by defendant on appeal were not challenged at trial, and thus the court ruled that they had been waived. The reference to prior felony convictions under Cal. Penal Code § 190.3 included felony convictions of other states. Though the court erroneously admitted prior charges against defendant of a nonviolent crime, this was not reversible error because there was no reasonable possibility that it effected the verdict.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court and denied defendant's petition for a writ of habeas corpus. The court ruled that many of the defendant's claims had no merit, and those that had merit either were waived because they had not been raised at trial, or there was no reasonable probability that their absence would have affected the outcome of the trial, and thus were not grounds for reversal.

People v. Taylor


No. S004717, Crim. No. 25505


Supreme Court of California

Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied March 13, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 30929, Michael J. Phelan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant's automatic appeal followed the sentence of death imposed upon him by the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), after his conviction by jury of three counts of rape, robbery in an inhabited dwelling, attempted rape, and three counts of burglary. The jury also found three prior conviction enhancements were true: murder while the defendant was engaged in the commission or attempted commission of robbery and burglary.


OVERVIEW: Defendant raised numerous errors in his appeal of his death sentence, murder, and felony convictions. Further, there was no legal requirement that the jury return a unanimous verdict on each aggravating circumstance which it found during the penalty phase of the trial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, finding no error in many of defendant's claims; and in those cases where the court found error, it also found that there was no reasonable probability that the jury would have reached a more favorable verdict in the absence of such error. The court also found no error in the instructions given to the jury during the penalty phase or in the argument made by the prosecutor during this phase.

Raven v. Deukmejian


No. S016137


Supreme Court of California


52 Cal. 3d 336; 801 P.2d 1077; 276 Cal. Rptr. 326; 1990 Cal. LEXIS 5494; 90 Daily Journal DAR 14642; 90 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9333



December 24, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Respondents' petition for a rehearing was denied February 14, 1991.



DISPOSITION: The petition for a peremptory writ of mandate is granted to the extent that it seeks to compel respondents to refrain from enforcing section 3 of Proposition 115. In all other respects the petition is denied. The parties shall bear their own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, taxpayers and voters, sought issuance of an original writ of mandate or prohibition from a District Court of Appeal (California), against respondents, public officials and courts, pursuant to Cal. Const. art. II, § 8(d) and Cal. Const. art. XVIII, in a suit involving the validity of amendments adopted in Proposition 115.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, taxpayers and voters, challenged the validity of Proposition 115, claiming that its broad diversity embraced multiple subjects, violating the "single subject" rule of Cal. Const. art. II, § 8(d); they also claimed that the measure was a constitutional revision rather than an amendment, in violation of Cal. Const. art. XVIII, §§ 1-2. Petitioners sought an appellate court issuance of an original writ of mandate or prohibition against respondents, public officials and courts, who were responsible for implementing the initiative. However, the suit was transferred and the court exercised original jurisdiction, holding that respondents should refrain from enforcing § 3 of Proposition 115, because it contemplated such a far-reaching change in the state's governmental framework as to amount to a qualitative constitutional revision. Section 3 would have vested all judicial interpretive power, as to fundamental criminal defense rights, in the United States Supreme Court. Being severable from § 3, the remaining initiative sections could be given effect, because all parts were reasonably germane to each other and to the general purpose of promoting the rights of crime victims.


OUTCOME: Exercising original jurisdiction, the court granted petitioners, taxpayers and voters, a writ of mandate or prohibition against respondents, public officials and courts, as to enforcement of one section of an initiative, because its effect amounted to a constitutional revision beyond the scope of the initiative process. In all other respects, the petition was denied, because initiative sections were germane to each other and to a general purpose.
