280 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Curl v. Superior Court


No. S010655


Supreme Court of California


51 Cal. 3d 1292; 801 P.2d 292; 276 Cal. Rptr. 49; 1990 Cal. LEXIS 5240; 90 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8933; 90 Daily Journal DAR 14035



December 10, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's application for a rehearing was denied February 21, 1991. Baxter, J., did not participate therein. Broussard, J., was of the opinion that the application should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 380748-4, Gene M. Gomes, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeal denying the petition for writ of mandate is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the order of the Court of Appeal (California), which denied his petition for writ of mandate against respondent court. Respondent, upon defendant's motion and after an evidentiary hearing, upheld the constitutional validity of defendant's prior murder conviction underlying the prior-murder special circumstance attached to his murder charge.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with first degree murder with a prior-murder special circumstance. Defendant sought to strike the special circumstance because he was under the influence of drugs when he pled guilty to the prior murder and was not advised of his constitutional rights. Respondent court held an evidentiary hearing and denied defendant's motion. The appellate court denied defendant's petition for writ of mandate. The court affirmed, finding that the appellate court had properly denied the writ, but for the wrong reasons. The court held that the governing statutes did not require that the jury hear a constitutional attack on a prior murder conviction. Under Cal. Pen. Code § 190.1, the jury was to determine the truth of the special circumstance, but the trial court was required to hold an evidentiary hearing prior to trial to determine the constitutionality of the challenged conviction. Defendant had to prove the constitutional invalidity of the prior conviction by a preponderance of the evidence. The prior judgment carried a presumption of regularity. The testimony of the interested parties during the evidentiary hearing supported respondent's finding of validity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the appellate court denying defendant's petition for writ of mandate, but for different reasons. The court held that defendant could challenge the constitutionally of the prior murder conviction underlying his prior-murder special circumstance by way of pretrial motion. Respondent court properly held an evidentiary hearing on the challenge, and the evidence supported respondent's finding of validity.

People v. Gonzalez


Nos. S004384, S004384, S012508, Crim. Nos. 22136, 24041


Supreme Court of California


51 Cal. 3d 1179; 800 P.2d 1159; 275 Cal. Rptr. 729; 1990 Cal. LEXIS 5233; 90 Daily Journal DAR 13736; 90 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8746



December 3, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied February 27, 1991. Broussard, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.
Rehearing denied by, Motion denied by People v. Gonzalez, 1991 Cal. LEXIS 874 (Cal., Feb. 27, 1991)
Stay granted by People v. Gonzalez, 1991 Cal. LEXIS 3227 (Cal., July 17, 1991)
Writ of certiorari denied Gonzalez v. California, 502 U.S. 835, 112 S. Ct. 117, 116 L. Ed. 2d 85, 1991 U.S. LEXIS 5304 (1991)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Dismissed by Gonzalez v. Ayers, 2008 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 82816 (C.D. Cal., Sept. 29, 2008)
Motion granted by People v. Gonzalez (Jesse Edward), 2010 Cal. LEXIS 1034 (Cal., Feb. 10, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A524625, Sam Cianchetti, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment on appeal is affirmed in its entirety. The petitions for habeas corpus are denied. Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue, directing the Superior Court of Los Angeles County to vacate its order, dated August 17, 1989, granting postjudgment discovery in the action entitled People v. Gonzalez, Action No. A524625.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) for first degree murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 187, 189, assault on a peace officer by means likely to produce great bodily injury under Cal. Penal Code § 245(c), and his sentence to death. Defendant also petitioned for habeas corpus and the state petitioned for mandamus to overturn the trial court's postjudgment discovery order.


OVERVIEW: Defendant killed a police officer who was executing a search warrant and was convicted of first degree murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 187, 189, and assault on a peace officer under Cal. Penal Code § 245(c). The jury found as a special circumstance under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(7), that defendant intentionally killed a peace officer engaged in the performance of duty. Defendant was sentenced to death. The court affirmed because the warrant was valid and the testimony of a jailhouse informant was properly admitted to show that defendant was aware that the victims were peace officers executing a search warrant. The court held that when criminal statutes accorded special treatment to violence against peace officers engaged in duties, such duties included service of facially valid warrants, regardless of the legal sufficiency of the facts supporting the warrant. The court denied defendant's habeas corpus petition because he received effective assistance of counsel. The court granted the state's petition for mandamus because the trial court had no authority to entertain a postjudgment discovery motion unrelated to a proceeding pending before it.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions and death sentence were affirmed because the victim was a police officer executing a valid search warrant and defendant was aware of those facts. The court denied defendant's petition for habeas corpus because he had received effective assistance of counsel. The court granted the state's petition for mandamus because the trial court lacked authority to order postjudgment discovery.

People v. Medina


No. S004758


Supreme Court of California


51 Cal. 3d 870; 799 P.2d 1282; 274 Cal. Rptr. 849; 1990 Cal. LEXIS 5054; 90 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8358



November 19, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied February 14, 1991. Mosk, J., and Broussard, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-56201, James K. Turner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in its entirety.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant automatically appealed from the Superior Court of Orange County (California) which, after a jury trial, imposed the death penalty following defendant's conviction of three counts of first-degree murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 187, accompanied by three special circumstance findings, namely, robbery-murder, burglary-murder, and multiple murder. Defendant was also convicted of several other counts.


OVERVIEW: Defendant automatically appealed the death penalty imposed after a jury convicted defendant of three counts of murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 187, accompanied by three special circumstance findings: robbery-murder, burglary-murder, and multiple murder. The jury also convicted defendant of several other counts. The court affirmed the trial court decision. Defendant was properly found competent to stand trial and legally sane at the time of the offenses. Cal. Penal Code § 1369 properly placed the burden on defendant to prove, by a preponderance of the evidence, that he was incompetent to stand trial. Defendant failed to carry his burden. Testimony was properly admitted during the competency hearing. Adoptive admissions were properly admitted because defendant's silence in the face of accusatory statements indicated that defendant believed the statements to be true. No reversible prosecutorial misconduct was shown, and defendant did not establish ineffective assistance of counsel. Defendant's right to be present during trial was not violated. There was sufficient evidence of aggravating circumstances, and the lack of mitigating evidence, to support death penalty imposition.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a trial court decision that imposed the death penalty after the jury convicted defendant of three counts of first-degree murder with special circumstances. There was sufficient evidence to support the jury's finding that defendant was sane at the time of the offenses and that the murders were premeditated and deliberate. There were sufficient aggravating circumstances to support the imposed death penalty.

 

283 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Southwest Concrete Products v. Gosh Construction Corp.


No. S012846


Supreme Court of California


51 Cal. 3d 701; 798 P.2d 1247; 274 Cal. Rptr. 404; 1990 Cal. LEXIS 4786; 13 U.C.C. Rep. Serv. 2d (Callaghan) 985



November 1, 1990 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. I-42496, Frank R. Moore, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant buyer appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which found in favor of plaintiff seller, and awarded prejudgment interest at 18 percent per year in plaintiff's action to recover an unpaid contract balance from defendant.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff seller brought suit against defendant buyer to recover an unpaid balance on a contract for sewer pipe. A lower court found for plaintiff and awarded prejudgment interest at 18 percent per year. Defendant appealed the lower court's order on the ground that the 18 percent prejudgment interest was usurious. The court affirmed the lower court's judgment, concluding that interest payments on overdue commercial accounts were not subject to the usury law. The court determined that the parties' transaction was exempt from the usury laws because it was a credit sale. The court applied the principle that a transaction that was not usurious at its inception could not become usurious by virtue of a debtor's voluntary default. The court found that the late charges were a part of the contract under Cal. Com. Code § 2207. The court concluded that the late charge provided in the contract was not subject to the usury law because it did not constitute payment for the loan or the forbearance of any money. Additionally, it was exempt from the usury law under the time-price doctrine and the principle that a debtor by voluntary act could not render an otherwise valid contract usurious.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a lower court's judgment awarding prejudgment interest at 18 percent per year to plaintiff seller in an action for an unpaid contract balance against defendant buyer because interest payments on overdue commercial accounts were not subject to the usury law. The parties' transaction was a credit sale and the late charge was not subject to the usury law because it did not constitute payment for a loan or the forbearance of money.

 

284 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Taxpayers to Limit Campaign Spending v. Fair Pol. Practices Com


No. S012016


Supreme Court of California


51 Cal. 3d 744; 799 P.2d 1220; 274 Cal. Rptr. 787; 1990 Cal. LEXIS 4788



November 1, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Petitioner's application for a rehearing was denied January 18, 1991. Arabian, J., did not participate therein. Mosk, J., Broussard, J., and Kennard, J., were of the opinion that the application should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Ct. of Appeal B039177.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is reversed and the matter is remanded with directions to deny the petition for writ of mandate.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent election commission sought review of an order of the Court of Appeal, Second Appellate District, Division Four (California), which considered two competing election reform initiatives that were approved in the same election, one of which was sponsored by petitioner taxpayers association, and determined that provisions in petitioners' initiative that corresponded to the other initiative could be enforced despite receiving less votes.


OVERVIEW: Two competing campaign finance reform initiatives, one of which was sponsored by petitioner taxpayers association, were approved in the same election, with the competing measure receiving more votes than that of petitioners. Many of the provisions of the two measures conflicted, and respondent election commission determined that the conflicting provisions in petitioner's measures were invalid. Petitioners moved for a writ of mandate that sought an order compelling respondent to enforce the provisions of petitioner's measure. The appellate court determined that the measures invalidated by respondent should be enforced. Respondent sought review. The court reversed and remanded with directions to deny the petition for writ of mandate, noting that Cal. Const. art. II, § 10(b) stated that if provisions of two or more measures were approved at the same general election conflict, those of the measure receiving the highest affirmative vote would prevail. Thus, the court held that when two measures were competing initiatives, because each created a regulatory scheme related to the same subject, only the provisions of the measure receiving the most votes would be enforced.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the court of appeals and ordered it to deny petitioner taxpayer's petition for writ of mandate. The court held that when two or more measures were competing initiatives, the constitution mandated that only the provisions of the measure receiving the highest number of affirmative votes could be enforced. Individual provisions of the measure receiving the lower number of affirmative votes could not be enforced.

People v. Whitt


No. S004689, Crim. No. 24585


Supreme Court of California


51 Cal. 3d 620; 798 P.2d 849; 274 Cal. Rptr. 252; 1990 Cal. LEXIS 4681

DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in its entirety.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A conviction of first degree murder, robbery, assault with a deadly weapon, a plea of guilty to possession of a firearm by an ex-felon, and a sentence of death was automatically appealed from the Superior Court of San Bernadino County (California).


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court. Defendant went on a crime spree which included shooting at several persons, robbing a store, driving under the influence, and murdering a person after the robbery. Defendant was convicted of first degree murder, robbery, assault with a deadly weapon, and pled guilty to possession of a firearm by an ex-felon. The court further held that the 1978 death penalty was not unconstitutional per se.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court on the grounds that instructions to the jury were proper, there was not a violation of defendant's due process rights, defendant's challenges to an element were immaterial and the death penalty was not unconstitutional per se.

People v. Sanders


No. S004439, Crim. No. 22512


Supreme Court of California


51 Cal. 3d 471; 797 P.2d 561; 273 Cal. Rptr. 537; 1990 Cal. LEXIS 4385



September 27, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for a rehearing was denied November 28, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, No. 22079, Gerald K. Davis, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The heinous, atrocious and cruel special circumstance, as well as the burglary-murder special circumstance, is set aside. The judgment as to both guilt and penalty is otherwise affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his conviction in the Superior Court of Kern County (California) for first-degree murder, attempted murder, robbery, burglary, and attempted robbery. Defendant was sentenced to death based on special circumstance allegations that the murder was committed while he was engaged in the commission of burglary, the victim was intentionally killed to prevent her testimony, and the murder was especially heinous, atrocious, and cruel.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder, attempted murder, robbery, burglary, and attempted robbery regarding an attempt to rob drug dealers and to prevent his identification for the crimes. The jury found four special circumstance allegations were true, including the fact that the murder was committed while defendant was engaged in the commission or attempted commission of the robbery and burglary. In addition, the victim was intentionally killed to prevent her testimony in a criminal proceeding and the murder was especially heinous, atrocious, and cruel. The jury set the penalty at death. The court set aside the special circumstance findings based on the commission of a burglary and the conclusion that the murder was heinous, atrocious, and cruel, because the findings were constitutionally infirm. However, the court otherwise affirmed the judgment in its entirety because defendant did not demonstrate the race neutral procedures used to assemble the master jury list were constitutionally impermissible. Also, there was sufficient evidence of defendant's guilt based on eyewitnesses' identification, and the physical evidence linked him to the crimes.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions were affirmed, but the special circumstance finding that murder was based on the commission of a burglary was set aside because burglary based on intent to assault did not support a felony-murder jury instruction. Also, the special circumstance finding that murder was especially heinous, atrocious, and cruel was unconstitutionally vague. However, the death penalty was affirmed.

People v. Hedgecock


No. S005882


Supreme Court of California


51 Cal. 3d 395; 795 P.2d 1260; 272 Cal. Rptr. 803; 1990 Cal. LEXIS 4039



September 6, 1990 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, Nos. CR 71286 and CR 70436, William L. Todd, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: We reverse the judgment of the Court of Appeal, and direct that court to remand the matter to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion. In the habeas corpus proceeding, we affirm the Court of Appeal's order directing the issuance of an alternative writ returnable before the superior court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his convictions in the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), for conspiracy to violate state and local financial disclosure laws and perjury based on errors or omissions by him in disclosure statements. Appellant argued that he was entitled to a new trial based on juror misconduct, and that the question of whether appellant's misstatements and omissions were material should have been determined by the jury.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of conspiracy to violate state and local financial disclosure laws and perjury based on errors or omissions by him in disclosure statements. Appellant challenged his convictions on the grounds that he was entitled to a new trial based on juror misconduct, and that the question of whether appellant's misstatements and omissions were material should have been determined by the jury. The court reversed and remanded. The court held that in a perjury prosecution based on errors or omissions in disclosure statements, materiality is an element of the offense, and must therefore be determined by the jury. Thus, the court held that the trial court's instruction completely deprived the jury of an opportunity to consider the materiality of appellant's omissions and that constituted reversible error. Additionally, the court held that the trial court abused its discretion by failing to conduct an evidentiary hearing at which jurors could be subpoenaed to testify as to jury misconduct. The court held that permitting jurors to testify at evidentiary hearings is consistent with procedures at other stages of criminal proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded appellant's perjury conviction because in a perjury prosecution based on errors or omissions in disclosure statements, materiality was an element of the offense, and must therefore had to be determined by the jury. Thus, the trial court's instruction completely deprived the jury of an opportunity to consider the materiality of appellant's omissions, which constituted reversible error.

Davis v. City of Berkeley


No. S002285


Supreme Court of California


51 Cal. 3d 227; 794 P.2d 897; 272 Cal. Rptr. 139; 1990 Cal. LEXIS 3506



August 9, 1990 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 602403-5, Henry Ramsey, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), granting summary judgment to respondents, city and intervenors, when a ballot measure that specified the maximum number of low-rent housing units to be developed failed to specify any other information about the proposed project, allegedly contrary to Cal. Const. art. 34, § 1.


OVERVIEW: Respondent city received voter authorization to develop low-income public housing. Respondent subsequently submitted a federal funding application, which was accompanied by a certificate indicating that the voter approval required by Cal. Const. art. 34, § 1, had been obtained. Appellant taxpayers sought to have the project placed before the city's voters for approval. Appellants' request was denied and they filed an action to stay the development of the housing project on the basis that the ballot measures did not describe the project and was not approved by the voters. Two indigent residents intervened on behalf of the city. The trial court granted judgment in favor of respondents and appellants challenged. The court affirmed, holding that the approved ballot measures complied with the constitutional requirements and that respondent city was not compelled to secure further voter authorization before proceeding with the low-income housing proposal. A specific project description was not required, and respondents complied with the consistent interpretation of Cal. Const. art. 34, § 1, throughout its history.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed. Respondents, city and intervenors, were entitled to summary judgment on a low-income housing development proposal, because the ballot measures approved by the voters complied with the constitutional requirements and respondent city was not compelled to secure further voter authorization before proceeding with the low-income housing proposal.

Bay Development, Ltd. v. Superior Court


No. S000888


Supreme Court of California


50 Cal. 3d 1012; 791 P.2d 290; 269 Cal. Rptr. 720; 1990 Cal. LEXIS 2116



May 31, 1990 


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 46921, Douglas R. Woodworth, Judge.



DISPOSITION: In sum, we conclude that the trial court properly found that Home's settlement was a good faith settlement under section 877.6, subdivision (c), and that the settlement barred Bay's and Bowen's claim against Home for implied contractual indemnity.

The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant real estate broker sought review of an order of the Court of Appeal (California), which had upheld respondent trial court's denial of appellant's extraordinary writ. Appellant requested indemnity from real party in interest, seller, in an underlying action against appellant for fraudulent misrepresentation. The trial court held that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 877.6 barred appellant's claim.


OVERVIEW: Appellant real estate broker filed a cross-complaint against real party in interest, seller, and sought indemnification on the basis of both equitable indemnity and implied contractual indemnity theories if appellant was to be held liable for fraudulent misrepresentation in a sale of condominiums. Real party in interest entered into a settlement and was released and respondent trial court denied appellant's writ for relief. The appeals court affirmed and held that a good faith settlement under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 877.6(c) barred a claim for implied contractual indemnity. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that absent an express indemnification agreement specifying that one party was to bear all of the liability for a loss for which both parties were responsible, the loss was to be apportioned under comparative indemnity principles. The court held that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in finding that appellant had failed to demonstrate that the settlement was not in good faith. The court held that the evidence before the trial court provided substantial support that the settlement was in proportion to the responsibilities of the parties.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, which denied appellant real estate broker's request for an extraordinary writ. The court held that appellant's claim for indemnity was barred by a good faith settlement and release because there had been no indemnity agreement between the parties.

Supreme Court of California

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment imposed by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) under the 1978 death penalty law, Cal. Pen. OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions of defendant and imposition of the sentence of death on each of four murder convictions as well as defendant's determinate terms for attempted murders in their entirety, finding no violation of constitutional rights.

People v. Marshall, 1990 Cal. LEXIS 2027 (Cal., May 17, 1990)



DISPOSITION: For the foregoing reasons, we conclude that the appeal must be rejected and the judgment affirmed in number S004713 (Crim. No. 25438), and that the petition for writ of habeas corpus in number S009001 must be denied. It is so ordered.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Tulare County (California), which found him guilty under the 1978 death penalty law, Cal. Penal Code § 190.1 et seq., of murder under Cal. Penal Code § 187, robbery under Cal. Penal Code § 211, and burglary of a residence under Cal. Penal Code § 459, and sentenced him to death. The trial court's response to a jury request to define terms, while not perfect, was acceptable. The trial court's findings on individual and intracase proportionality were proper and it was correct in refusing to perform an intercase proportionality review. The court then denied defendant's habeas corpus petition, as defendant had effective assistance of counsel and although defendant sufficiently alleged juror misconduct, the presumption of prejudice was rebutted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and death sentence as the trial court correctly decided the issues of defendant's confessions, the prosecutor's peremptory challenges and summation comments, jury instructions, jury clarification questions, and proportionality review. The court further denied defendant's habeas corpus petition because he had effective assistance of counsel and the juror misconduct complained of did not prejudice him.

Delaney v. Superior Court


No. S006866


Supreme Court of California


50 Cal. 3d 785; 789 P.2d 934; 268 Cal. Rptr. 753; 1990 Cal. LEXIS 1842; 17 Media L. Rep. 1817



May 3, 1990  
May 3, 1990


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for rehearing of Real Parties in Interest DENIED July 11, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. HC206320, HC206321, Aurelio Munoz, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Court of Appeal is affirmed. The Court of Appeal is directed to issue a peremptory writ of mandate compelling respondent Los Angeles Superior Court: (1) to vacate its orders entered December 16, 1987, in case numbers HC 206320 and HC 206321, entitled In re Roxana Kopetman and In re Roberto Santiago Bertero, respectively, which orders granted their petitions for writs of habeas corpus; and (2) to simultaneously make new and different orders denying the petitions for writs of habeas corpus.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Real parties in interest reporters challenged the decision of the California Court of Appeals, which granted the request of petitioners, defendant and the State, to vacate respondent superior court's orders granting the reporters' petitions for writs of habeas corpus. The reporters claimed immunity from contempt under the shield law, Cal. Const. art. I, § 2(b), for their refusal to testify.


OVERVIEW: At issue was whether the term "unpublished information" in the state newsperson's shield law, Cal. Const. art. I, § 2(b), included a newsperson's nonconfidential, eyewitness observations of an occurrence in a public place and, if so, whether a newsperson could nevertheless be held in contempt for refusing to disclose such information in a criminal proceeding. Defendant was charged in a misdemeanor complaint with possession of brass knuckles in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 12020(a). Defendant sought testimony from reporters who witnessed his search and arrest, contending that he had not consented to the search by police officers. The reporters refused to testify and were found in contempt. They filed petitions for writs of habeas corpus, which were granted by the superior court. Agreeing with the Court of Appeals, the court held that the reporters' protection under the shield law had to yield to defendant's constitutional right to a fair trial when the reporters' refusal to disclose information would unduly infringe on that right. The superior court accordingly erred by granting the reporters' petitions for writs of habeas corpus.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the Court of Appeals' judgment and directed the Court of Appeals to issue a peremptory writ of mandate compelling the superior court to vacate its orders granting that granted the reporters' petitions and simultaneously issue new orders denying the petitions for writs of habeas corpus.

People v. Clark


No. S004662, Crim. No. 24342


Supreme Court of California


50 Cal. 3d 583; 789 P.2d 127; 268 Cal. Rptr. 399; 1990 Cal. LEXIS 1272



April 5, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified; Appellant's petition for rehearing DENIED June 7, 1990.
Modified by People v. Clark, 50 Cal. 3d 1157a, 1990 Cal. LEXIS 2283 (1990)
Rehearing denied by, Writ of certiorari denied Clark v. California, 498 U.S. 973, 111 S. Ct. 442, 112 L. Ed. 2d 425, 1990 U.S. LEXIS 5815, 59 U.S.L.W. 3363 (1990)
Stay granted by In re Clark, 1991 Cal. LEXIS 1423 (Cal., Mar. 27, 1991)
Writ of habeas corpus granted, Remanded by Clark v. Brown, 2006 U.S. App. LEXIS 6611 (9th Cir. Cal., Mar. 17, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A083960, Harry Mock, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The finding that defendant committed the murder charged in count II under the special circumstance of murder by means of explosives (§ 190.2, subd. (a)(6)) is stricken. The judgment is modified to direct that the term imposed on count V is stayed pending service of the terms imposed on counts II, III, and IV, the stay to become permanent upon service of those terms. As modified, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In his appeal, defendant asserted that many points of error occurred during the guilt and penalty phases of his trials that resulted in judgments of conviction and sentences for rape, first degree murder, attempted second degree murder, and arson by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of the rape of his former wife, first degree murder of his social worker's husband, attempted second degree murder of his social worker and her daughter, and arson of the social worker's home. The court rejected almost all of defendant's challenges to the convictions and sentences. The court held that the trial court erred when it applied the delivery of explosives special circumstance of Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(6) that rendered the death penalty applicable to defendant's first degree murder charge. Since gasoline was not an explosive and it was not delivered, the gasoline fire caused by defendant was not within the meaning of § 190.2(a)(6). Also, since the purpose of defendant's arson was for killing the victim, the court held that the trial court committed harmless error when it refused to instruct the jury that before the arson special circumstance of Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17)(viii) could be found true, the jury had to find that defendant had an independent felonious purpose for the commission of arson. Also, the court held that since defendant ignited fires in the victim's home, Cal. Penal Code § 654 required the arson term to be stayed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment as modified by the court. The court found merit in defendant's assertion that a delivery-of-explosives special aggravating circumstance was inapplicable because defendant's conduct was not within the meaning of the statute that imposed it. The court also held that one determinate term for arson had to be stayed because the crimes were committed by the act of igniting fires in the victim's home.

People v. Douglas


No. S004666


Supreme Court of California


50 Cal. 3d 468; 788 P.2d 640; 268 Cal. Rptr. 126; 1990 Cal. LEXIS 1224



April 2, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appellant's petition for rehearing DENIED June 21, 1990; As Modified June 21, 1990.

PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C-54321, Ronald Eugene Owen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of death is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California) that imposed death following his conviction of two counts of first degree murder pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 187 et seq., accompanied by the special circumstance finding of multiple murder pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(3).


OVERVIEW: Defendant convinced two victims to accompany him and his accomplice into the desert to make a film involving sexual bondage. Several hours after arriving at the desert the victims were tied and defendant forced them to have sex at gunpoint, and then choked, cut, and murdered the victims. Defendant was sentenced to death by the trial court for two counts of first degree murder under Cal. Penal Code § 187 and a special circumstance finding of multiple murder under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2. Defendant on appeal asserted numerous points of error against the State and trial court that occurred during the guilt and penalty phases of his trial. Defendant alleged error under the United States Constitution, California Constitution, and the California Penal Code. The court rejected every one of defendant's assertions and affirmed the trial court's judgment and sentence of death.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of conviction for two counts of first degree murder, and the accompanying sentence of death based on special circumstance finding of multiple murder, because the trial court and state did not commit any error during defendant's case.

DISPOSITION: Affirmed as modified.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Cross-complainant appealed from a judgment in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that granted in part complainant's motion for judgment notwithstanding the verdict, in an action for slander, invasion of privacy, and intentional and negligent infliction of emotional distress.


OVERVIEW: Complainant-respondent (respondent) sued cross-complainant (appellant) for slander, invasion of privacy, and intentional and negligent infliction of emotional distress. A cross-complaint was filed for violations of Cal. Penal Code § 632 and intentional infliction of emotional distress .The jury found for appellant on the complaint. On the cross-complaint, the jury found respondent had electronically recorded 24 confidential communications between appellant and his father, and awarded appellant $ 5,000 for each recording. The trial court granted in part respondent's motion for judgment notwithstanding the verdict. The evidence supported a finding of only two confidential communications that were surreptitiously recorded by respondent. The trial court should have granted respondent's motion for judgment notwithstanding the verdict as to the other 22 telephone conversations.


OUTCOME: Judgment was affirmed as modified. The evidence supported a finding that only two of the 24 confidential communications were surreptitiously recorded by complainant-respondent.

State Farm Mut. Auto. Ins. Co. v. Quackenbush


No. A080461.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE

December 23, 1999, Decided

PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Super. Ct. No. 977832. William Cahill, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The trial court's order is affirmed. State Farm shall recover its costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant insurance company and appellant insurance commissioner appealed an order from the Superior Court, City and County of San Francisco (California), which issued an order partly granting and partly denying the petition for a writ of administrative mandamus, in a case concerning regulatory scheme governing rate rollbacks.


OVERVIEW: This appeal arose from administrative proceedings to determine whether the appellant insurance companies would have been required to roll back their 1989 premium rates under Cal. Ins. Code §1861.01 et seq. The appellant insurance commissioner rejected a proposed decision by an administrative law judge which concluded appellant owed no refund to its policyholders. Appellant challenged the commissioner's ruling by petitioning for a writ of administrative mandamus. The court issued an order partly granting and partly denying the petition, and both parties appealed. The court held that the trial court's order was supported by substantial evidence and fully consistent with the regulatory scheme governing rate rollbacks.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed; the trial court's order partly granting and partly denying the petition for a writ of administrative mandamus was supported by substantial evidence and fully consistent with the regulatory scheme governing rate rollbacks.

 

298 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Hammond v. Agran


No. G024266.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


76 Cal. App. 4th 1181; 90 Cal. Rptr. 2d 876; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 1078; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9652; 99 Daily Journal DAR 12457



December 9, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:   [***1] Appeal from an order of the Superior Court of Orange County. Super. Ct. No. 798469. John C. Woolley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: It was error for the trial judge to have stricken the challenged language from Larry Agran's candidate's statement. The order is reversed with directions to enter a new order declaring that the language should not have been stricken.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant candidate challenged the Superior Court of Orange County's (California) order that granted appellee's petition for writ of mandate requesting that paragraphs of appellant candidate's statement be stricken, contending that they contained false, misleading and/or inconsistent information.


OVERVIEW: At issue in the case was the scope of the word "qualifications" as used in the Elections Code statute governing the content of candidates' statements in voters' pamphlets under Cal. Elec. Code § 13307. The court held that the word encompassed a candidate's ideas or platform. The court stated that the legislature envisioned that a candidate's ideas and views (as distinct from attacks on opponents) could fairly constitute "qualifications" within the meaning of the statute, and that given the breadth of the word, it would be inconsistent with the plain meaning of the statute to confine the idea of "qualifications" for office to resume items like degrees and experience in a profession.


OUTCOME: Order reversed and remanded, because it was error for the trial judge to have stricken the challenged language from appellant candidate's statement. The order was reversed with directions to enter a new order declaring that the language should not have been stricken.

Hoechst Celanese Corp. v. Franchise Tax Bd.


C030702


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


76 Cal. App. 4th 914; 90 Cal. Rptr. 2d 768; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 1063; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9553; 99 Daily Journal DAR 12285



December 3, 1999, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified on Denial of Rehearing of January 3, 2000, Reported at: 2000 Cal. App. LEXIS 1.
Review Granted March 1, 2000 (S085091), Reported at: 2000 Cal. LEXIS 1720.
Reprinted without change in the Review Granted Opinions Cumulative Supplement (July 2000) to permit tracking pending review and disposition by the Supreme Court.
Supreme Court of California Opinion of May 14, 2001, Reported at: 25 Cal. 4th 508.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County. Super. Ct. No. 96AS01954. William M. Gallagher, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant plaintiff challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which found the apportioned share of a pension reversion to them from a qualified pension trust constituted business income under Uniform Division of Income for Tax Purposes Act, Cal. Rev. & Tax Code § 25120 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellant filed a complaint for refund. Appellant created and maintained a benefit plan which covered both active and retired employees. Appellant also created a tax exempt trust to receive and invest their contributions. Appellant terminated the plans after it purchased annuities for retirees. Once the security plan obtained necessary approvals and satisfied all its obligations, it paid appellant a reversion. Appellant used the pension reversion funds to carry out a stock redemption program to reduce the number of outstanding shares. The trial court found that Cal. Rev. & Tax Code § 2510 (a) provides for two alternative, independent tests to classify business income: the transactional test and the functional test. The court found the pension reversion represented business income under the functional test, but not the transactional test. The court determined that the pension reversion was not business income under either test.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed; the record did not support a determination that the pension reversion was business income under either the transactional test or functional test to determine what constitutes business income for purposes of UDITPA.

Montclair Parkowners Assn. v. City of Montclair


No. E024137.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


76 Cal. App. 4th 784; 90 Cal. Rptr. 2d 598; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 1051; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9453; 99 Daily Journal DAR 12197



December 2, 1999, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of part II.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied Macrch 15, 2000, Reported at: 2000 Cal. LEXIS 2002.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from the Superior Court of Riverside County. Super. Ct. No. RCV 35814. Peter Norell, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appeal by plaintiffs of judgment of the Riverside County Superior Court (California), sustaining defendant city's demurrer that municipal rent control ordinance, as amended, was facially constitutional and dismissing plaintiff's action seeking to invalidate ordinance as unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs sought to invalidate ordinance enacted by defendant city on grounds it was unconstitutional on its face. Ordinance was enacted to amend existing rent control ordinance. Plaintiffs contended that enactment of the ordinance resulted in a regulatory taking of their property, without just compensation in violation of Cal. Const. art. I, § 19. The trial court sustained defendant's demurrer on the ground that ordinance was facially constitutional and dismissed the case. The court affirmed, concluding that ordinance on its face was not an unconstitutional regulatory taking. Protection of current mobile homeowners' equity in their homes and protection of prospective mobile homeowners from excessive rents were legitimate government interests, and defendant could have reasonably concluded that limiting plaintiffs' ability to raise rent upon sale or transfer of mobile homes would accomplish that goal by making mobile homes more attractive sale items and making mobile homeownership more feasible for prospective buyers.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed; ordinance on its face was not an unconstitutional regulatory taking, as protection of current mobile homeowners' equity in their homes and protection of prospective mobile homeowners from excessive rents were legitimate government interests.

Farmers Ins. Exch. v. Hurley


No. E023510.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


76 Cal. App. 4th 797; 90 Cal. Rptr. 2d 697; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 1056; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9488; 99 Daily Journal DAR 12193



December 2, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified on Denial of Rehearing of December 17, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 1096. Review Denied February 16, 2000, Reported at 2000 Cal. LEXIS 1151.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from the Superior Court of Riverside County. Super. Ct. No. 302566. Charles D. Field, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Costs to respondent.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant appealed from a decision of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which granted summary judgment in favor of appellee automobile insurer in an underinsured motorist benefits claim under Cal. Ins. Code § 11580(p)(3).


OVERVIEW: Appellant was injured in an automobile accident. The other driver was driving a rental car. Appellant sued both driver and rental company. Appellant did not collect any payment from the driver, however, the rental company agreed to a $ 15,000 judgment, its liability coverage limits. The parties agreed that if the rental company paid $ 5,000 within two months, it would fully satisfy the judgment. Appellant received her $ 5,000; dismissed the claim against the rental company; and, claimed underinsured motorist benefits under her policy. The trial court granted appellee's motion for summary judgment and appellant appealed. The court affirmed summary judgment in favor of appellee, holding that the plain language of Cal. Ins. Code § 11580.2(p)(3) required appellant to obtain actual payment of the rental company's full liability limits before she could recover underinsurance benefits from her own insurer.


OUTCOME: Summary judgment was affirmed because the plain language of Cal. Ins. Code § 11580.2(p)(3) required appellant to obtain actual payment of the rental company's full liability limits before she could recover underinsurance benefits from her own insurer.

People v. Lugas


H018805


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


76 Cal. App. 4th 696; 91 Cal. Rptr. 2d 11; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 1035; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9405; 99 Daily Journal DAR 12079



November 30, 1999, Filed


NOTICE:  [***1] NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Granted March 1, 2000 (S085015), Reported at: 2000 Cal. LEXIS 1718.
Reprinted without change in the Review Granted Opinions Cumulative Supplement (July 2000) to permit tracking pending review and disposition by the Supreme Ct.
Reprinted without change in the January 2001 Review Granted Opinions Pamphlet to permit tracking pending review and disposition by the Supreme Court.
Review Dismissed and cause remanded to the Ct. App. 6th Dist., November 14, 2001, Reported at: 2001 Cal. LEXIS 7840.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County. Super. Ct. No. 68830. LaDoris H. Cordell, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant criminal defendant challenged an order from the Santa Clara County Superior Court (California), which denied his petition for a certificate of rehabilitation under Cal. Penal Code §4852.01 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellant pled guilty to charges involving sexual misconduct against his daughter. Appellant was placed on probation and sentence was suspended. Appellant subsequently filed a petition for a certificate of rehabilitation. The trial court denied his request; Cal. Penal Code §4852.13, as amended in 1996, made issuance of a certificate of rehabilitation to a petitioner who met the statutory prerequisites discretionary, whereas under prior law issuance of a certificate was mandatory. He appealed, arguing that that application violated laws against ex post facto clauses, his constitutional due process rights, and it was inappropriately applied retroactively. The court held that the last act to trigger application of § 4852.13 as amended was the actual filing of the petition in 1997. At that time, existing law provided fair notice of the applicable judicial standard. Thus, § 4852.13 as amended in 1996 was applied prospectively to a petition filed after its effective date.


OUTCOME: Affirmed. At time of last act that triggered statute as amended, existing law provided fair notice of the applicable judicial standard, thus the amended statute was applied prospectively to a petition filed after its effective date, thus there was no retroactivity problem here.

Garcetti v. Superior Court


No. B133053.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


76 Cal. App. 4th 685; 90 Cal. Rptr. 2d 581; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 1036; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9389; 99 Daily Journal DAR 12065



November 30, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:   [***1] PETITION for Writ of Mandate. Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. ZM001967. Harold E. Shabo, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The respondent court is directed to vacate its decision and order granting the motion of real party in interest to dismiss the petition for commitment filed by the district attorney, and to enter a new and different order denying that motion, and to proceed with the petition according to law.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Request by petitioner district attorney for writ of mandate seeking to direct respondent Los Angeles County Superior Court (California) to vacate its earlier decision to dismiss petitioner's underlying petition, seeking probable cause hearing and trial as to whether real party in interest was sexually violent predator.


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest was convicted of forcible rape in 1972 and was sentenced to an indeterminate term. He was convicted of several crimes against another victim in 1982, including the sexually violent offense of rape, for which he was sentenced to a determinate term. Principal questions in case were: (1) whether conviction of a sexually violent offense could be counted as a predicate conviction under the Sexually Violent Predators Act (Act), Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6600 et seq., if defendant (real party in interest) was sentenced to an indeterminate term for that crime; and (2), if so, whether this was true even though a petition for commitment under the Act was filed before the 1996 amendments to the Act. The court concluded that the answer to both questions was yes, and issued a writ directing the respondent court to vacate its ruling to the contrary.


OUTCOME: Petition for writ granted; 1996 amendments to act pertaining to sexually violent predators applied to 1972 forcible rape conviction suffered by real party in interest.

People v. Martinez


No. B126166.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


76 Cal. App. 4th 489; 90 Cal. Rptr. 2d 517; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 1027; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9328; 99 Daily Journal DAR 11987; 99 Daily Journal DAR 12131



November 29, 1999, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for partial publication. The portions of the opinion to be deleted are the sections entitled "Discovery" and "Sufficiency of Evidence."


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied February 23, 2000, Reported at: 2000 Cal. LEXIS 1479.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. VA047004. John A. Torribio, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant criminal defendant challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of attempted murder and found that appellant personally and intentionally discharged a firearm causing great bodily injury.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted appellant of attempted murder and found that appellant personally and intentionally discharged a firearm causing great bodily injury. The court sentenced appellant to a base term of five years for attempted murder plus an enhancement of 25 years to life as required by Cal. Penal Code § 12022.53. On appeal, appellant contends that the trial court erred by imposing the 25-years-to-life enhancement pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 12022.53, and that imposing it was cruel or unusual punishment. The court held that Cal. Penal Code §§ 12022, 12022.7 may not be compared to § 12022.53, because they enhance the sentence for "any felony," whereas § 12022.53 is limited to designated felonies of a very serious type. More significantly, the legislature determined in enacting § 12022.53 that the use of firearms in commission of the designated felonies is such a danger that substantially longer prison sentences must be imposed.


OUTCOME: Judgment was affirmed. The enhancement law's purpose was to deter the use of firearms and save lives, and it did not amount to cruel or unusual punishment.

People v. Balestra


No. D031953.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


76 Cal. App. 4th 57; 90 Cal. Rptr. 2d 77; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 972; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8859; 99 Daily Journal DAR 11293



November 4, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied February 23, 2000, Reported at: 2000 Cal. LEXIS 1505.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County. Super. Ct. No. SCD134034. Richard J. Hanscom, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order granting probation) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, following her guilty plea to a charge of elder abuse, challenged a probation order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) requiring that appellant submit to a search condition, a drug and alcohol testing condition, and that she pay restitution to the victim.


OVERVIEW: Appellant pled guilty to a charge of elder abuse after she, having consumed alcohol, beat and terrorized her elderly mother following a domestic dispute. Appellant was granted probation on condition that she serve 210 days in custody, submit to searches with or without probable cause, undergo drug and alcohol testing if so directed, pay restitution to the victim, and repay probation and legal representation costs. Appellant argued that the restitution order was not supported by substantial evidence and that improper search and drug-testing probation conditions were imposed. The court held that the trial court did not abuse its discretion when it set the restitution amount because the evidence demonstrated a rational basis for its determination. The court further held that the imposition of the consent to search and drug-testing conditions of appellant's probation was proper because the conditions were reasonably related to the crime of which appellant was convicted.


OUTCOME: Probation order requiring that appellant submit to a search condition, drug and alcohol testing, and restitution was affirmed. Evidence demonstrated a rational basis for the trial court's restitution determination. Further, the imposition of the consent to search and drug-testing conditions of appellant's probation was proper because the conditions were reasonably related to the crime of which appellant was convicted.

 

308 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Vichroy


No. B127891.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


76 Cal. App. 4th 92; 90 Cal. Rptr. 2d 105; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 974; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8888; 99 Daily Journal DAR 11347



November 4, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied November 15, 1999. As Modified November 15, 1999. Review Denied February 2, 2000, Reported at: 2000 Cal. LEXIS 871.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. VA047633. Michael A. Cowell, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged criminal conviction from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), contending that the statute authorizing admission of evidence of prior sexual misconduct violated due process and Cal. Evid. Code § 352, and that the court erroneously gave the jury Cal. Jury Instructions- Criminal (CALJIC) No. 2.50.01.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of committing a lewd act upon a child ( Cal. Penal Code § 288(a)) and two counts of committing a forcible lewd act upon a child (§ 288(b)(1)). On appeal, appellant argued that the statute authorizing admission of evidence of prior sexual misconduct violated due process and Cal. Evid. Code § 352, and that Cal. Jury Instructions- Criminal (CALJIC) No. 2.50.01 was also unconstitutional. The court rejected appellant's first argument because no objection on that ground was raised at trial. The court, however, reversed on the second argument, holding that CALJIC No. 2.50.01 was constitutionally infirm, because the instruction permitted the jury to find appellant guilty of the current charges solely because he had committed prior sexual offenses, and the court could not assume the jury followed the constitutionally correct conflicting instruction.


OUTCOME: Conviction reversed, because the jury instruction in question permitted the jury to find appellant guilty of the current charges solely because he had committed prior sexual offenses, and the court could not assume the jury followed the constitutionally correct conflicting instruction.

 

309 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Wilson v. State Bd. of Education


No. A084485.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


75 Cal. App. 4th 1125; 89 Cal. Rptr. 2d 745; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 940; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8594; 99 Daily Journal DAR 10965



October 26, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied November 24, 1999. Review Denied January 25, 2000, Reported at: 2000 Cal. LEXIS 684.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, San Francisco County Super. Ct. No. 995602. Raymond D. Williamson, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The Charter Schools Act rests on solid constitutional ground. We affirm the judgment.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought review of denial of petition for a writ of mandate from the San Francisco Superior Court (California) commanding respondent board of education to refrain from granting charters or expending public funds under Charter Schools Act of 1992, Cal. Educ. Code §47600 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellants sought review of denial of petition for a writ of mandate commanding respondent to refrain from granting charters or expending public funds under Charter Schools Act of 1992 (Act), Cal. Educ. Code §47600 et seq. The appellant alleged the Act was unconstitutional on numerous grounds. The court determined that charter schools were public and thus under the jurisdiction of the public school system. In addition, the legislature had plenary power over public schools and broad discretion to determine the types of programs and services which broadened the purposes of education. The Act as amended did not run afoul of the constitutional prohibitions against public appropriations in aid of sectarian purposes or institutions. The Act did not conflict with the textbook adoption requirement of the state constitution or education laws and did not impermissibly delegate legislative powers. The court struck down appellants' challenges to the Act and ruled the Act rested on solid constitutional grounds.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed; challenges to the constitutionality of the Charter Schools Act were struck down because charter schools were determined public schools under jurisdiction of public school system which legislature had plenary power over.

People v. Green


F029122


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


75 Cal. App. 4th 1002; 89 Cal. Rptr. 2d 613; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 929; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8516; 99 Daily Journal DAR 10869



October 21, 1999, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted February 16, 2000 (S083960), Reported at: 2000 Cal. LEXIS 991. (See 81 Cal. App. 4th 463).


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Stanislaus County. Edward M. Lacy, Jr. Super. Ct. No. 55640. Aldo Girolami and Glen A. Ritchey, Jr., Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appeal of judgment of the Stanislaus County Superior Court (California) denying appellant's motion to strike his prior out of state murder conviction in failure to stop at scene of accident resulting in injury or death case.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was charged by information with failure to stop at the scene of an accident resulting in injury or death. The information further alleged that, during the commission of the offense, appellant inflicted great bodily injury and that appellant had suffered a 1978 serious felony conviction for murder in Texas. Appellant moved to strike his prior Texas murder conviction on the ground it was obtained in violation of Boykin/Tahl, but trial court denied the motion. The court affirmed, finding there was nothing in the record to indicate that Texas required Tahl-like admonishments when appellant's plea was taken. Allowing defendant to challenge a plea based on an out of state conviction not entered under Tahl-like protections was judicially inefficient and would saddle California trial courts with obligations not required by either the federal or state constitutions.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed; allowing defendant to challenge plea based on out of state conviction not entered under California recognized case law protections was judicially inefficient and would saddle California trial courts with obligations not required by either federal or state constitutions.

 

311 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Jordan v. Dep't of Motor Vehicles


No. C028340.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


75 Cal. App. 4th 449; 89 Cal. Rptr. 2d 333; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 895; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8161; 99 Daily Journal DAR 10369



October 1, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from the judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County. Superior Court Nos. 95AS03903, 95AS05228, 96AS06108. Joe S. Gray, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with respect to the order that defendants enter claims for refund on behalf of those who pay the motor vehicle smog impact fee after the date the judgment is entered and notify such persons that this system for refunds is in place. The judgment is also reversed with respect to the order that defendants review their records and file individual claims for refund or set up and file a class action refund claim on behalf of all those who paid the motor vehicle smog impact fee on or after September 19, 1992, but prior to entry of judgment. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed. The parties shall bear their own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed judgment in favor of the plaintiff by the Superior Court of Sacramento (California) which held Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code §§6261-6263 unconstitutional under the Commerce Clause, U.S. Const., art. I, § 8, cl. 3, and Cal. Const. art XIX, and ordered a refund of fees.


OVERVIEW:  Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code §§6261-6263 imposed a fee on vehicles last registered outside California, unless vehicle was California-certified. Plaintiffs, who moved to California, sought a refund and a declaration of unconstitutionality under the Commerce Clause, U.S. Const., art. I, § 8, cl. 3, and Cal. Const. art. XIX. The trial court found for plaintiffs. Defendants appealed. The court affirmed in part, holding Cal. Const. art. XIX was violated because it required fees and taxes imposed on vehicles to be used for transportation purposes, not deposited in the general fund. The fee violated the Commerce Clause because it discriminated between federally-certified and California-certified vehicles and defendants failed to show a legitimate local purpose that could not be adequately served by reasonable nondiscriminatory alternatives. The court reversed the fee refund, holding the trial court exceeded its jurisdiction in fashioning a remedy for taxpayers who were not properly before it.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed; fee violated state constitution as vehicle fees were to be used for transportation purposes and not deposited in general fund; commerce clause was violated due to discrimination between federally-certified and California-certified vehicles. Court reversed refund as trial court lacked jurisdiction for taxpayers not properly before it.

People v. Superior Court (perez)


No. B131539.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


75 Cal. App. 4th 394; 89 Cal. Rptr. 2d 326; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 889; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8129; 99 Daily Journal DAR 10339



September 30, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] ORIGINAL PROCEEDING; petition for writ of mandate. Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Super. Ct. No. ZM002592. Harold E. Shabo, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Let a writ of mandate issue directing the superior court to set aside its order granting real party's motion to dismiss, and to issue a new and different order denying the motion. The order to show cause is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Original proceeding for petition for writ of mandate, challenging the superior court's decision to dismiss the Sexually Violent Predators Act petition filed against real party in interest, as violating his equal protection rights.


OVERVIEW: The people filed a Sexually Violent Predators Act (SVP) petition against an offender, a legal immigrant, alleging that he was a sexually violent offender. The offender moved to dismiss the petition claiming that because he would be deported before he could receive the benefit of outpatient treatment provided for in the SVP Act, any commitment would violate his equal protection rights. The trial court dismissed the offender's SVP petition. The people sought a writ of mandate to set aside the dismissal. The court held that neither the action of the people in filing the SVP Act petition nor the decision of the federal government to defer action on the offender's deportation resulted in invidiously discriminatory disparate treatment under the otherwise neutral SVP Act. Further, because the attorney general had the nonreviewable discretion to defer deportation proceedings in favor of a SVP petition, the order dismissing the petition was premature.


OUTCOME: Writ granted; superior court directed to set aside its order granting real party's motion to dismiss, and to issue a new and different order denying the motion.

McCabe v. Snyder


No. C028944.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


75 Cal. App. 4th 337; 89 Cal. Rptr. 2d 315; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 881; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8083; 99 Daily Journal DAR 10251



September 29, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified October 21, 1999.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from the judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County. Super. Ct. No. 97SC02820. Judge Ronald B. Robie.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appeal of judgment of the Sacramento County Superior Court (California) denying petition for writ of mandamus to compel California Department of Motor Vehicles to disclose persons who paid motor vehicle smog impact fee.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff appealed from a judgment denying her petition for a writ of mandate compelling the California Department of Motor Vehicles (DMV) to disclose the names and addresses of those who paid the motor vehicle smog impact fee. Plaintiff sought this information in order to file a class refund claim based on her contention that the smog impact fee was unconstitutional. The court affirmed the denial of the petition, concluding that DMV had no implied duty to disclose the names and addresses of those who paid the smog impact fee in order to facilitate a class claim. Mandatory duty to disclose the names and addresses of those who paid a tax subject to future challenge would open door to potential abuse, as disclosure at the claim stage would be without court oversight.


OUTCOME: Denial of petition for writ affirmed, as Department of Motor Vehicles had no implied duty to disclose the names and addresses of those who paid the smog impact fee in order to facilitate a class claim.

 

314 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Van Winkle


No. F030661.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


75 Cal. App. 4th 133; 89 Cal. Rptr. 2d 28; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 865; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7964; 99 Daily Journal DAR 10053



September 24, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied December 15, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 8822.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Mariposa County. Super. Ct. No. 1763A. Richard L. McMechan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his jury conviction on two counts of lewd and lascivious conduct with a child in the Superior Court of Mariposa County (California) which used the Cal. Jury Instructions - Criminal No.§ 2.50.1, 2.50.01.


OVERVIEW: Prosecution convicted defendant of two counts of lewd and lascivious acts upon a child. Prosecution presented evidence of defendant's prior sexual misconduct. Defendant challenged the constitutionality of Cal. Jury Instructions - Criminal No.§ 2.50.1, 2.50.01. The constitutional attack against using prior sexual misconduct to prove defendant committed the current offense was rejected. Evidence of uncharged sexual offenses increasing the evidence of defendant's guilt did not reduce prosecution's burden of proving guilt beyond a reasonable doubt. The court held that prior sexual offenses that were collateral circumstances and proved by a preponderance of the evidence could be used to infer that the defendant committed the current sexual offense. Permissive inferences were constitutional because a rational factfinder could make the connection. Based on the jury instructions, prosecution still had the burden of proving every element beyond a reasonable doubt and the jury could not convict solely on proof of the other crimes.


OUTCOME: Conviction affirmed; the jury instructions did not unconstitutionally lower prosecution's burden of proving current sexual offenses beyond a reasonable doubt because the instructions met constitutional due process requirements and prior sexual offenses did not need to be proved beyond a reasonable doubt, but only by preponderance of the evidence.

Reprinted without change in the January 2001 Review Granted Opinions Pamphlet to permit tracking pending review and disposition by the Supreme Court.
Review Dismissed May 23, 2001, Reported at: 2001 Cal. LEXIS 3498.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Tulare County. Super. Ct. No. 37752. Howard R. Broadman and Joseph A. Kalashian, Judges. * 


*  Judge Broadman ruled on the People's motion to reconsider his ruling on defendant's pretrial motion to set aside the allegations that defendant tied or bound the victim in the commission of the offense; Judge Kalashian was the trial and sentencing judge.

 [***2]  



DISPOSITION: The case is remanded for resentencing on counts II and III. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appeal from a judgment of the Superior Court of Tulare County (California). Defendant was found guilty of two counts of forcible rape (counts I and II) and one count of forcible oral copulation (count III). Defendant was sentenced to consecutive life terms for counts I and III.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of two counts of forcible rape (counts I and II) and one count of forcible oral copulation (count III). The jury also found he engaged in tying or binding the victim during the crimes within meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 667.61 (e)(6). The appellate court considered the "tying or binding" allegations and the availability of consecutive and/or life sentences, and remanded the case to the trial court to resentence defendant on counts II and III, but affirmed the convictions and sentence in all other respects. The "One Strike Law," found in Cal. Penal Code § 667.61, required an indeterminate life term when defendant committed forcible sexual crimes in combination with enumerated factors. Cal. Penal Code §667.61's use of the term "single occasion" should be given its ordinary meaning, and defendant could be sentenced to only one life term under § 667.61.


OUTCOME: Convictions for two counts of forcible rape and one count of oral copulation were affirmed, but case was remanded for resentencing on counts II and III. Defendant could be sentenced to only one life term for his convictions since the crimes occurred during the same episode with the same victim.

COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE

September 22, 1999, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for partial publication. The portions of this opinion to be deleted from publication are those portions enclosed within double brackets, [[]].


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied December 21, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 9088.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. KA037355. Robert W. Armstrong, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the Los Angeles Superior Court, assigned by the Chief Justice pursuant to article VI, section 6 of the California Constitution.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified as follows: the conviction on count 3 for carjacking is vacated; the convictions on counts 4 and 5 for simple kidnapping are vacated; one of the section 667, subdivision (a), enhancements is vacated. In addition, Moore is to be credited with an additional three days of actual presentence custody. As modified, the judgment is affirmed. The superior court is directed to prepare an amended abstract of judgment accordingly.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) entering a term of imprisonment following his conviction for unlawful driving or taking of a vehicle, carjacking, kidnapping, and burglary.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of unlawful driving or taking of a vehicle, carjacking, kidnapping, and burglary. Defendant appealed claiming that his conviction for kidnapping during carjacking under Cal. Penal Code § 209.5 was unconstitutional. Specifically, defendant alleged that § 209.5 was unconstitutionally vague. He contended that the trial court erred by giving the jury California Jury Instruction No. 9.54.1, which defined the asportation element of section 209.5 as a substantial distance from the vicinity of the carjacking, a distance more than slight, brief, or trivial. On appeal, the court rejected the defendant's contention that the statute was unconstitutional and upheld the conviction. The court reasoned that a statute was not void for vagueness where a reasonable and practical construction could be given to its language.


OUTCOME: Judgment convicting defendant was upheld, because statute was not unconstitutionally vague.

People v. Buffington


No. C025957.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


74 Cal. App. 4th 1149; 88 Cal. Rptr. 2d 696; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 834; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7633; 99 Daily Journal DAR 9651



September 14, 1999, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of part 1 of the Discussion.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from the Judgment of the Superior Court of the County of Sacramento. Super. Ct. No. CR56225. Gary Ranson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of commitment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appeal by defendant from a judgment of the Superior Court of the County of Sacramento (California) that imposed on the defendant, who had served a prison term, a two-year civil commitment under the Sexually Violent Predators Act, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6600 et seq.


OVERVIEW: After serving a prison term for nine forcible rape convictions, defendant was found beyond a reasonable doubt to be an sexually violent predator and was committed to the state Department of Mental Health for two years under the Sexually Violent Predators Act (SVPA), Cal. Welf. & Inst. § 6600 et seq. Defendant appealed the commitment, alleging a due process and equal protection violation. The court affirmed, holding that the SVPA's requirement that the offender was likely to engage in sexually violent criminal behavior did not unconstitutionally circumvent or dilute the statute's reasonable doubt standard, thus no due process violation. Furthermore, the court found no equal protection violations as the state had a compelling state interest and the law treated individuals similarly.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, because a finding that the offender was likely to engage in sexually violent criminal behavior did not unconstitutionally circumvent or dilute statutory reasonable doubt standard; furthermore, there was no equal protection violation as the state had a compelling interest and the law treated individuals similarly.
