Lagatree v. Luce, Forward, Hamilton & Scripps


No. B124263, No. B125272.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


74 Cal. App. 4th 1105; 88 Cal. Rptr. 2d 664; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 829; 15 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 865; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7593; 99 Daily Journal DAR 9559



September 13, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied January 19, 2000, Reported at: 2000 Cal. LEXIS 262.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALS from judgments of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. BC185963. Super. Ct. No. BC185962. Wendell Mortimer, Jr., and Alexander H. Williams III, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appeal by plaintiff of judgments of the Los Angeles County Superior Court (California) sustaining defendants' demurrers in action for wrongful termination of employment.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff filed separate actions against defendant law firms, alleging that plaintiff had been terminated in violation of public policy for refusing to sign predispute arbitration agreements requiring that work-related disputes be resolved through binding arbitration. Defendants' demurrers to the complaints were sustained without leave to amend. The court affirmed, concluding that the terminations did not violate public policy. The rights at issue were subject to waiver by agreement, and additional factors weighed against recognition of a public policy claim. General social policies would not have been advanced by not allowing a wrongful termination claim in this case, as public policy favors the resolution of disputes through arbitration, and to impose liability on defendants would have thwarted that policy.


OUTCOME: Judgments affirmed; plaintiff could not state a cause of action for wrongful termination in violation of public policy, where he was discharged for refusing to sign arbitration agreement requiring that work-related disputes be resolved through binding arbitration.

People v. Hunt


No. C025177.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


74 Cal. App. 4th 939; 88 Cal. Rptr. 2d 524; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 814; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7358; 99 Daily Journal DAR 9351



September 7, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied October 5, 1999.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Tehama County. Super. Ct. No. CI41544. Edward J. King, III, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed, and the trial court is directed to dismiss the petition for defendant's commitment as a sexually violent predator.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from judgment of Superior Court of Tehama County (California), which found defendant sexually violent predator under Sexually Violent Predators Act, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6600 et seq. Defendant contended court-martial conviction in Germany did not qualify as conviction of sexually violent offense within meaning of Act.


OVERVIEW: Defendant criminal appealed from judgment finding him to be a sexually violent predator. Defendant contended that a court-martial conviction in Germany for indecent acts with a child did not qualify as conviction of sexually violent offense within meaning of Sexually Violent Predators Act (Act), Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6600 et seq. The court applied established principles of statutory interpretation and concluded that the statute's use of the term "a conviction in another state" was limited to convictions in one of the United States and did not encompass a conviction via military court-martial in Germany. Accordingly, the state failed to establish that defendant was convicted of a sexually violent offense against two or more persons within the meaning of the Act.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed and the petition for commitment dismissed because state failed to establish that defendant was convicted of a sexually violent offense against two or more persons. The court-martial conviction in Germany was not "a conviction in another state" under the statute.

Howard Jarvis Taxpayers Assn. v. City of La Habra


G020573


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


74 Cal. App. 4th 707; 88 Cal. Rptr. 2d 273; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 794; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7133; 99 Daily Journal DAR 9003



August 27, 1999, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted November 23, 1999 (S082591), Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 8168.
Reprinted without change in the January 2000 Review Granted Opinions Pamphlet to permit tracking pending review and disposition by the Supreme Ct.
Reprinted without change in the January 2001 Review Granted Opinions Pamphlet to permit tracking pending review and disposition by the Supreme Ct.
Supreme Court of California Opinion of June 4, 2001, Reported at: 25 Cal. 4th 809.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County. Super. Ct. No. 761512. Ronald C. Kline, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant appealed the order of the Orange County Superior Court (California) dismissing, in favor of respondents, as to when the statute of limitations commences to run on an action against a city which imposed a general tax on its residents with complying with Cal. Gov't code §§ 52720-53730.


OVERVIEW: Appellant brought an action against respondent city and county for declaratory relief, taxpayer's injunctive relief, and ordinary mandamus, contending that respondent city's utility tax was invalid under Cal. Gov't Code § 53723 (Proposition 62). The statute of limitations period commenced when respondent city's ordinance was enacted in December 1992. It was absurd for appellant to suggest it was forestalled or intimidated by prevailing case law from filing its suit at the time respondent city passed its ordinance. The basis of appellant's complaint was the legality of the ordinance imposing a utility tax without complying with Proposition 62's voter approval requirements of. The legality of that act had to be timely challenged and could not accrue continuously with each month's utility bill. Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 526a did not authorize an injunction to prevent the illegal collection of a tax. The taxpayer's injunctive relief cause of action was properly dismissed.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed; statute of limitations in Proposition 62 action commenced to run on the date respondent city enacted its ordinance authorizing the tax, not when it was declared to be constitutional or each month when the tax was paid.

Apartment Assn. of Los Angeles County, Inc. v. City of Los Angeles


B130243


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


74 Cal. App. 4th 681; 88 Cal. Rptr. 2d 255; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 792; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7038; 99 Daily Journal DAR 8951



August 26, 1999, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted December 15, 1999 (S082645), Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 8643.
Reprinted without change in the January 2000 Review Granted Opinions Pamphlet to permit tracking pending review and disposition by the Supreme Ct.
Supreme Court of California Opinion of January 8, 2001, Reported at: 24 Cal. 4th 830.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. BC195216. Charles W. McCoy, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) sustaining appellee city's demurrer and finding that the fee imposed by appellee to fund a program for the eradication of substandard housing was not subject to the requirements of Cal. Const. art. XIIID, § 6.


OVERVIEW: Appellants filed suit against appellee city for declaratory and injunctive relief, alleging that a city ordinance that imposed a fee on residential rental properties to fund a program for the eradication of substandard housing was a charge upon real property and, therefore, in violation of Cal. Const. art. XIIID, § 6. The trial court sustained appellee's demurrer, finding that the fee imposed by appellee was not subject to the requirements of Cal. Const. art. XIIID, § 6. On appeal, appellee contended that the fee was imposed upon a business activity, the rental of residential dwellings, that was separate and apart from property ownership, and purely for regulatory purposes. The court disagreed, holding that appellee's fee was void and unenforceable because it was imposed upon a parcel or upon a person as an incident of property ownership and was, therefore, subject to the procedural requirements of Cal. Const. XIIID, § 6.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed and cause remanded to the trial court with directions to vacate its order sustaining appellee's demurrer; appellee's fee was imposed upon a parcel or upon a person as an incident of property ownership and was, therefore, subject to constitutional procedural requirements.

Jenkins v. County of Los Angeles


No. B121351.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


74 Cal. App. 4th 524; 88 Cal. Rptr. 2d 149; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 780; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6882; 99 Daily Journal DAR 8743



August 23, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. BC157953. Wendell Mortimer, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. Appellant is to have his costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which granted summary judgment in favor of respondents under the Personal Responsibility Act of 1996, Cal. Civ. Code §3333.3.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sued the county and certain deputy sheriffs, claiming that he was injured as a result of an unlawful use of force. Summary judgment was granted in favor of the defendants on the ground that Cal. Civil Code §3333.3 barred the suit. On appeal the judgment was reversed because Cal. Civ. Code §3333.3 did not apply to causes of action for intentional torts, and appellant raised a triable issue of material fact as to whether his injuries were sustained during immediate flight from the commission of a felony.


OUTCOME: Judgment was reversed because the Personal Responsibility Act of 1996 did not apply to causes of action for intentional torts, and appellant raised a triable issue of material fact as to whether his injuries were sustained during immediate flight from the commission of a felony.

Pan Asia Venture Capital Corp. v. Hearst Corp.


Nos. A077561, A079071.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


74 Cal. App. 4th 424; 88 Cal. Rptr. 2d 118; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 773; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6844; 99 Daily Journal DAR 8651; 1999-2 Trade Cas. (CCH) P72,624



August 20, 1999, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts II., III., and IV.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified on Denial of Rehearing of September 20, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 851.
Review Denied December 15, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 8662.


PRIOR HISTORY: San Francisco County Superior Court. Super. Ct. No. 962780. Honorable Donald Mitchell, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment and attorney fee order are reversed. The order denying the motion for judgment not withstanding the verdict is affirmed. Appellants shall recover their costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant defendant appealed a judgment from the San Francisco County Superior Court (California), which denied its motions for a new trial and for judgment not withstanding the verdict in a contracts case concerning California's Unfair Practices Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 17043.


OVERVIEW: This suit between two newspaper competitors arose out of a contract to be the city's official newspaper. The jury returned a unanimous "special" verdict for the plaintiff, defendant's motions for a new trial and for judgment not withstanding the verdict were denied, and it appealed. The court held that an unbroken line of decisional authority holds that just what constitutes an economic actor's "cost" is an issue of fact to be decided by the trier of fact. In this case, however, the trial court decided the cost issue as a matter of law in circumstances that virtually ensured a verdict for the plaintiff. The court further held that each side pointed to flaws in other's economic model to determine cost, and neither was so strong that it could have banished other. Therefore, both approaches were sufficiently reasonable that both ought to have been put before jury.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed; each side pointed to flaws in other's economic model to determine cost, and neither was so strong that it could have banished other. Therefore, both approaches were sufficiently reasonable that both ought to have been put before jury.

People v. Callahan


No. F028124.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


74 Cal. App. 4th 356; 87 Cal. Rptr. 2d 838; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 768; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6774; 99 Daily Journal DAR 8591



August 18, 1999, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts I(C), I(D), II and III.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied November 17, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 8126.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Kern County. Super. Ct. No. 68788A. Clarence Westra, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his conviction by the Superior Court of Kern County (California) for committing a lewd or lascivious act upon a child under the age of 14 in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288 (a).


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of a lewd or lascivious act with a child under the age of 14 in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288 (a). Appellant challenged the conviction, alleging evidentiary rulings deprived him of a fair trial. In affirming, the court held that Cal. Evid. Code § 1108 permitted appellee state to present evidence of an appellant's commission of another sexual offense. The court held that although the trial court erred in preventing appellant from presenting rebuttal evidence, the error did not warrant reversal because the evidence would not have affected the outcome of the case.


OUTCOME: Judgment was affirmed. Appellee state was permitted to present evidence of appellant's commission of other sexual offenses. Denial of appellant's request to present character witnesses was not prejudicial because it would not have affected the outcome.

DiLoreto v. Board of Education


No. B127050.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


74 Cal. App. 4th 267; 87 Cal. Rptr. 2d 791; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 756; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6665; 99 Daily Journal DAR 8473



August 17, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied December 1, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 8551.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. VC024435. Thomas I. McKnew, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) entered after the trial court granted summary judgment in favor of respondents. Amicus curiae supported appellant's appeal in this right to free speech case.


OVERVIEW: Appellant challenged a judgment entered after the trial court granted summary judgment in favor of respondents. The appellate court affirmed. On appeal, the court was asked to determine whether a public school's rejection of a sign featuring the Ten Commandments, which was submitted by a private party in response to a fund-raising solicitation for commercial advertisements to be posted on the school's baseball field, violated that individual's right to free speech under U.S. Const. art. I, § 2. The court had to decide whether establishment clause considerations embodied in Cal. Const. art. I, § 4 outweighed free speech rights. Applying the three-part Lemon test, the court held the posting would have violated the establishment clause and would have given the impression that the state had placed its imprimatur on a particular religious creed.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed. Posting of advertisement featuring Ten Commandments on public school's baseball field would have violated establishment clause and would have given impression that state placed its imprimatur on particular religious creed.

 

327 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Durgom v. Janowiak


No. E022422.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


74 Cal. App. 4th 178; 87 Cal. Rptr. 2d 619; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 748; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6552; 99 Daily Journal DAR 8349



August 12, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from the Superior Court of Riverside County. Super. Ct. No. 87813. Robert G. Taylor, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. Janowiak has asked that each party bear its own costs. Durgom has not opposed this request, and we find it to be in the interests of justice. (See Cal. Rules of Court, rule 26(a).) Accordingly, each party shall bear its own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed order of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California) that granted defendant's motion to dismiss for lack of state court jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: Defendant failed to make payments to plaintiff pursuant to an assignment of royalties from a copyrighted song. In his answer, defendant asserted that, as a matter of federal law, the beginning of a renewal term had revested the copyright in the author and had cut off plaintiff's right to receive royalties. Defendant urged that the presence of this issue invoked exclusive federal copyright jurisdiction. The trial court agreed and dismissed the complaint. On appeal, the court reversed. Nonpayment of royalties was a contract issue not preempted by federal copyright law, and the states are expressly allowed to regulate activities violating legal or equitable rights that are not equivalent to any of the exclusive rights within the general scope of copyright law. Since a case could not be removed to the federal court on the basis of a federal copyright defense, the trial court erred in dismissing the complaint.


OUTCOME: Judgement reversed; the trial court erred in dismissing case because nonpayment of royalties was contract issue not preempted by federal copyright law, so the state court had jurisdiction.

People v. Felarca


H018080


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


74 Cal. App. 4th 972; 88 Cal. Rptr. 2d 587; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 819; 99 Daily Journal DAR 9413



August 6, 1999, Filed


NOTICE:  [***1] NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: The Publication Status of this Document has been Changed by the Court from Unpublished to Published September 3, 1999. Review Denied November 10, 1999 and the Reporter of Decisions directed not to published this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 7977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Santa Clara County Superior Court. Super.Ct.No. 199219. Honorable Edgar Taylor, Trial Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff state appealed the judgment of the Santa Clara County Superior Court (California) finding that sex offender registration under Cal. Penal Code §290 constituted cruel and unusual punishment as applied to defendant, after his conviction.


OVERVIEW: The trial court granted defendant's motion to declare that sex offender registration would constitute cruel and unusual punishment in violation of the Eighth Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. VIII, and Cal. Const. art. VIII, § 17. Plaintiff contended that it was not cruel and unusual because defendant was convicted of two felony sex offenses. Defendant was 28 years old, and although did not use physical force, it could not be said that the victim consented to the acts as part of a romantic relationship. The registration requirement did not shock the conscience and offend fundamental notions of human dignity. The classification did not withstand equal protection scrutiny, as Cal. Penal Code §290 required those who engaged in oral copulation with minors under 16 to register as sex offenders, but making no requirement for those who engaged in sexual intercourse with minors under 16 was a distinction without rational support.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed; sex offender registration did not constitute cruel and unusual punishment but did not withstand equal protection scrutiny; as the statute made distinctions in certain sex crimes that were without rational support.

Kim v. Workers' Comp. Appeals Bd.


No. B125203.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN


73 Cal. App. 4th 1357; 87 Cal. Rptr. 2d 382; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 723; 64 Cal. Comp. Cases 968; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6271; 99 Daily Journal DAR 7996



August 4, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified August 18, 1999.


PRIOR HISTORY: PETITION for review of a decision of the Workers' Compensation Appeals Board. W.C.A.B. Nos. MON 225854; MON 222050.



DISPOSITION: The petition for review is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employee sought review of a decision of the Workers' Compensation Appeals Board (California), which ruled that his interpreter expenses were subject to a $ 16,000 statutory cap, pursuant to Cal. Lab. Code § 139.5.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner employee injured his arm while working. He was found entitled to vocational rehabilitation services paid for through workers' compensation. Petitioner required the assistance of an interpreter for all vocational training. Petitioner contended that the interpreter expenses associated with his rehabilitation should not be included among the expenses subject to the $ 16,000 cap under Cal. Lab. Code § 139.5. The appellate court affirmed, holding that the agency's construction was in accord with the plain language of the statute, which specified an intent to broadly include within the cap all expenses associated with vocational rehabilitation services. The court further held that there was no explicit discrimination against petitioner. Finally, petitioner made no showing that Cal. Lab. Code § 139.5's expense cap disproportionately impacted a protected class of persons, and he offered no evidence of a legislative purpose or intent to discriminate.


OUTCOME: Petition denied; plain language of statute specified an intent to broadly include within the cap all expenses associated with vocational rehabilitation. Petitioner made no showing that the expense cap disproportionately impacted a protected class of persons, and he offered no evidence of a legislative purpose or intent to discriminate.

People v. Hurtado


D029586


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


73 Cal. App. 4th 1243; 88 Cal. Rptr. 2d 389; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 721; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6218; 99 Daily Journal DAR 7947



August 3, 1999, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted October 20, 1999 (S082112), Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 7204.
Reprinted without change in the January 2000 Review Granted Opinions Pamphlet to permit tracking pending review and disposition by the Supreme Ct.
Reprinted without change in the January 2001 Review Granted Opinions Pamphlet to permit tracking pending review and disposition by the Supreme Court.
Reprinted without change in the January 2002 Review Granted Opinions Pamphlet to permit tracking pending review and disposition by the Supreme Court.
Supreme Court of California Opinion of August 22, 2002, Reported at: 28 Cal. 4th 1179.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from an order of the Superior Court of Imperial County. Super. Ct. No. 13328A. Joseph Zimmerman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Imperial County (California) committing him to two years in accordance with the Sexually Violent Predators Act, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code, § 6600 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellant prisoner sought review of his commitment under the Sexually Violent Predators Act (Act), Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6600 et seq. The court found that the Legislature inadvertently failed to add the term "predatory" to its definition of "sexually violent predator" in §6600(a) during the amendment process. While the instruction given to the jury did not require it to find appellant was likely to engage in "predatory" behavior, under the circumstances, it was harmless and did not prejudice appellant. The court found that the two classes of offenders in the Act and another sex offender statute posed different risks to the general public and were not similarly situated. Further, the Act was narrowly drafted to serve a legitimate/compelling state interest. Thus, the Act did not violate equal protection guarantees. The court also found the 10-day time period in the statute was directory, not jurisdictional.


OUTCOME: Affirmed; because while jury instruction did not require it to find appellant was likely to engage in "predatory" behavior, under circumstances, it was harmless and did not prejudice appellant and because Act was narrowly drafted to serve legitimate/compelling state interest. Thus, Act did not violate equal protection guarantees.

 

332 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Golden Gateway Center v. San Francisco Residential Rent Stabilization & Arbitration Bd.


No. A083297.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


73 Cal. App. 4th 1204; 87 Cal. Rptr. 2d 332; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 717; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6191; 99 Daily Journal DAR 7905



August 2, 1999, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of part III.D.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Super. Ct. No. 982216. Raymond D. Williamson, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: We hold that a landlord who undertakes to perform reasonably necessary repair and maintenance work on rental property, which has the effect of temporarily interfering with or preventing the tenant's full use of housing services, but does not substantially interfere with the right to occupancy of the premises as a residence, does not effectuate a decrease in housing services within the meaning of the San Francisco rent control ordinances. Accordingly, we reverse the trial court's denial of GGC's petition for writ of mandate seeking to overturn the Board's decision ordering a reduction in the petitioning tenants' rent. We affirm, however, the trial court's denial of GGC's claim for declaratory relief, as it failed to present evidence to support its claim. The matter is remanded for further proceedings. GGC shall recover its costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed from an order in the Superior Court of San Francisco County (California), which denied its petition for a writ of mandate seeking to overturn the arbitration board's decision ordering a reduction in tenants' rent under the Residential Rent Stabilization and Arbitration Ordinance, San Francisco Admin. Code § 37.1 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, apartment owner, undertook repair work on its rental property. For four months, certain tenants suffered discomfort and began filing petitions with respondent San Francisco Residential Rent Stabilization and Arbitration Board, alleging that they had suffered decreases in housing services. After a hearing, 37 tenants were awarded rent reductions of $ 100 per month for each of the four months their decks were not fully usable. After respondent denied petitioner's administrative appeal, petitioner requested a writ of administrative mandamus in the trial court as well as declaratory relief, which were both denied. This appeal followed. The appellate court reversed on the merits, holding that a landlord who undertakes to perform reasonably necessary repair and maintenance that temporarily prevents the full use of services, but does not substantially interfere with the right to occupancy, does not effectuate a decrease in housing services under San Francisco Admin. Code § 37.1 et seq.


OUTCOME: Denial of declaratory relief was affirmed because petitioner failed to present evidence to support its claim. Denial of petition for writ of mandate was reversed and remanded because interference with housing services resulting from reasonably necessary repair to rental units did not represent a decrease in services justifying a rent reduction.

People v. Bersamina


A082268


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


73 Cal. App. 4th 930; 87 Cal. Rptr. 2d 43; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 691; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5983; 99 Daily Journal DAR 7607



July 27, 1999, Filed


NOTICE:  [***1] NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied October 20, 1999 and the Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 7236.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County. Contra Costa County Super. Ct. No. 9715285. James J. Marchiano.



DISPOSITION: The conviction under count one (§ 288) is reversed; the remainder of the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction by the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California) for lewd conduct on a child, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 228(a), and residential burglary, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 459- 460(a).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of lewd conduct on a child, pursuant to Cal. Pen. Code § 288(a), and residential burglary. pursuant to Cal. Pen. Code § 459- 460(a). Appellant challenged. The court found the jury instruction to be constitutionally infirm. In affirming defendant's burglary conviction, the court held that there was substantial evidence to support the conviction.


OUTCOME: Conviction for lewd conduct on a child was reversed. The jury was erroneously instructed that it could conclude that defendant committed the crime of lewd conduct on a child if it found, by a preponderance of the evidence, that he had committed a prior sexual offense. Substantial evidence supported the burglary conviction.

People v. Custodio


No. C027999.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


73 Cal. App. 4th 807; 87 Cal. Rptr. 2d 18; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 679; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5842; 99 Daily Journal DAR 7469



July 22, 1999, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts II-IV.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Lassen County. Super. Ct. No. CC-012258. Robert A. Barclay, Judge. *


* Judge of the former Justice Court for the Modoc Judicial District, assigned by the Chief Justice pursuant to article VI, section 6 of the California Constitution.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified to give defendant credit for 55 days of actual custody and 26 days for good conduct, a total of 81 days of presentence credit. As modified, the judgment is affirmed. The trial court is directed to amend the abstract of judgment to reflect the modification, and to send a copy of the amended abstract to the Department of Corrections.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appeal from conviction for possessing a sharp instrument while confined in a penal institution, in the Superior Court of Lassen County (California), asserting that Cal. Penal Code § 4502(a) was unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted appellant of possessing a sharp instrument while confined in a penal institution, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 4502(a), and found that he had a prior serious felony conviction within the meaning of the "three strikes law" (§ 667(d), (e)) and had served two prior separate prison terms within the meaning of § 667.5(b). He was sentenced to an aggregate prison term of eight years. On appeal, the court rejected appellant's contention that § 4502 was unconstitutionally vague, because a person of ordinary intelligence would know what is and what is not prohibited by the term "sharp instrument" in the statute. The "cup pick" found in appellant's possession was capable of being used to inflict injury as a stabbing device, and the instrument was not necessary for defendant to have in his possession. The judgment was modified to award the appropriate presentence credits.


OUTCOME: Conviction affirmed; the statute was not unconstitutionally vague, because a person of ordinary intelligence would know what is and what is not prohibited by the term "sharp instrument" in the statute, and the "cup pick" was not necessary for defendant to have in his possession.

People v. Tillman


No. A079134, No. A083670.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


73 Cal. App. 4th 771; 86 Cal. Rptr. 2d 715; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 675; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5827; 99 Daily Journal DAR 7403



July 21, 1999, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to rules 976(b) and 976.1, California Rules of Court, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of part I.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied August 19, 1999. Review Denied November 10, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 7823.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County. Superior Court No. SC39904A. Carl W. Holm, Judge.

Original Opinion of March 8, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 193.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. The petition for writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction for failing to register as a sex offender from the San Mateo County Superior Court, Superior Court (California), and petitioned for a writ of habeas corpus.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed from a conviction of failing to register as a sex offender. He claimed he received ineffective assistance and petitioned for a writ of habeas corpus. Defendant additionally urged the use of his prior rape conviction both as an element of the substantive registration offense and as a strike to enhance his sentence was improper. The court determined that the lower court properly used defendant's prior rape conviction both as an element of the Cal. Penal code § 290(a)(1) violation and as a violation as a strike that augmented defendant's sentence under Cal. Penal Code § 1170.12(c)(2). The court determined that the Three Strikes law was intended to apply to all recidivists coming within its terms. This intent would be frustrated by allowing the limitation of prior convictions that could be used to trigger application of the Three Strikes law. Prohibiting the dual use of prior convictions was inconsistent with the Three Strikes law as a whole.


OUTCOME: Judgment was affirmed. A prior rape conviction was properly used as both an element of failure to register violation and as a strike that augmented defendant's sentence. Prohibiting the dual use of prior convictions was inconsistent with the Three Strikes law. The writ of habeas corpus was denied.

San Diego County Court Clerks Ass'n v. Superior Court


D032781


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


73 Cal. App. 4th 725; 86 Cal. Rptr. 2d 679; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 671; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5786; 99 Daily Journal DAR 7373



July 20, 1999, Filed


NOTICE:  [***1] NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied October 27, 1999 and the Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 7732.



DISPOSITION: The petition is denied. Petitioner to pay respondents' costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, the San Diego County Court Clerks Association, sought a writ of mandate ordering the San Diego County Superior Court (California) to immediately restore their civil service status.


OVERVIEW: This case arose from the unification of the municipal and superior courts of San Diego County, in accordance with a majority vote of the judges taken after the electorate's approval of Proposition 220, Cal. Const. art. VI, § 5, cl. (e). Petitioners, the San Diego County Court Clerks Association, which represented the employees automatically transferred from municipal to superior court, sought a writ of mandate ordering the superior court to immediately restore their civil service status. The court examined Cal. Gov't Code § 70217(a), and noted that it provided that upon unification, the transferred employees would maintain their current classifications and pay, and other benefits. But the Legislature elected not to provide that any existing civil service status would be recognized after court unification. The court reasoned that the petitioners lacked any vested right to civil service status. Therefore, the court rejected the petition.


OUTCOME: The petition was denied. The employees automatically transferred from municipal to superior court under Proposition 220 had no statutory or otherwise vested right to civil service status.

 

337 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Howard Jarvis Taxpayers Assn. v. City of Riverside


No. E022717.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


73 Cal. App. 4th 679; 86 Cal. Rptr. 2d 592; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 666; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5739; 99 Daily Journal DAR 7303



July 16, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied October 20, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 7373.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from the Superior Court of Riverside County. Super. Ct. No. RIC-299784. Stephen D. Cunnison, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. The City shall recover costs on appeal against plaintiffs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, tax reduction advocacy organizations, appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California) that defendant City of Riverside's streetlighting assessments came within Proposition 218's exemption for assessments imposed exclusively to finance the maintenance and operation of streets and sidewalks.


OVERVIEW: When government entities circumvented proposition 13 "tax" restrictions by passing "special assessments," the electorate adopted Proposition 218, which amended the California Constitution by placing analogous restrictions on assessments, fees, and charges. Plaintiffs, three organizations that advocated tax reduction, challenged the defendant City of Riverside's (City) annual assessment to pay electricity for streetlights after an election to continue the assessment did not pass by the two-thirds vote required by Proposition 218. The court held that the City's pre-existing streetlighting assessment was grandfathered and exempt under Proposition 218. The court found that electrical current was necessary to operate streetlights; which were necessary to operate streets and sidewalks by making streets and sidewalks safer. The Cal. Const. art. XIII D, § 5 (a), exempting assessments which financed the capital costs or maintenance and operation expenses for sidewalks, streets, sewers, water, flood control, drainage systems, or vector control, applied to this assessment.


OUTCOME: Judgment was affirmed because pre-existing streetlighting assessment was grandfathered and exempt under Proposition 218 since electrical current was necessary to operate streetlights, and streetlights were necessary to operate streets and sidewalks by making streets and sidewalks safer.

 

338 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Connecticut Indemnity Co. v. Superior Court


C027794, C027795


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


73 Cal. App. 4th 540; 86 Cal. Rptr. 2d 515; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 655; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5567; 99 Daily Journal DAR 7065



July 12, 1999, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted September 29, 1999 (S065841, S065822), Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 6598.
Reprinted without change in the January 2000 Review Granted Opinions Pamphlet to permit tracking pending review and disposition by the Supreme Ct.
Supreme Court of California Opinion of July 24, 2000, Reported at: 23 Cal. 4th 807.


PRIOR HISTORY: ORIGINAL PROCEEDING: Application for a writ of mandate. Super. Ct. No. CV002615. Super. Ct. No. CV002615.



DISPOSITION: Writ Issued.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner insurers sought writs of mandate to compel respondent, the Superior Court of San Joaquin County (California), to quash administrative subpoenas duces tecum issued by the City of Lodi under Cal. Gov't Code § 37104 or to grant protective orders limiting the documents that they could be compelled to produce.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, several insurance companies, sought writs of mandate to compel the Superior Court of San Joaquin County to quash administrative subpoenas issued by the City of Lodi (Lodi) under Cal. Gov't Code § 37104 or to grant protective orders limiting the documents that they could be compelled to produce. Lodi was charged with the responsibility for cleaning up a contaminated groundwater site by prosecuting potentially responsible parties (PRPs). Petitioners argued that the subpoenas sought premature prelitigation discovery prohibited by Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 2017 and sought documents protected by the right of privacy. The court held that the privacy limitations that applied to discovery by subpoena in civil litigation also applied to the investigatory subpoenas issued by Lodi under Cal. Gov't Code § 37104. Therefore, the court granted petitioners' writ of mandate and issued an order limiting disclosure to matters prescribed in Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 2017(b).


OUTCOME: The writ of mandate was granted. Petitioners were entitled to an order limiting disclosure of privileged matters, because privacy limitations that applied to discovery by subpoena in civil litigation also applied to pre-litigation investigatory subpoenas.

 

339 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Hoffman v. Connell


No. A085214.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


73 Cal. App. 4th 1194; 87 Cal. Rptr. 2d 272; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 716; 99 Daily Journal DAR 7913



July 2, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Publication Status of this Document has been Changed by the Court from Unpublished to Published July 30, 1999.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sonoma County. Super. Ct. No. 217934. Arnold D. Rosenfield, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. The trial court is ordered to enter a new judgment in favor of the executors.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the executors of the estate of the decedent, sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Sonoma County (California), contending that it erred when it ruled they were obligated to pay California estate tax on property that had been placed into a qualified terminable interest property trust pursuant to 26 U.S.C.S. § 2056(b)(7) by the decedent's late husband.


OVERVIEW: Decedent's late husband, a resident of the United States Virgin Islands, established a qualified terminable interest property (QTIP) trust pursuant to 26 U.S.C.S. § 2056(b)(7) whereby she received income, but the corpus was to pass to his son upon her death. The decedent resided in California until her death. The state controller issued tax deficiency notice to appellants, executors of estate, because state tax return did not include value of trust. Appellants filed a complaint to have deficiency declared invalid. The parties filed cross-motions for summary judgment. The trial court ruled deficiency was proper. Appellants then sought review. The court concluded that whether tax was owed under Cal. Rev. & Tax Code § 13302 depended on whether decedent owned the property in the QTIP trust, and analyzed whether decedent could be said to have owned the property under federal, California, or United States Virgin Islands law. The court found that she would not be considered the owner of the trust property under federal, California, or United States Virgin Islands law. Since decedent did not own the property, her estate was not obligated to pay tax on it, and the judgment was reversed.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed, and the trial court was ordered to enter a new judgment in favor of appellants, because the decedent did not own the property in the qualified terminable interest property trust under federal, California, or the United States Virgin Islands law, and her estate was not obligated to pay tax on it.

 

340 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Gammoh v. City of Anaheim


No. G020502.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


73 Cal. App. 4th 186; 86 Cal. Rptr. 2d 194; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 625; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5247; 99 Daily Journal DAR 6685



June 29, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified on Denial of Rehearing July 29, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 703. Review Denied September 29, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 6982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County. Super. Ct. No. 736182. Tully H. Seymour, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment in the city's favor is reversed. The court is directed to issue a writ of mandate to approve Gammoh's second application (the one for the scaled back theater), and the case is otherwise remanded for trial on Gammoh's civil rights cause of action. Gammoh will recover his costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which held to be constitutional an ordinance passed by respondent that effectively precluded the granting of appellant's application to open an adult nightclub.


OVERVIEW: Appellant brought suit against respondent for both injunctive relief and damages for violations of his civil rights. The suit resulted from respondent's denial of appellant's application to open an adult nightclub. While the application was pending, respondent amended its adult entertainment ordinance, which effectively precluded appellant's application from being granted. The trial court found that the ordinance was constitutional, based on a previous denial of appellant's request for a preliminary injunction. On appeal, the court reversed because it found that the amended ordinance did not pass constitutional muster. The court found that respondent could not utilize "redevelopment" as a means to arbitrarily exclude adult businesses. Respondent could not use its power to zone as a pretext for suppressing appellant's U.S. Const. amend. I rights. As such, the court ordered that the judgment of the trial court be reversed and the case remanded.


OUTCOME: Finding that respondent's retroactive redevelopment constituted an unconstitutional pretext to suppress appellant's First Amendment rights, the court reversed the judgment of the trial court and remanded for a trial on appellant's civil rights cause of action.

People v. Orange County Charitable Services


No. G017604.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


73 Cal. App. 4th 1054; 87 Cal. Rptr. 2d 253; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 711; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6119; 99 Daily Journal DAR 7781



June 29, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Publication Status of this Document has been Changed by the Court from Unpublished to Published July 29, 1999. As Modified on Denial of Rehearing July 29, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 710. Review Denied September 29, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 6981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County. Super. Ct. No. 700247. Robert A. Knox, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the Orange Superior Court, assigned by the Chief Justice pursuant to article VI, section 6 of the California Constitution.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. The Attorney General shall recover costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Finding appellants conducted charity fundraising activities for profit in disregard of fiduciary duties and statutory law, Superior Court of Orange County (California), enjoined appellants from fundraising pending a complete accounting, ordered appellants to register as commercial fundraisers and maintain required bonds, imposed charitable trust on moneys recovered, and reserved jurisdiction to determine authorized civil penalties and costs.


OVERVIEW: Seeking to vindicate the public interest, respondent attorney general brought action against the predatory practices of more than 130 individual and business entities engaged in commercial fundraising. Causes of action included conspiracy to defraud, unfair competition, false advertising, and breach of fiduciary duty. Pending an accounting, the trial court enjoined appellants from fundraising and imposed a charitable trust on any moneys recovered. Because civil penalties were authorized by various statutes, the court reserved jurisdiction to determine the proper formula to be applied upon completion of the accounting. Under the Uniform Supervision of Trustees for Charitable Purposes Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 12598(b)-(c), the trial court concluded that respondent was entitled to an award for the costs of investigating and bringing the action. Appellants sought review. The court affirmed, holding that evidence was sufficient to bar appellants from carrying on fundraising activities until they complied with the law. Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 17206, 17536, authorized penalties of up to $ 2,500 for each separate act in violation of false advertising and unfair competition laws.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment enjoining appellants from commercial fundraising until they complied with law and accounted for prior fundraising activities because recordkeeping deficiencies and overall pattern of misrepresentation supported detailed injunctive relief. Moreover, public interest justified imposition of constructive charitable trust. Costs were awarded because respondent's action corrected breach of trust.

 

342 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Yamaha Corp. of Am. v. State Bd. of Equalization


No. B095911.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


73 Cal. App. 4th 338; 86 Cal. Rptr. 2d 362; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 640; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5382; 99 Daily Journal DAR 6865



June 29, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied July 15, 1999. Review Denied October 20, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 7254.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County, following remand by the Supreme Court. Super. Ct. No. BC079444. Daniel A. Curry, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. Costs on appeal are awarded to the Board.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the State Board of Equalization, appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), following remand by the Supreme Court, in favor of respondent corporation for a refund of use tax and interest on musical instruments and promotional brochures stored in California and shipped as gifts to out-of-state donees.


OVERVIEW: Respondent purchased musical instruments outside of California under resale certificates and stored them in a California warehouse. Some of the instruments were given as promotional gifts. The donated instruments were delivered via common carrier. Respondent contested the imposition of tax on the gifts to out-of-state recipients by the State Board of Equalization (SBE) and filed suit for refund on the ground that the property was exempt under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code §6009.1, which exempted property stored in California for the purposes of transporting it out of state. The trial court ruled in favor of respondent. The court of appeal reversed, relying primarily on an annotation prepared by sales tax counsel for the SBE. The supreme court reversed the court of appeal and remanded the case for reconsideration because the court of appeal had given improper weight to the sales tax counsel's opinion. On remand, using its independent interpretation of §6009.1, and the principles of gift law, the court still held respondent liable for use tax because the gifts were complete when respondent delivered the property to common carriers in California.


OUTCOME: The superior court's judgment refunding use tax and interest to respondent was reversed because the taxation of the gifts was properly imposed when such gifts were complete when respondent delivered the property to common carriers in California, and the taxation did not violate the "dormant" commerce clause.

 

343 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Centers


No. E022259.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


73 Cal. App. 4th 84; 86 Cal. Rptr. 2d 151; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 620; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5167; 99 Daily Journal DAR 6621



June 28, 1999, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of part IV.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied October 6, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 7134.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from the Superior Court of Riverside County. Super. Ct. No. HEF-970038. Dennis A. McConaghy, Judge. *


* Judge of the former Municipal Court for the Western Riverside Judicial District, assigned by the Chief Justice pursuant to art. VI, § 6 of the California Constitution.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, who was convicted of kidnapping for ransom and other offenses by the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), appealed the jury's finding pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 209(a) that he had intentionally confined the victim in a manner that either caused the victim to suffer bodily harm or exposed the victim to a substantial likelihood of death. Defendant also appealed his sentences for his burglary and kidnapping convictions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of kidnapping and other offenses. He appealed the jury's finding that he intentionally confined the victim in a manner that either caused him to suffer bodily harm or exposed him to a substantial likelihood of death. Defendant also appealed the trial court's imposition of separate and unstayed sentences for both his burglary and kidnapping convictions. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that there was sufficient evidence for the jury to have found that defendant intentionally confined the victim in a manner that exposed the victim to a substantial likelihood of death so as to support a sentence of life without the possibility of parole under Cal. Penal Code. § 209(a). With respect to defendant's separate sentences, the court held that his burglary was a violent crime for purposes of the "multiple victim" exception to Cal. Penal Code § 654, which prohibited multiple punishment for a single act, because defendant had used a firearm during the burglary. The court further held that the trial court could determine the identify of the victim of a violent crime for such purposes even though no victim was named in the information or by the jury.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the jury's finding and held that there was sufficient evidence that defendant had intentionally confined his kidnapping victim in a manner that either caused the victim to suffer bodily harm or exposed the victim to a substantial likelihood of death. The court also affirmed the trial court's imposition of separate and unstayed sentences for defendant's burglary and kidnapping convictions.

 

344 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Martinez


No. B116969.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


73 Cal. App. 4th 265; 86 Cal. Rptr. 2d 346; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 621; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5164; 99 Daily Journal DAR 6669



June 28, 1999, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for partial publication. The portions to be published are the first four paragraphs of the opinion, part 8 of the Discussion and the Disposition.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified and Certified for Partial Publication July 1, 1999.
Review Denied October 20, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 7261.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALS from judgments of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. TA045346. George H. Wu, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment as to defendant Martinez is affirmed. The judgment as to defendant Salazar is modified to impose a $ 200 parole revocation fine, to remain suspended unless and until Salazar violates parole. The judgment as to Salazar is affirmed as modified. The abstracts of judgment for both defendants shall be modified in accordance with this opinion. The clerk of the superior court is ordered to prepare corrected abstracts of judgment that will be forwarded to the Department of Corrections.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed from judgments of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), entered following convictions for, inter alia, special circumstance first degree murder and for second degree murder, respectively. The People appealed, contending the trial court erred by failing to impose a parole revocation fine and by failing to impose state and county penalty assessments in connection with an award of direct victim restitution.


OVERVIEW: Upon convictions for, inter alia, special circumstance first degree murder and for second degree murder, respectively, defendants appealed. The People also appealed, contending the trial court erred in not imposing a parole revocation fine and for not imposing state and county penalty assessments in connection with the imposition of direct victim restitution. On review, the court affirmed judgments with modification that a $ 200 parole revocation fine, to remain suspended unless and until defendant violates parole, be imposed. But because the restitution statute clearly stated that the court did not collect direct victim restitution, the trial court acted properly in not imposing state and county penalty assessments against defendant in connection with the award of direct restitution pursuant to statute.


OUTCOME: Judgments as to defendants' convictions affirmed, and judgment was affirmed and modified to impose a $ 200 parole revocation fine, to remain suspended unless and until defendant violates parole. The restitution statute made clear that direct victim restitution was not collected by the court, and, thus, the trial court did not err by failing to impose state and county penalty assessments in connection with the direct victim restitution order.

 

345 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Bowen v. Workers' Comp. Appeals Bd.


No. B124899.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


73 Cal. App. 4th 15; 86 Cal. Rptr. 2d 95; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 615; 64 Cal. Comp. Cases 745; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5113; 99 Daily Journal DAR 6521



June 24, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Proceeding to review a decision of the Workers' Compensation Appeals Board. W.C.A.B.No. VNO 0356279.



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Workers' Compensation Appeals Board is annulled. The matter is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a professional baseball player, sought review of the decision of respondent, the Workers' Compensation Appeals Board, which concluded that it was without jurisdiction to proceed with petitioner's claim for benefits under the California's Workers' Compensation Act for an injury petitioner suffered while playing baseball in Florida.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, a California resident who was injured in Florida while playing baseball, sought benefits under the California Workers' Compensation Act. Respondent workers' compensation appeals board decided it did not have jurisdiction to proceed with the claim. Respondent concluded that the contract of hire was not made in California because it was subject to the approval of the baseball commissioner in New York. The court annulled the decision and concluded that respondent erred in finding that it lacked jurisdiction. The court determined that California's workers' compensation law afforded compensation to employees hired in California and injured out of state under Cal. Lab. Code § 3600.5 and Cal. Lab. Code § 5305, and that respondent failed to heed the mandate of Cal. Lab. Code § 3202 that those sections be construed liberally. The court concluded that the contract was formed in California when petitioner accepted the team's offer and signed the contract and that the commissioner's approval was a condition subsequent to employment. The court further concluded that even if the contract was not formed until approved by the commissioner, petitioner was still entitled to benefits.


OUTCOME: The court annulled respondent's decision. The court concluded that respondent had jurisdiction to proceed with petitioner baseball player's claim for workers' compensation benefits, that the contract of hire was formed in California, and that respondent failed to construe California's workers' compensation law liberally with the purpose of extending benefits for the protection of persons injured in the course of their employment.

People v. Hedge


No. D031243.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


72 Cal. App. 4th 1466; 86 Cal. Rptr. 2d 52; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 606; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5040; 99 Daily Journal DAR 6431



June 23, 1999, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. 1 


1 Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts II and III.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County. Super. Ct. No. CR108176. Terry J. Knoepp, Judge, * and Michael D. Wellington, Judge. * 


* Retired judge of the San Diego Superior Court, assigned by the Chief Justice pursuant to article VI, section 6 of the California Constitution.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which ordered his commitment to the state mental health facility following a jury finding that he was a sexually violent predator based on the Sexually Violent Predators Act, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6600 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed from a judgment ordering his commitment to the state mental health facility following a jury finding that he was a sexually violent predator (SVP) based on the Sexually Violent Predators Act (Act), Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6600 et seq. He contended that the trial court lacked jurisdiction to proceed on a second petition filed to determine whether he was an SVP because his first petition was still pending review and since he was not serving a valid parole revocation term when current proceedings were instituted against him. Appellate court affirmed judgment, concluding that the trial court had jurisdiction to proceed on his second filed petition due to the nature of commitment proceedings that focused on his current mental condition and dangerousness as a potential SVP, thus permitting initiation of proceedings whenever the potential SVP was scheduled to be released from state custody.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed judgment ordering defendant's commitment to mental health facility as a sexually violent predator (SVP) since the trial court had jurisdiction to proceed on his second filed petition due to proceedings focusing on his current mental condition and dangerousness as potential SVP.

People v. Pierce


F027557


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


72 Cal. App. 4th 1448; 86 Cal. Rptr. 2d 30; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 598; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4901; 99 Daily Journal DAR 6237



June 21, 1999, Filed


NOTICE:   [***1] CERTIFIED FOR PARTIAL PUBLICATION - Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts I.-II., III.B., and IV.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Granted October 6, 1999 (S081047), Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 7028.
Review Dismissed December 15, 1999 and cause remanded to the Ct. of App., 5th Dist. ( rule 29.4(c), Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 8660.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Stanislaus County. Super. Ct. No. 33065. William A. Mayhew, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Stanislaus County (California) that convicted him of oral copulation by force or fear, sodomy by force or fear, and false imprisonment.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted on one count of oral copulation by force or fear, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288a, subd. (c); one count of sodomy by force or fear, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 286, subd. (c); and one count of false imprisonment, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 236. He was sentenced to 12 years 8 months in prison. The court reversed that conviction, finding appellant had been denied a fair trial because a juror was mentally incompetent. In the second trial, appellant was convicted and sentenced to 16 years in prison. Appellant challenged both the convictions and the sentence on numerous grounds. After review, the court concluded that Cal. Evid. Code § 1108, which permitted the admission of evidence of another sexual offense committed by appellant, did not violate the due process and equal protection clauses of the federal Constitution. Section 1108 also did not violate the constitutional violation against ex post facto laws and the principle of double jeopardy. The trial court's judgment was affirmed, but the case was remanded because the trial court erred in imposing a greater sentence than that imposed after the first trial.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment convicting appellant of oral copulation, sodomy, and false imprisonment was affirmed because evidence statute did not violate the due process and equal protection clauses of the Constitution, and did not violate ex post facto clause and the principle of double jeopardy. The case was remanded for sentencing because the trial court erred in imposing a greater sentence than was imposed after the first trial.

 

348 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



In re Anthony J.


No. A080758.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


72 Cal. App. 4th 1326; 85 Cal. Rptr. 2d 783; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 587; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4728; 99 Daily Journal DAR 6021



June 15, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied September 29, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 6888.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Super. Ct. No. 122098. Charlotte Walter Woolard, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The dispositional order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed that part of an order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) which convicted and sentenced defendant for lynching.


OVERVIEW: Officers succeeded in handcuffing defendant. After being handcuffed, defendant continued his attempts to break away and called for assistance from a gathering crowd. The crowd finally charged the officers and pulled defendant away. Defendant was charged with resisting arrest, lynching, aggravated assault on an officer, assault, and petty theft. The juvenile court upheld the charges and convicted and sentenced defendant for the same. On appeal, defendant challenged only the finding on the lynching charge, contending that the lynching statutes were enacted to protect victims of lynching from harm, and therefore could not be violated by a person who was the subject of the lynching. The court affirmed, holding that under California law, lynching included any participation in riotous conduct aimed at freeing a person from the custody of a peace officer. Because defendant took part in a riot leading to his escape from custody, he could indeed be convicted of his own lynching.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's lynching conviction because under California law, lynching included defendant's participation in riotous conduct which was aimed at freeing himself from the custody of the peace officer.

 

349 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Barlow v. Davis


No. A084574.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


72 Cal. App. 4th 1258; 85 Cal. Rptr. 2d 752; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 575; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4663; 99 Daily Journal DAR 5933



June 11, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified July 12, 1999. Review Denied September 15, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 6272.


PRIOR HISTORY: Trial Court: Alameda County Superior Court. Super. Ct. No. 796308-9. Trial Judge: Honorable Henry E. Needham, Jr.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs appealed from the Alameda County Superior Court (California), which denied their request for a writ of mandate and injunctive and declaratory relief against defendant governor because it found that Cal. Pub. Cont. Code § 10115.5 requiring reporting of the number of minority contracts awards was not severable from the remainder of the affirmative action statute that had been declared unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs filed suit seeking to enforce Cal. Pub. Cont. Code § 10115.5 that required state agencies to report on the number of minority contracts awarded. Defendant governor had issued an executive order directing agencies not to comply with the statute after the courts struck down the affirmative action program of which Cal. Pub. Cont. Code § 10115.5 was a part. The trial court found that the reporting statute was not severable from the unconstitutional provisions and denied relief. Plaintiffs appealed and the court affirmed. The statute contained a savings clause, Cal. Pub. Cont. Code § 10115.8, but the court found that the reporting provision was not complete in itself and would not have been adopted without the invalidated portion. The court examined the statute on three bases, looking at whether it was grammatically, mechanically, and volitionally separable. The reporting was tied to the enforcement of the statute and was not intended simply as a reporting law to gather information. The court said that when the main purpose of a statute was defeated by the unconstitutionality of a part, the whole act was invalid.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of relief to plaintiffs seeking to enforce the reporting element of an unconstitutional affirmative action program. The provision of the statute that required reporting of minority contracts awarded by state agencies was not complete in itself and would not have been adopted independent of the invalidated affirmative action provisions of the statute.
