Broden v. Marin Humane Society


No. A079356.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


70 Cal. App. 4th 1212; 83 Cal. Rptr. 2d 235; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 242; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2208



March 25, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied April 20, 1999.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Marin County. Super. Ct. No. 167449. Gary W. Thomas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified as follows: (1) paragraphs "1)" and "3)" showing that plaintiff is to recover $ 2,540 "as damages for the loss of his property" and a "net total" of $ 1,004, respectively, are deleted; and, (2) paragraph "2)" is amended to show that defendant is entitled to recover boarding fees in the amount of $ 1,536, impound fees in the amount of $ 420, and veterinary expenses in the amount of $ 912.50, for a total of $ 2,868.50. As so modified the judgment is affirmed. The parties shall bear their respective costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff and defendant both appealed an order from the Superior Court of Marin County (California) offsetting plaintiff damages for the value of lost property from boarding fees due defendant pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 597.1. Defendant also appealed the refusal to award it veterinary expenses.


OVERVIEW: The court modified a judgment reversing plaintiff pet store owner's award for damages for lost property, and awarding defendant humane society its boarding and veterinary expenses. The court found plaintiff waived the issue of whether his due process rights were violated because the issue was not preserved for appeal. The court agreed that under Cal. Penal Code § 597.1, the police officer's warrantless entry into plaintiff's exotic pet store was proper to protect the health of the animals. The officer could smell dead animals from outside the store, could not see inside or find plaintiff. The court held that plaintiff was properly denied an evidentiary hearing because he had received relief from default twice, over six weeks had passed since damages were decided, and it would not have affected the result. The court found plaintiff was not entitled to damages because plaintiff failed to pay the lien charges, and to give assurances that he would provide the animals with necessary care, and thus, the animals were abandoned under § 597.1. The court ruled defendant was entitled to recover its costs and fees because defendant's lien made plaintiff personally liable as a matter of law.


OUTCOME: Plaintiff's award for his seized animals was reversed because he no longer owned them under the law. Because plaintiff failed to pay the lien charges, and to give assurances that he would provide the animals with necessary care, the animals were deemed abandoned. The court also awarded defendant boarding and veterinary fees because plaintiff was personally liable after a lien had been issued.

 

377 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Aubrey


No. A081058.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


70 Cal. App. 4th 1088; 83 Cal. Rptr. 2d 209; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 224; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 2089



March 22, 1999, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts II.D. and II.E.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Humboldt County. Super. Ct. No. CR971130S. Dale A. Reinholtsen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The case is remanded to the trial court to determine the local conduct credits in accordance with the views expressed in the unpublished portion of our opinion, to modify the abstract of judgment to include such credits, and to forward a certified copy of the amended abstract to the Department of Corrections. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his conviction for carrying a concealed "dirk or dagger," pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 12020, contending that his trial counsel was ineffective, that the instructions were erroneous, and that presentence conduct credits should have been awarded.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of carrying concealed knives. At trial his attorney admitted he was carrying a "dirk or dagger," but denied that appellant either intended to conceal it or use it. Although appellant was entitled to have the jury determine his mens rea, the court found the admission that appellant was carrying a knife was a trial tactic by counsel. The record contained substantial evidence that the defense was based on: (1) a thorough evaluation of the applicable statute and relevant cases; (2) an adequate investigation; and (3) a reasonable professional judgment that it would be too risky to open the door to evidence of appellant's prior conviction by explicitly arguing and requesting instruction on a theory of "innocent use." Defense counsel made a valid choice of tactics, his representation was not deficient in any material way, and appellant's claim for ineffective assistance of counsel failed as a matter of law. Any error in the trial court's failure to instruct was plainly harmless, based on appellant's concession that he was carrying a knife.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision as it found the failure to instruct on the mens rea element of the definition of "dirk or dagger" was harmless error as he had admitted he was carrying a knife under the definition in the statute.

 

378 of 1449 DOCUMENTS




Notrica v. State Comp. Ins. Fund


No. B097529.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


70 Cal. App. 4th 911; 83 Cal. Rptr. 2d 89; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 210; 64 Cal. Comp. Cases 378; 99 Daily Journal DAR 2503



March 17, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. (Super. Ct. No. BC016464. James E. Satt, Judge. *


* Judge of the San Diego Superior Court, assigned by the Chief Justice pursuant to article VI, section 6 of the California Constitution.



DISPOSITION: The judgment with regard to punitive damages is reversed and the matter is remanded for a new trial only on the issue of punitive damages unless, within 30 days from remand, Notrica shall file with the clerk of the court and serve upon SCIF a written consent to a reduction of the punitive damages award to the sum of $ 5 million, in which event the judgment shall be modified to reflect an award of punitive damages in favor of Notrica in that amount and be affirmed in its entirety. Costs on appeal are awarded to Notrica.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California), which held in favor of plaintiff in an action in tort alleging tortious breach of the implied covenant of good faith and fair dealing, and unfair, unlawful, or fraudulent business practices in violation of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code, § 17200.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff insured brought an action against defendant, his workers' compensation insurer, to recover for unfair business practices, based on allegations relating to defendant's case reserve and claims handling policies. Defendant appealed a judgment in favor of plaintiff, awarding damages and issuing an injunction against defendant, arguing an insured employer was prohibited from recovering tort damages where the only damage claimed was the impact on future premiums, its reserving practices could not support a bad faith judgment, and the punitive damages award must be reversed. The court ruled that an insurer could be liable if it processed claims and set reserves without good faith regard for their impact on the insured's premiums and potential dividends. Further, the court held that there was substantial evidence which supported the finding of bad faith in defendant's implementation of Cal. Ins. Code § 923.5. The court affirmed the judgment, and reversed the punitive damage award, concluding that the goal of punitive damages was to award an amount that was sufficient to deter the conduct but was not excessive and that the amount awarded by the trial jury was excessive.


OUTCOME: Judgment in favor of plaintiff was affirmed, and the judgment with regard to punitive damages was reversed and the matter was remanded for a new trial only on the issue of punitive damages, where defendant's actions constituted substantial evidence in support of a finding of bad faith, defendant was treated as a private enterprise, and the amount of punitive damages awarded was excessive.


 

379 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Moreno v. Draper


No. F028500.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


70 Cal. App. 4th 886; 83 Cal. Rptr. 2d 82; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 209; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1902; 99 Daily Journal DAR 2414



March 15, 1999, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts I. and III.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from an order of the Superior Court of Stanislaus County. Super. Ct. No. 114684. Lynne R. Meredith, Commissioner.



DISPOSITION: This matter is remanded for the trial court to redetermine Draper's child support in conformity with the guideline amounts. Each party shall pay its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother challenged an order from the Superior Court of Stanislaus County (California), which deviated from a calculated child support amount in determining appellee father's child support payments.


OVERVIEW: Appellee filed for an income and expense declaration. At the time, his son received public assistance. The lower court calculated an amount based upon appellee's gross income. After deciding on the calculations, the lower court chose to deviate from the presumed amount based on the previous support order, additional children, and appellee's current expenses. Appellant claimed that the trial court erred in granting appellee a deduction pursuant to Cal. Fam. Code § 4071.5. The appellate court analyzed whether the statute was constitutional. It applied a rational basis standard because the statute identified non-suspect classes of parents, children, and siblings. The statute implicated the fundamental right to raise one's own children in his or own home. The court concluded that the statute's requirements that a noncustodial parent take full responsibility for his or her calculated guideline share of support in raising a noncustodial child was rationally related to the allocation of limited public funds. Therefore, the statute was constitutional. On remand, the trial court was directed to redetermine appellee's child support payments in conformity with guideline amounts.


OUTCOME: The trial court's order that awarded child support after it deviated from guideline amounts was reversed. As a nonconstodial parent with a child on public assistance, appellee could not obtain a deduction under state law. The case was remanded to recalculate the child support.

 

380 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



City v. Garnett


No. A081566.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


70 Cal. App. 4th 845; 82 Cal. Rptr. 2d 924; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 203; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1851; 99 Daily Journal DAR 2371



March 12, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Super. Ct. No. DA34190. Marjorie A. Slabach, Commissioner.



DISPOSITION: The order under review is reversed. The trial court shall recompute Garnett's child support obligation in accordance with the provisions of section 4071.5. The parties shall bear their own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff government appealed the order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) that refused to apply Cal. Fam. Code §4071.5, which precluded a hardship deduction, when it calculated the child support owed by respondent father for his daughter because the court found that § 4071.5 unconstitutionally discriminated against low income parents.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff government filed a motion to modify the child support that defendant father owed for his daughter. His daughter lived with her maternal grandmother and the child received welfare. Defendant had full custody of his son, who lived with him. The trial court refused to apply Cal. Fam. Code § 4071.5, which precluded a parent from receiving a hardship deduction on child support if welfare payments were made on the child's behalf. Plaintiff sought review. On appeal, defendant argued that his son's equal protection rights were violated by § 4071.5 because without the hardship deduction, defendant had less disposable income to spend on his son, a situation not faced by children whose nonresident siblings do not receive welfare. The appellate court found that the rational basis test, instead of the strict scrutiny test, applied. The court reversed the order and determined that disallowing parents' reduction in their support obligations for nonresident children who receive public assistance was related to the legitimate state purposes of relieving the public burden of supporting the impoverished, and maximizing the benefit payments available for all needy children.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed an order that refused to apply a statutory preclusion of hardship deduction to defendant father. The court held that the statute was constitutional under equal protection analysis. Thus, the statute applied because questions respecting its wisdom were not within the province of courts.

People v. Tillman


A079134, A083670


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


70 Cal. App. 4th 710; 83 Cal. Rptr. 2d 56; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 193; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1753



March 8, 1999, Filed


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Granted April 7, 1999.
Petition for Writ of Habeas Corpus Denied July 21, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 675. (73 Cal. App. 4th 771).


PRIOR HISTORY: San Mateo County Superior Court No. SC39904A. Honorable Carl Holm.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. The petition for writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his conviction for failing to register as a sex offender in the San Mateo County Superior Court (California) and filed a related petition for writ of habeas corpus, claiming he received ineffective assistance of counsel.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of failing to register as a sex offender. On review, appellant claimed he received ineffective assistance in that his attorney failed to defend against the charge on the ground that appellant had not been constitutionally convicted of the prior offense. The court found appellant could not demonstrate a reasonable probability that a collateral challenge to the prior conviction would have been successful. Therefore, his counsel was not ineffective by failing to pursue that defense. Appellant also petitioned for a writ of habeas corpus, urging that the use of his prior rape conviction both as an element of the substantive registration offense and as a sentence enhancement was improper. The court determined the lower court properly used defendant's prior rape conviction as a "strike" that augmented defendant's sentence under the "Three Strikes" law, Cal. Penal Code §1170.12(d)(2). The court determined that §1170.12(d)(2) was intended to apply to all recidivists coming within its terms, and thus its specific language overruled the general rule that a single fact could not be the basis both of an element of the charged offense and of a sentence enhancement.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction and sentence and denied the habeas corpus petition because appellant's prior rape conviction was properly used as both an element of failure to register violation and as a strike that enhanced his sentence, and his counsel was not ineffective for failing to defend on the basis that the prior conviction was unconstitutional.

People v. Barrera


No. B120948.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO

SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied March 23, 1999. Review Denied June 3, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 3807.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. LA028217. Michael M. Duffey, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified to reflect a $ 200 restitution fine pursuant to Penal Code section 1202.4, subdivision (b) and a $ 200 fine pursuant to Penal Code section 1202.45, the latter fine to be suspended unless appellant's parole is revoked. The abstract of judgment is to be amended to reflect these fines. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) for check forgery in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 470(a), with findings of two prior serious felony convictions within the meaning of the Three Strikes law, Cal. Penal Code §§ 1170.12(a)-(d), 667(b)-(i).

The court also held that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in failing to strike a prior conviction, given defendant's criminal history, and held that defendant's sentence was not cruel and unusual punishment.

383 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Jahr v. Casebeer


No. C028749.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


70 Cal. App. 4th 1250; 83 Cal. Rptr. 2d 172; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 251



February 25, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Publication Status of this Document has been Changed by the Court from Unpublished to Published March 26, 1999.
As Modified March 26, 1999. Review Denied May 12, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 3076.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Shasta County. Super. Ct. No. 133713. Carroll A. Ragland, Commissioner.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, county voters and members of a county deputy sheriff's association, sought review of the judgment from the Superior Court of Shasta County (California). The trial court held that a proposed initiative that allowed voters to set county supervisors' salaries by initiative was unconstitutional under Cal. Const. art. XI, § 1(b).


OVERVIEW: Defendants, members of a county deputy sheriff's association, submitted an initiative to the county clerk that would have permitted local voters to set county supervisors' salaries by initiative. Plaintiff, county counsel, sought a declaration that the proposed initiative was unconstitutional. The trial court granted the relief plaintiff had requested, holding that the initiative was unconstitutional pursuant to Cal. Const. art. XI, § 1(b). The court affirmed. The plain language of Cal. Const. art. XI, § 1(b) authorized voters to challenge county supervisors' salaries by referendum, not by initiative. Further, the specified "governing body" that had the constitutional power to prescribe by ordinance the compensation of its members could only refer to a local legislative body, not to voters. The court rejected defendants' argument that the right to referendum implied the right to initiative. Referendum and initiative had different purposes: initiative allowed voters to propose new legislation, while referendum permitted voters to reject legislation that had already been adopted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment that an initiative proposed by defendants, members of a county deputy sheriff's association, violated the state constitution. The state constitution plainly authorized voters to challenge county supervisors' salaries only by referendum, not by initiative, and the right to referendum did not imply the corollary right to initiative.

 

384 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Waffer Internat. Corp. v. Khorsandi


No. B114443.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


69 Cal. App. 4th 1261; 82 Cal. Rptr. 2d 241; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 112; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1225; 99 Daily Journal DAR 1471



February 16, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. BC103229. Malcolm H. Mackey, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The summary judgment is reversed. The matter is remanded for further proceedings not inconsistent with this opinion. Appellant to recover costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporation appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which entered summary judgment for defendants on the grounds that plaintiff's writ of attachment, in a related but different case against a corporate defendant, barred plaintiff from pursuing tort remedies in this case against two individual defendants.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff obtained and levied on a writ of attachment against defendant in a parallel action brought by plaintiff. The trial court entered summary judgment for defendants on grounds that plaintiff's election of remedies sacrificed its tort claims against individual defendants in the action. The court reversed the lower court's grant of summary judgment, finding that the election of remedies doctrine did not apply. The court held that plaintiff's attachment was obtained against a different party on a different claim, which did not involve those individual defendants and was obtained in a different action. Plaintiff sued individual defendants for conversion and fraud, not breach of the settlement agreement. The claims against individual defendants did not arise out of the same operative facts (the settlement agreement and its breach) but instead out of different operative facts (the false promises, the presentation of false information to the international shipper, the hiding of assets, etc.). Further, the court held that individual defendants did not show they were "substantially prejudiced" by enforcement of plaintiff's statutory rights.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's summary judgment in favor of defendants, on grounds that plaintiff's act of obtaining an attachment against a corporate defendant in a parallel action did not bar it pursuing its tort claims against individual defendants. The court held plaintiff's claim against defendant in a parallel action arose out of operative facts different from plaintiff's claim against individual defendants in this action.

Augustine v. Superior Court


No. E022778.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


71 Cal. App. 4th 990; 84 Cal. Rptr. 2d 487; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 373; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3139; 99 Daily Journal DAR 4047



February 5, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Publication Status of this Document has been changed by the Supreme Court from Unpublished to Published April 28, 1999.
As Modified on Denial of Rehearing March 5, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 380. Review Denied April 28, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 2925.


PRIOR HISTORY: ORIGINAL PROCEEDINGS; petition for writ of mandate. Superior Court of Riverside. Super. Ct. No. CR42784. Ronald L. Taylor, Judge. *


* Judge of the Municipal Court for the Western Riverside Judicial District, assigned by the Chief Justice pursuant to art. VI, § 6 of the California Constitution.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ, having served its purpose, is discharged. The petition for writ of mandate is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, felony convicts, sought a writ of mandate that the Superior Court of Riverside (California) be required to retain petitioners' trial exhibits in its own custody and not destroy them in case petitioners decided to collaterally attack their convictions in the future.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners were persons who had been convicted of felony criminal offenses. Petitioners, through the public defender, objected to the destruction of their exhibits by the trial court pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §1417.1. Petitioners sought a writ of mandate that the trial court be forced to retain in its own custody petitioners' exhibits in case petitioners elected to collaterally attack their convictions in the future. The court denied petitioners' request and held that the trial court's destruction of petitioners' exhibits did not deny them due process. The court went on to hold that the trial court was not obligated to hold an evidentiary hearing on their requests because petitioners failed to make a specific, detailed showing concerning the potential merit of a collateral attack and the significance of particular exhibits to the defense. Finally, the court recognized that the trial court does not have a never-ending duty to preserve the integrity of every exhibit.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioners' request that the trial court retain their trial exhibits in its own custody and held that the trial court was not obligated to preserve the integrity of every exhibit, nor was it obligated to hold an evidentiary hearing on petitioners' requests because petitioners failed to make a detailed showing concerning the potential merit of a collateral attack and the significance of particular exhibits to the defense.

East Bay Asian Local Dvelopment Corp. v. State of California


C024192


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


69 Cal. App. 4th 1033; 81 Cal. Rptr. 2d 908; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 93; 99 Daily Journal DAR 1235; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 1001



February 4, 1999, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted May 26, 1999 (S077396), Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 3349.
Reprinted without Change in the Review Granted Opinions Cumulative Supplement (July 1999) to permit tracking pending review and disposition by the Supreme Ct.
Reprinted without change in the January 2000 Review Granted Opinions Pamphlet to permit tracking pending review and disposition by the Supreme Ct.
Supreme Court of California Opinion of December 21, 2000, Reported at: 24 Cal. 4th 693.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from the judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County. Super. Ct. No. 95AS02560. Joe S. Gray, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed a decision of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) that entered judgment for plaintiffs in an action brought seeking a declaration that Cal. Gov't. Code § 25373(d)(e) (1994) was unconstitutional. After the parties filed cross-motions for summary judgment, the lower court granted plaintiffs' motion and denied that of the state, concluding that § 25373(d)(e) violated state and federal establishment clauses.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs were the City and County of San Francisco and various private, nonprofit organizations which challenged the constitutionality of certain state landmark preservation exemptions given to non-profit religiously affiliated organizations. The lower court entered summary judgment for the plaintiffs holding that Cal. Gov't Code § 25373(d)(e) (1994), violated the state and federal establishment clauses and was an unconstitutional delegation of governmental powers to private entities. On review, the court reversed, holding that the statute was not unconstitutional because the exemptions from historic preservation laws was for the express purpose of ensuring the protection of religious freedom guaranteed by the state and federal constitutions, not to advance religion. The court further found that the statute was not an impermissible delegation of power to private entities, holding that protection of religious freedom was more than a local concern and that the religious organizations had not been given authority to designate or regulate properties to be preserved as landmarks.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the lower court and held the statute in question was constitutional. The statute did not constitute either an endorsement or infringement of religion, but merely eliminated a burden imposed by the government. The statute was also not an unlawful delegation of legislative powers as religious organizations had not been given any authority to designate or regulate properties to be preserved as landmarks.

Griset v. Fair Political Practices Com.


G018853


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


69 Cal. App. 4th 818; 82 Cal. Rptr. 2d 25; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 71; 99 Daily Journal DAR 1063; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 879



January 29, 1999, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted May 12, 1999 (S077219), Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 2981.
Reprinted without Change in the Review Granted Opinions Cumulative Supplement (July 1999) to permit tracking pending review and disposition by the Supreme Ct.
Reprinted without change in the January 2000 Review Granted Opinions Pamphlet to permit tracking pending review and disposition by the Supreme Ct.
Reprinted without change in the January 2001 Review Granted Opinions Pamphlet to permit tracking pending review and disposition by the Supreme Ct.
Supreme Court of California Opinion of May 24, 2001, Reported at: 25 Cal. 4th 688.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County. Super. Ct. No. 638654. Floyd H. Schenk, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed with directions in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which held that Cal. Gov't Code § 84305, which prohibited distribution of anonymous campaign literature, was constitutional and could not be challenged under the law of the case doctrine.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs distributed anonymous campaign literature in violation of Cal. Gov't Code § 84305, and the constitutionally of the statute was upheld by the California Supreme Court, which remanded back to the trial court for adjudication. After remand, the U.S. Supreme Court found that a similar Ohio statute was unconstitutional under U.S. Const. amend. I. Plaintiffs then at the trial level, again attacked the constitutionality of the state statute, and the trial court refused to consider their arguments, holding the law of the case doctrine precluded their arguments. Plaintiffs appealed the trial court's ruling, and the court reversed. The court held that the law of the case doctrine was a procedural rule, that will not be adhered to where the application would result in an unjust decision. The court stated that the U.S. Supreme Court's decision invalidating a similar state law had a direct effect upon the present litigation. The court concluded that based upon the U.S. Supreme Court's interpretation of the U.S. Const. amend I, the statute was unconstitutional. The court granted plaintiff's challenge to the law, and remanded for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court and held that law of the case doctrine did not apply due to an intervening U.S. Supreme Court case, and, therefore, plaintiff's challenge to the constitutionality of the state statute was correct, as the statute violated the First Amendment. The court remanded for further proceedings in accordance with its decision.

Nat'l Union Fire Ins. Co. v. Nationwide Ins. Co.


No. G018963.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


69 Cal. App. 4th 709; 82 Cal. Rptr. 2d 16; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 67; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 830; 64 Cal. Comp. Cases 207; 99 Daily Journal DAR 1033



January 28, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County. Super. Ct. No. 718725. James J. Alfano, Sr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Respondents are entitled to their costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant insurer challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which held that appellant was entitled to defense costs from appellee insurer of subcontractor whose employee was injured, but no indemnity because appellant's insured, the general contractor, was solely negligent in a slip and fall action.


OVERVIEW: Subcontractor's employee slipped and fell on standing water during an inspection. The indemnity agreement held subcontractor liable for everything but the general contractor's sole negligence. Appellant, general contractor's insurer defended the underlying suit and filed a declaratory action against appellee, subcontractor's insurer. Contractor also filed for indemnity and declaratory relief. Cross-claims went to arbitration. Appellant was awarded defense costs, but no indemnity because its insured, the general contractor, was solely negligent. On appeal, the court affirmed, having jurisdiction because the arbitration was changed to a reference nunc pro tunc, the gist of the proceedings were a reference, it reflected the parties intent, and the proper legal formalities were preserved. The court held that sufficient evidence supported the findings. The contractor was in sole control of the area where the flooding was and could have prevented or ameliorated the hazards. The court ruled that the general contractor was not entitled to indemnity as an additional insured because the endorsement was limited to general supervision, but was fully protected under its own policy.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the findings that appellant, the general contractor's insurer, was entitled to defense costs, but not indemnification because the general contractor was solely negligent, and subcontractor's insurance policy, issued by appellee insurer, as well as the parties' indemnification agreement, covered everything but contractor's sole negligence.

Estevanovich v. City of Riverside


E018016


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


69 Cal. App. 4th 544; 81 Cal. Rptr. 2d 684; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 56; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 713; 99 Daily Journal DAR 845



January 25, 1999, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified February 9, 1999. Review Granted April 21, 1999 (S077146), Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 2409.
Reprinted without Change in the Review Granted Opinions Cumulative Supplement (July 1999) to permit tracking pending review and disposition by the Supreme Ct.
Reprinted without Change in the January 2000 Review Granted Opinions Pamphlet to permit tracking pending review and disposition by the Supreme Ct.
January 13, 2000 Review Transferred to the Court of Appeal, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two, with directions to vacate its decision and to reconsider the cause in light of Warden v. State Bar (1999) 21 Cal. 4th 628, Reported at: 2000 Cal. LEXIS 31.
Opinion filed July 17, 2000 not for publication.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from the Superior Court of Riverside County. Super. Ct. No. 230892. Stephen D. Cunnison, Judge and Dennis McConaghy *


* Judge of the Municipal Court, assigned by the Chief Justice pursuant to art. VI, § 6 of the California Constitution.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which ruled that a city ordinance that required poolrooms to close between 2:00 a.m. and 6:00 a.m. was unconstitutional because the classification of poolrooms bore no rational relationship to the purpose of the ordinance.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city had an old ordinance that required poolrooms to have licenses. The ordinance required the city council to investigate applicants for licenses. When four poolrooms opened and applied for licenses, the city council placed a moratorium on permits because they did not want to do the investigations. A police officer was assigned to draft a new ordinance that transferred the duty of investigating applicants to the police department. The officer inserted a mandatory four-hour closure period for poolrooms between 2:00 a.m. and 6:00 a.m. The ordinance was passed. Appellee, who owned a poolroom open 24 hours, filed a lawsuit challenging the ordinance. Applying the rational basis test, the trial court found the ordinance unconstitutional under the equal protection clauses of both the federal and state constitutions. On appeal, the court found that the purpose of the ordinance was to transfer investigatory power to the police department and that regulation of hours was unrelated to this purpose. Also, the court determined that singling poolrooms out from other places of amusement constituted a classification scheme that was arbitrary, discriminatory, and unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the ruling that a city ordinance that required poolrooms to close between 2:00 a.m. and 6:00 a.m. was unconstitutional. Analyzing the ordinance under the rational basis test, the court concluded that the classification scheme, which treated poolrooms differently from other places of amusement, was arbitrary in light of appellant city's stated purpose of reducing crime.

 

390 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Reo Broad. Consultants v. Martin


No. B118068.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


69 Cal. App. 4th 489; 81 Cal. Rptr. 2d 639; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 51; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 671; 99 Daily Journal DAR 757



January 22, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from an order of dismissal of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. BC149320. Peter D. Lichtman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed. Defendant shall recover her costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff partnership appealed an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which dismissed its breach of contract action against defendant singer and vacated its motion for leave to file an amended complaint to raise a constitutional issue.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff partnership entered into a written contract with defendant singer. Pursuant to the contract, plaintiff was to act as defendant's career consultant in exchange for a percentage of her gross annual earnings. Plaintiff alleged that it performed its contractual obligations. Defendant informed plaintiff that their association was terminated, and defendant refused to make any payments under the contract. Plaintiff sued defendant for breach of contract. The trial court stayed the action because defendant filed a petition to determine controversy with the Division of Labor Standards Enforcement (DLSE). The DLSE issued a final determination that the contract was void and unenforceable. While plaintiff had only 15 days to file a notice of appeal to obtain de novo review by the trial court, plaintiff filed an appeal three months later. The trial court granted defendant's motion to dismiss the appeal as untimely. The appellate court affirmed the dismissal because the time limit was jurisdictional, and even a constitutionally based attack was subject to the time limit.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of plaintiff partnership's breach of contract action against defendant singer because the trial court lacked jurisdiction due to the untimeliness of the filing of plaintiff's appeal of an adverse administrative determination. Also, the court found no error in the trial court's decision to deny plaintiff's motion for leave to file an amended complaint.

 

391 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Lynch


No. B123081.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


69 Cal. App. 4th 313; 81 Cal. Rptr. 2d 552; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 34; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 539; 99 Daily Journal DAR 603



January 19, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied May 12, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 3049.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. BA152595. Terry A. Green, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, the state of California, sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied its motion for an order directing the magistrate to reinstate the complaint charging defendant with eight counts of lewd conduct with a child.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with eight counts of lewd conduct with a child, all of which allegedly occurred between 1965 and 1972. Defendant demurred arguing that the statute of limitations in effect at the time of the charged offenses had expired and could not be revived by a 1994 amendment extending the limitations period. The demurrer was overruled, but a division of the appellate court granted defendant's petition for a writ of mandate. The legislature again amended the statute, and defendant was again charged. Defendant successfully demurred, and the lower court denied plaintiff state's motion for an order directing that the complaint be reinstated. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court concluded that the legislature could put reasonable restrictions upon the constitutional functions of the courts provided that such legislatively imposed restrictions did not defeat or materially impair the exercise of the courts' functions, but the legislature could not undertake to readjudicate controversies that had been litigated in the courts and resolved by final judicial judgment. The court held that the legislature did not have the power to readjudicate the division's final decision.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the lower court's order denying plaintiff's motion for the reinstatement of the complaint against defendant. The amendment, which extended the statute of limitations for the offenses charged against defendant, was unconstitutional as applied to defendant because it constituted an effort by the legislature to readjudicate a controversy that had been litigated in the courts and resolved by a final judicial judgment.

People v. Myers


No. B117230.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


69 Cal. App. 4th 305; 81 Cal. Rptr. 2d 564; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 35; 99 Daily Journal DAR 607; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 537



January 19, 1999, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of part IV.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied April 14, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 2246.
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Myers v. Curry, 2008 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 120744 (C.D. Cal., June 19, 2008)
Decision reached on appeal by People v. Myers, 2016 Cal. App. Unpub. LEXIS 6453 (Cal. App. 2d Dist., Sept. 1, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. KA027232. William J. McVittie, Judge.



DISPOSITION: For the foregoing reasons, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) after he was found guilty by a jury of being a felon in possession of a firearm in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 12021(a)(1).


OVERVIEW: Appellant was found guilty of being a felon in possession of a firearm, a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 12021(a)(1). Appellant previously was found to have been convicted of three serious or violent felonies within the meaning of the "Three Strikes" law. Appellant argued he was entitled to be resentenced because the trial court abused its discretion in denying his motion to strike prior convictions, failed to obtain a supplemental probation report, miscalculated his presentence custody credits, and imposed an unconstitutionally cruel and unusual punishment. The lower court judgment was affirmed. Appellant failed to demonstrate the lower court abused its discretion in denying appellant's motion to strike prior convictions. Because appellant failed to request a supplemental probation report before he was sentenced, he waived the ability to claim that the lower court erred on appeal. Appellant and his counsel stipulated to the amount of presentence custody credits and, therefore, appellant again waived any arguments on appeal.


OUTCOME: The lower court judgment was affirmed because appellant failed to demonstrate that the lower court abused its discretion in denying appellant's motion to strike prior convictions, appellant failed to request a supplemental probation report before he was sentenced and therefore waived the ability to claim it as error on appeal, and appellant and his counsel stipulated to the amount of presentence custody credits thus waiving any error.

GM Corp. v. City & County of San Francisco


Nos. A081239, A081246, A081253.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


69 Cal. App. 4th 448; 81 Cal. Rptr. 2d 544; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 32; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 479; 99 Daily Journal DAR 533



January 14, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied February 16, 1999. Review Denied April 14, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 2214.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Super. Ct. Nos. 926031, 937044, 949259, 967575 and 984536. David A. Garcia, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cases remanded to the trial court for entry of judgment in favor of General Motors on its tax refund claim. ( Gov. Code, § 935, subd. (a), 940.4, 945; S.F. Mun. Code, pt. III, § 1017.) General Motors is not entitled to any relief or attorney fees under federal civil rights law. (42 U.S.C. § 1983, 1988(b).) General Motors shall recover costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed a decision from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) denying it a refund of taxes paid and granting defendant municipality summary judgment based on its finding that the business tax municipality imposed on plaintiff was proper.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff sued for refund of taxes it paid to defendant municipality, claiming the business tax illegally distinguished between manufacturer-sellers, those who sold goods they manufactured within defendant, and non-manufacturing sellers, those who sold goods they manufactured outside defendant. The trial court granted summary judgment to defendant and plaintiff appealed. The court ruled the business tax was unlawful as it discriminated against those who manufactured outside defendant, in violation of constitutional commerce protections. Defendant argued it did not have to refund all of the taxes collected. The court found that defendant's proposed refund failed to remove the discrimination and failed to provide clear and certain relief. However, plaintiff was not entitled to either damages or attorney fees under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, as there was an adequate state remedy.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision, as it found that the tax imposed by defendant municipality improperly discriminated against out-of-city manufacturers, and it ordered defendant to refund all taxes it had improperly collected. The court denied plaintiff's request for damages and attorney fees as there was an adequate state remedy.

People v. Oskins


No. B117298.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


69 Cal. App. 4th 126; 81 Cal. Rptr. 2d 383; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 24; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 385; 99 Daily Journal DAR 434



January 12, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified on Denial of Rehearing February 9, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 101. review Denied March 17, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 1574.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. No. PA027738. Kenneth R. Freeman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), entered upon a jury verdict, convicting him of possession of an illegal "dirk or dagger," in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 12020(a), following exclusion of evidence defendant sought to present as to his intent with respect to the instrument allegedly possessed and an instruction to the jury that intent was not an issue.


OVERVIEW: During a traffic stop, a fixed blade knife was discovered in defendant's pocket. The blade was wrapped in cardboard and packaging tape. Defendant was charged with possession of an illegal "dirk or dagger," in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 12020(a). At trial, he sought to present evidence that he used the knife in his work as a mechanic and had forgotten that it was in his pocket, but the evidence was excluded upon the prosecution's objection that how the knife was used was not relevant or at issue. The jury was later given an instruction that effectively said defendant's intent was not an issue. Defendant was convicted and he appealed. In reversing, the court ruled that mens rea was an element of § 12020, which required the prosecution to prove that defendant carried the knife for use as a weapon, not as a tool. The offense was not a strict liability offense. Accordingly, defendant was entitled to present evidence of the lack of a criminal intent and his intended innocent use. For the same reason, the jury was improperly instructed.


OUTCOME: The conviction was reversed and the case remanded for retrial because the intent evidence was improperly excluded and the jury improperly instructed as to intent; possession of an illegal weapon was not a strict liability offense, rather, the prosecution was required to prove that defendant possessed a stabbing weapon and carried it for use as a weapon and not for an innocent purpose.

 

395 of 1449 DOCUMENTS




Scheiding v. Dinwiddie Construction Co.


No. A076930.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


69 Cal. App. 4th 64; 81 Cal. Rptr. 2d 360; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 20; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 348; 99 Daily Journal DAR 373



January 11, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Super. Ct. No. 964534. David A. Garcia, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed a grant of summary judgment from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), in favor of defendant construction company, in an action following plaintiff's diagnosis with asbestosis and asbestos-related pleural disease.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff brought an action naming hundreds of defendants and alleging injury from asbestos exposure during work as a laborer and electrician. Defendant asked no questions at deposition and conducted no other discovery. Defendant's motion for summary judgment contended that plaintiff had no evidence to suggest defendant's presence at work sites where plaintiff's asbestos exposure allegedly occurred. The trial court failed to discern any triable issue of fact relating to the element of causation and granted defendant's motion. On appeal, plaintiff contended that without the deposition having particularly addressed defendant's presence or absence from the job-sites, the initial burden could not have been met. The court held that the initial burden of producing evidence expressly resided with the moving party and could not be shifted without the "evidence and inferences" described by the statute. Defendant's motion, relying upon argument rather than evidence, was insufficient to support a reasonable inference that plaintiff could not produce further evidence to support an element crucial to the claims. Thus the burden never shifted to plaintiff and the court reversed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the grant of summary judgment in favor of defendant on the grounds that defendant, as the moving party, had not met its initial burden of producing evidence. The burden could not be shifted without the "evidence and inferences" described by the statute. The burden never shifted to plaintiff, thus summary judgment was inappropriate.


 

396 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Rancho Publ'ns v. Superior Court


No. G023316.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


68 Cal. App. 4th 1538; 81 Cal. Rptr. 2d 274; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 16; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 288; 99 Daily Journal DAR 307



January 8, 1999, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original proceedings; petition for a writ of mandate/prohibition to challenge an order of the Superior Court of Orange County. Super. Ct. No. 767126, 767130, 767063. Tully H. Seymour, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing respondent court to vacate its orders denying petitioner's motion to quash the subpoena and holding petitioner in contempt, and to enter a new and different order granting the motion to quash. The alternative writ is discharged upon the return of the remittitur. Costs are awarded to petitioner.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner newspaper sought a writ of mandate/prohibition to challenge an order of the Superior Court of Orange County (California) denying its motion to quash respondent's subpoena for allegedly private materials and holding petitioner in contempt.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner newspaper published unsigned paid advertorials criticizing a hospital that had filed several defamation suits. The hospital subpoenaed petitioner demanding production of all documents pertaining to the advertorials. Respondent court denied petitioner's motion to quash the subpoena and for a protective order based on the California constitutional right to privacy, Cal. Const. art. I, § 1, and the state media shield law. Petitioner was adjudicated in contempt, but the order was stayed. Petitioner challenged the order. The court granted petitioner the relief it sought, but through the qualified constitutional privilege, not the media shield law, because it did not necessarily extend to paid advertisements. The court stressed the limited reach of its decision. Petitioner failed to meet its burden of proof to show the materials for which it invoked the shield law qualified as protected information. The court applied the balancing test and held that the hospital failed to meet its burden to defeat the qualified constitutional privilege by showing a compelling need to disclose the names of the anonymous authors of the advertorials, which involved core political speech.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate directing respondent court to vacate its orders denying petitioner's motion to quash the subpoena and holding petitioner in contempt, ordered it to grant the motion to quash, and awarded costs to petitioner.

People v. Superior Court (Whitley)


No. A084054.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


68 Cal. App. 4th 1383; 81 Cal. Rptr. 2d 189; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 4; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 173; 99 Daily Journal DAR 181



January 5, 1999, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied February 3, 1999. Review Denied March 17, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 1552.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County. Super. Ct. No. 981229-8. Richard S. Flier, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We conclude that the trial court erred when it granted Whitley's motion to dismiss the petition for commitment under the Act.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state appealed an order from the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California) dismissing its petition for civil commitment of a party under the Sexually Violent Predators Act, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code, § 6600 et seq. The court concluded the Act could be applied only to a person who was in custody and was either serving a prison sentence or whose parole had been revoked lawfully, and concluded it lacked jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner state appealed an order dismissing its petition for civil commitment of a party under the Sexually Violent Predators Act, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code, § 6600 et seq. The party argued that the Act could be applied only to a person who was in custody and was either serving a determinate prison sentence or whose parole had been revoked lawfully. The trial court agreed, reasoning that because the party's parole revocation had been held unauthorized by the appellate court, the trial court had no jurisdiction. The appellate court disagreed. It granted petitioner state's petition and directed the trial court to vacate its order of dismissal and conduct a probable cause hearing to determine whether the party was a sexually violent predator under the Act. The court held that because the party was a person in custody at the time the petition was filed, and it had not yet been determined that the revocation of his parole was unauthorized, in light of the purpose of the Act, the proceedings were still valid.


OUTCOME: The appellate court concluded that the trial court erred when it granted the motion to dismiss the state's petition for commitment of a party under the Sexually Violent Predators Act. The court granted petitioner state's petition and directed the trial court to vacate its order of dismissal and conduct a probable cause hearing to determine whether the party was a sexually violent predator under the Act. .

People ex rel. State Air Res. Bd. v. Wilmshurst


No. C022579.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


68 Cal. App. 4th 1332; 81 Cal. Rptr. 2d 221; 1999 Cal. App. LEXIS 7; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 139; 99 Daily Journal DAR 193



January 5, 1999, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts I.B, I.C, II, III.E, IV, VI, VII, and VIII of the Discussion.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied February 1, 1999. Review Denied April 14, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 2304.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from a judgment after trial of the Calaveras County Superior Court. Super. Ct. No. 18490. Richard Tuttle, Judge. * 


* Retired judge of the Sacramento Superior Court, assigned by the Chief Justice pursuant to article VI, section 6 of the California Constitution.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a car dealership and owner, appealed a judgment from the Calaveras County Superior Court (California), holding that defendants violated div. 26, pt. 5, ch. 2, art. 1.5 of the Cal. Health & Safety Code § 43150- 43156, in an action over vehicles not certified for the state's air emission standards.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, a dealership and owner, bought vehicles out of state. After selling them in California defendants were sued by plaintiff state for violating div. 26, pt. 5, ch. 2, art. 1.5 of the Cal. Health & Safety Code (division 26) § 43150-43156 (§ 43156) because the vehicles were not certified to California air emissions standards. Defendants were fined. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment on several grounds. First, plaintiff's action was timely because the statute of limitations was three years for division 26 actions and was retrospective. Second, the Clean Air Act, 42 U.S.C.S. § 7401 et seq., and 42 U.S.C.S. § 7543(b), did not preempt state emissions legislation because they both expressly waived it for California, which did not violate the commerce clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 3. Fourth, § 43156 did not violate the equal protection clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, because it was presumed constitutional, and bore a rational relationship to a legitimate state purpose. Fifth, the penalties were not excessive under U.S. Const. amend. XIII because defendants could pay the fine and its purpose was to deter defendants from further violation of the statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that defendants, a dealership and owner, violated the state's air emissions statutes because plaintiff's action was timely under the three-year statute of limitations period, the Clean Air Act and federal legislation did not preempt the state emissions legislation because they expressly waived it for California, and the penalties were not excessive because it was for deterrence and defendants could pay the fine.

 

399 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Anderson v. Superior Court


No. B126094.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


68 Cal. App. 4th 1240; 80 Cal. Rptr. 2d 891; 1998 Cal. App. LEXIS 1085; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 42; 99 Daily Journal DAR 7



December 30, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied March 31, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 2132.


PRIOR HISTORY: Petition for Writ of Certiorari. Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Super. Ct. Nos. BS030821 and BS027127. Robert H. O'Brien, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of contempt is annulled. Each side to bear its own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state agency challenged an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which found petitioner in contempt of court for disobedience of the order to cease utilizing the manual of policy and procedure to determine eligibility for federal foster care payments. Petitioner argued that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 11402.1 divests petitioner of authority to comply with the trial court's order.


OVERVIEW: The trial court found that the manual of policy and procedure (MPP) provisions defining eligibility for federal foster care failed to track the federal statute. It ordered petitioner to cease utilizing those provisions. The parties disputed whether petitioner had authority to cease utilizing the MPP provisions determining eligibility for federal foster care. The court held that if petitioner had identified those eligible, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 11402.1 still precluded any state or federal payment until and unless federal financial participation is obtained. The court therefore annulled the order of contempt holding that even though petitioner's department played a principal role in drafting the legislation that precluded her from complying, the legislature, not petitioner, was empowered to and chose to enact the statute. The court stated that the statute enacted by the state legislature did not violate the federal Constitution, nor did Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 11402.1 violate the separation of powers principle of Cal. Const. art. III § 3, finding no reason why the legislature could not condition funding of a federally funded program on the assurance of federal funding.


OUTCOME: The court annulled the order of contempt holding that the recently enacted statute was a valid law which petitioner was bound to apply, and that, under the circumstances of this case it prevented her from complying with the underlying order. The court further held that it was unconscionable and illegal to threaten petitioner's liberty for her acts in obedience of the intervening state statute.

 

400 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



McCauley v. Howard Jarvis Taxpayers Assn.


No. G018411.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


68 Cal. App. 4th 1255; 80 Cal. Rptr. 2d 900; 1998 Cal. App. LEXIS 1086; 99 Cal. Daily Op. Service 48; 99 Daily Journal DAR 24



December 30, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied January 26, 1999. Review Denied March 31, 1999, Reported at: 1999 Cal. LEXIS 2158.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County. Super. Ct. No. 571562. Donald E. Smallwood, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment, including the award of attorney fees, is reversed. The trial court is directed to enter judgment in favor of defendants.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer association challenged a decision by the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which found in favor of respondent individual in respondent's action against appellant for alleged violations of the reporting requirements of the Political Reform Act of 1974, Cal. Gov't Code § 91000 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Respondent individual filed an action against appellant taxpayer association and alleged violations of the reporting requirements of the Political Reform Act of 1974, Cal. Gov't Code § 91000 et seq. The complaint alleged only one specific violation. Almost four years later, respondent filed his first amended complaint, which reiterated the allegations in the initial complaint and included additional violations of reporting laws. Respondent demurred to the first amended complaint on, among other grounds, the running of the statute of limitations. The trial court ruled in favor of respondent, based solely on reporting violations connected to a single letter. Appellant sought review. The court reversed the judgment of the trial court. The court held that respondent's action was barred by the statute of limitations set forth in Cal. Gov't Code § 91011. While respondent filed his original action within the limitations period, he did not specifically allege violations of the reporting statute until he filed his first amended complaint. The allegations set forth in respondent's amended complaint did not relate back to the original complaint for statute of limitations purposes.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court, holding that the statute of limitations barred respondent individual's action against appellant taxpayer association for alleged violations of the reporting requirements of the Political Reform Act of 1974. The court directed the trial court to enter judgment in favor of appellant.
